# Synthetic Data for Text-to-Speech: A Comprehensive Literature Review

> Coverage cutoff: **23 June 2026**. Generated: 2026-06-23.
>
> Compiled from a 14-angle web + arXiv search sweep: **392** raw hits deduplicated to **186** unique papers; the top **140** were deep-read and **all 140** independently verified to exist. Each paper below was analyzed for its synthetic-data role, architecture, base models, datasets, public code, replication recipe, and headline metrics. 86 lowest-signal candidates were not enriched (listed nowhere below).

This review covers every setting in which **synthetic, machine-generated, LLM-generated, pseudo-labeled, voice-converted, or otherwise non-human-authored data** is used to build, train, fine-tune, align, or improve a text-to-speech / speech-synthesis system.

**Category taxonomy** (used throughout; a paper may belong to several):

- **C1** - Training TTS models from scratch on synthetic data
- **C2** - Fine-tuning existing TTS models with synthetic data
- **C3** - Reinforcement learning & preference optimization for TTS (DPO/RLHF/GRPO/PPO)
- **C4** - Datasets built from LLM-generated text or synthetic speech
- **C5** - Self-training, pseudo-labeling & data augmentation
- **C6** - Synthetic speaker generation, accent/style transfer & multilingual TTS
- **C7** - Corpora partially or fully generated synthetically

## 1. Introduction & taxonomy

### 1. Introduction

#### 1.1 Why Synthetic Data Matters for Modern TTS

Text-to-speech (TTS) has shifted from concatenative and statistical parametric pipelines to large neural and language-model-based systems trained on tens of thousands of hours of audio. This shift has exposed a structural bottleneck: high-quality, transcribed, permissively licensed speech is scarce, expensive to record, and unevenly distributed across languages, accents, and speaking styles. Synthetic data has become the dominant response to four pressures at once.

**Data scarcity.** Most of the world's roughly 7,000 languages have little or no studio-grade recorded speech with aligned transcripts. Manual collection is slow and costly, and many domains (children's speech, emotional speech, code-switched speech, low-resource accents) are barely represented in public corpora. Generating speech and text fills gaps that recording cannot reach at scale.

**Scaling.** Modern TTS and the speech tokenizers feeding multimodal language models benefit from web-scale audio. Synthetic generation lets researchers expand corpora by orders of magnitude, producing the volume that data-hungry transformer and diffusion architectures need without proportional human effort.

**Controllability.** Synthetic pipelines can target specific attributes on demand: a chosen speaker identity, emotion, prosody, accent, recording condition, or linguistic content. This precise control over the data distribution is difficult to obtain by curating found audio and is central to building controllable and expressive systems.

**Alignment.** As TTS adopts preference-based training (DPO, RLHF, GRPO, PPO), synthetic generations become the substrate for optimization. Models produce candidate utterances, automatic or human judgments score them, and the resulting preference data steers systems toward naturalness, intelligibility, and speaker fidelity. Here synthetic data is not a supplement to training but the mechanism of alignment itself.

#### 1.2 Defining Synthetic Data in the TTS Context

We use "synthetic data" broadly to mean any training signal not drawn directly from a human recording paired with its original human-written, human-verified transcript. In the TTS literature this covers five overlapping forms:

- **Synthetic speech.** Audio produced by a TTS or voice-conversion model rather than recorded from a human speaker, used as training material for downstream speech or recognition models.
- **LLM-generated text.** Transcripts, prompts, and scripts authored by language models to supply diverse, controlled, or domain-specific content for synthesis.
- **Pseudo-labels.** Machine-produced annotations (transcripts from ASR, alignments, speaker or emotion tags) attached to unlabeled or weakly labeled audio.
- **Augmentation.** Transformations of existing data (speed, pitch, noise, room simulation, vocoder resynthesis) that enlarge and diversify the effective training set.
- **Synthetic speakers.** Voices that do not correspond to any real individual, created by sampling or interpolating speaker embeddings, enabling identity control and privacy-preserving data.

These forms frequently combine, for example LLM-written scripts spoken by synthetic speakers and then pseudo-labeled, which motivates a taxonomy organized by research goal rather than by data type alone.

#### 1.3 Taxonomy

This review organizes the literature into seven categories, each capturing a distinct way synthetic data is produced or used:

1. **Training TTS models from scratch on synthetic data.** Systems whose initial training relies substantially on generated speech or text rather than only natural recordings.
2. **Fine-tuning existing TTS models with synthetic data.** Adapting pretrained models to new speakers, styles, domains, or languages using generated material.
3. **Reinforcement learning and preference optimization for TTS.** Methods using DPO, RLHF, GRPO, PPO, and related objectives, where synthetic generations form the preference or reward signal.
4. **Datasets built from LLM-generated text or synthetic speech.** Released or described corpora whose content originates from language models or speech synthesis.
5. **Self-training, pseudo-labeling, and data augmentation.** Iterative bootstrapping, machine annotation of unlabeled audio, and signal-level transformations that expand training data.
6. **Synthetic speaker generation, accent and style transfer, and multilingual TTS.** Creating new voices, transferring accent or style, and extending coverage across languages.
7. **Corpora partially or fully generated synthetically.** Datasets explicitly assembled, in whole or in part, from synthetic sources, distinguished from category 4 by emphasis on the corpus artifact and its composition.

Categories are analytical rather than mutually exclusive; many papers contribute to more than one, and we place each work by its primary contribution.

#### 1.4 Methodology

We compiled the literature through multi-angle search across the web, arXiv, and major speech and machine-learning venues, querying combinations of TTS terminology with synthetic-data, preference-optimization, pseudo-labeling, and multilingual keywords, and following citation trails forward and backward from anchor papers. Candidate works were screened for relevance to TTS specifically, then each was checked against its primary source to confirm the claimed method, data, and results. Coverage extends through 23 June 2026. The review compiles 140 papers, of which 140 were independently verified against their primary sources.

---

## 3. Thematic review by category

### 3.1 C1

**Training TTS models from scratch on synthetic data**

This category groups work in which the acoustic targets, the text, the labels, or the speaker identities used to train a TTS model are wholly or substantially machine-generated, rather than drawn from human-annotated paired corpora. The common thread is a teacher-to-student transfer of capability, but the papers differ sharply in what exactly is synthesized and why.

#### Teacher-TTS-as-data-generator

The earliest formulation treats a high-quality but fragile model as a pure data source for a cheaper, more robust student. "TTS-by-TTS" (Hwang et al., 2020) uses an autoregressive Tacotron-style teacher to synthesize 179 hours of text-waveform-duration triples from only 5 hours of real audio, then trains a non-autoregressive FastSpeech 2 student (with Parallel WaveGAN vocoding) on the augmented corpus. The durations the teacher emits are the load-bearing signal, since they supply the alignment a non-AR model cannot learn from scarce data; MOS rose to 3.74, roughly 40% over the non-augmented baseline. "Training TTS Systems From Synthetic Data" (Finkelstein et al., 2022) applies the same distillation logic to a harder task: an unstable accent-transfer Tacotron generates accent-converted speech, and a robust CHiVE-BERT student is trained entirely on those outputs, transferring the capability into a deployable model while preserving speaker identity. "Cross-lingual Knowledge Distillation via Flow-based Voice Conversion" (Piotrowski et al., 2023) extends this to polyglot TTS: a flow-based, text-conditioned many-to-many VC teacher (built on Flow-TTS) converts foreign-language utterances into one target speaker's voice, and a FastSpeech 2 / LightSpeech student plus a locale-independent MultiBand MelGAN learns from the converted data, beating a large multilingual baseline (es-MX MUSHRA naturalness 69.60 vs 64.08).

By 2025 the teachers are large pretrained foundation models. "Training TTS Model with Purely Synthetic Data" (Zhou et al., 2025) trains a 20.9M-param Matcha-TTS (flow-matching, OT-CFM) student entirely on speech from XTTS-v2, CosyVoice2, and ChatTTS, then ablates text richness, speaker count (up to 1,000), noise, and style. Purely synthetic training matched or beat real LibriSpeech (WER 3.493 vs 4.428). "Bayesian Speech synthesizers" (Zhang et al., 2025) generalizes the single-teacher idea: BELLE prompts multiple TTS teachers on identical text-audio prompts to manufacture a one-to-many support set, then distills that variance via evidential learning (Normal-Inverse-Gamma head), cutting WER 25.8% relative using ~5k hours versus ~50k.

#### Pseudo-labeling untranscribed speech

A parallel line synthesizes the labels rather than the audio. "Simple and Effective Unsupervised Speech Synthesis" (Liu et al., 2022) is the first fully unsupervised recipe: wav2vec-U 2.0 produces pseudo-transcripts for untranscribed audio, and a standard TTS trains on these (audio, pseudo-label) pairs, reaching naturalness comparable to its supervised twin. "DQR-TTS" (2023) does semi-supervised pseudo-labeling with a dynamically growing phoneme codebook that expands when novel-phoneme distances exceed a threshold (MOS 3.12 with 120+600 min). "Virtuoso" (Saeki et al., 2022) and its successor "Extending Multilingual Speech Synthesis to 100+ Languages" (Saeki et al., 2024) fold untranscribed speech and unspoken text into a Maestro-based joint speech-text encoder, scaling to 100+ languages with under 10% CER gap and no transcripts. "ZipVoice-Dialog" (Zhu et al., 2025) builds OpenDialog (6.8k h) via a VAD-diarization-ASR-WhisperD pseudo-labeling pipeline for a 123M flow-matching dialogue model (English WER 3.25 vs MoonCast 23.62).

#### Synthesizing speakers, scripts, and self-generated rollouts

Other papers synthesize artifacts other than audio or transcripts. "Speaker Generation" (Stanton et al., 2021) learns a mixture prior over the speaker-embedding space (TacoSpawn) and samples entirely novel, nonexistent voices. "MoonCast" (Ju et al., 2025) LLM-generates the podcast script (Gemini 2.0 via INPUT2BRIEF then BRIEF2SCRIPT), while a 2.5B long-context speech LM produces the audio. RL-era systems train on self-generated rollouts: "GLM-TTS" (Cui et al., 2025) and "CosyVoice 3" (Du et al., 2025) score model-sampled speech tokens with multi-reward GRPO and a differentiable reward model (DiffRO), lowering CER (GLM-TTS_RL 0.89). "TTSOps" (Seki et al., 2025) uses synthesis-in-the-loop UTMOS scoring to select dark-data utterances, and "Self-distilled Representation Disentanglement" (Chen et al., 2025) generates content-preserving, speaker-swapped parallel pairs for a 22.5M student. "EmoVoice" (Yang et al., 2025) trains a Qwen2.5-backboned emotional TTS on the fully GPT-4o-synthesized EmoVoice-DB (WER 2.62%). Strong open generators like "VoxCPM" (Zhou et al., 2025) underpin much of this downstream synthesis.

### 3.2 C2

**Fine-tuning existing TTS models with synthetic data**

Papers in this category share a defining premise: rather than training a TTS system from scratch on synthetic corpora, they take an already-capable model and adapt it using machine-generated data, self-generated rollouts, automatic pseudo-labels, or voice-converted audio. The synthetic signal takes three broad forms, surveyed below.

#### Voice-conversion augmentation for expressive and cross-speaker styles

The earliest line treats voice conversion (VC) as a synthetic-data generator for speakers who lack expressive recordings. "Low-resource expressive text-to-speech using data augmentation" (Huybrechts et al., 2021) converts a donor speaker's stylistic recordings (newscaster, conversational) into a target voice, then trains a Tacotron-class acoustic model on synthetic-plus-15-minutes-of-real data and fine-tunes on the real subset. "Cross-speaker style transfer for text-to-speech using data augmentation" (Ribeiro et al., 2022) generalizes this to a single multi-style TTS pooling natural neutral and VC-synthesized expressive speech across many supporting speakers and languages. The Unicamp/CPQD group extended the recipe: "Improving Data Augmentation-based Cross-Speaker Style Transfer" (Marques et al., 2024) adds singing-voice conversion, a style classifier to filter weak conversions, and F0 matching to fix pitch mismatch, while "Exploring synthetic data for cross-speaker style transfer" (Ueda et al., 2024) trains a style-representation encoder with timbre perturbation and prototypical-angular loss to prevent identity leakage. "SelfTTS" (AI-Unicamp, 2026) closes the loop by using the model's own VC path for on-the-fly self-augmentation during a refinement stage on a VITS backbone. "Voice Filter" (Gabrys et al., 2022) inverts the framing, synthesizing time-aligned parallel source audio so few-shot adaptation becomes a VC post-processing task.

#### Preference optimization on self-generated speech

The dominant modern paradigm fine-tunes pretrained LLM-style codec TTS via preference optimization, building preference pairs entirely from the model's own generations scored by frozen reward models (ASR for intelligibility, speaker verification for similarity). "SpeechAlign" (Zhang et al., 2024) seeds this with iterative DPO contrasting golden codec tokens against synthetic tokens to close the train-inference gap. "Koel-TTS" (Hussain et al., 2025) formalizes the recipe with Pareto-ranked self-samples and Reward-aware Preference Optimization (RPO, generally beating DPO) plus classifier-free guidance. "MPO" (Xia et al., 2025) replaces single DPO pairs with multidimensional preference sets spanning intelligibility, speaker similarity, and prosody. "ARDM-DPO" (Liu et al., 2025) ports DPO to autoregressive diffusion (DiTAR), and "Emo-DPO" (Gao et al., 2024), "Emo-LiPO" (Lin et al., 2026), and EASPO (Shi et al., 2025) extend preference learning to emotion, the last operating at intermediate denoising steps.

Online RL variants replace fixed pairs with on-policy rollouts. GRPO-for-TTS (Liu et al., 2025) derives rewards from Whisper CER plus negative log-likelihood; "F5R-TTS" (Sun et al., 2025) reformulates F5-TTS flow-matching steps as Gaussian policies; "Align2Speak" (Hussain et al., 2025) drives GRPO with unpaired text in low-resource languages; "CosyEdit2" (2026) and "DMOSpeech 2" (Li et al., 2025) extend GRPO to speech editing and duration prediction. "DLPO" (Chen et al., 2024/2025) regularizes diffusion RLHF with the diffusion loss itself. "DiffRO" (Gao et al., 2025) computes differentiable rewards directly on codec tokens via Gumbel-Softmax, later folded into "CosyVoice 3" (Du et al., 2025) and "Qwen3-TTS" (2026). Notably, "No Verifiable Reward for Prosody" (Shin et al., 2025) shows verifiable-reward GRPO collapses prosody to monotone speech, motivating small human-in-the-loop DPO batches.

#### Pseudo-labeled and LLM-generated corpora for adaptation

A third strand fine-tunes on machine-labeled or LLM-authored data. "LLM-to-Speech" (Khamis and Ali, 2026) builds the fully synthetic 38-hour NileTTS (LLM text, synthesized audio, ASR transcription, diarization) to fine-tune XTTS v2 for Egyptian Arabic. "EmoVoice" (Yang et al., 2025) fine-tunes Qwen2.5 backbones on GPT-4o-generated EmoVoice-DB. "ZeSTA" (Choi et al., 2026) tags ZS-TTS-generated speech with a domain embedding and oversamples real data to prevent similarity degradation. "Scalable Controllable Accented TTS" (Li Xinyuan et al., 2025) pseudo-labels accents via a geolocation model plus kNN-VC augmentation, while "Bridging the Stability-Expressivity Gap" (Geng et al., 2026) diagnoses "Synthetic Erosion" when synthetic ratio exceeds 50% and proposes disentanglement-guided self-alignment.

Chronologically the field moved from VC augmentation (2021-2022) through pseudo-label and SSL-mixing adaptation (Huang et al., 2024) to preference and RL fine-tuning (2024-2026), with autoregressive codec LMs (CosyVoice, Koel-TTS, Llasa, XTTS), flow-matching DiTs (F5-TTS), and diffusion models as the dominant backbones. Headline gains are consistent: roughly 25-30% relative WER/CER reductions and maintained speaker similarity (for example F5R-TTS's ~29.5% WER drop, GRPO-TTS CER 1.41 to 1.07), with preference methods adding measurable naturalness and emotion-control improvements.

### 3.3 C3

**Reinforcement learning & preference optimization for TTS (DPO/RLHF/GRPO/PPO)**

#### Overview and chronological evolution

This category covers the shift from supervised next-token training to alignment-style post-training for TTS, where the dominant synthetic-data pattern is self-generated: the policy model samples its own candidate utterances, which are then scored by automatic reward signals to form preference pairs or reward-weighted rollouts. Human annotation, when present at all, is minimal.

The lineage begins in 2024 with "SpeechAlign: Aligning Speech Generation to Human Preferences" (Zhang et al., 2024), which framed the train-inference distribution gap as a preference problem: golden ground-truth codec tokens are the chosen side, the model's own synthetic codec tokens the rejected side, and DPO (compared against CoH, RLHF-PPO, and Best-of-N) closes the gap iteratively on a SpeechGPT codec LM. In parallel, "Robust Zero-Shot TTS with Reverse Inference Optimization" (Hu et al., 2024) and "Enhancing Zero-shot TTS with Human Feedback" (Chen et al., 2024) introduced the sampling -> annotating -> learning loop on a VoiceCraft backbone. RIO selects exemplars by a Bayesian reverse-inference criterion (a good sample should itself prompt faithful regeneration); UNO collects binary human labels with an uncertainty score and scales a KTO-style unpaired update by inverse uncertainty (WER 8.4% to 2.6% on the 330M model). "Preference Alignment Improves LM-Based TTS" (Tian et al., 2024) established the now-standard recipe empirically: sample 10 utterances per condition, take top/bottom 20% by automatic metrics as chosen/rejected, DPO wins (WER 4.5 to 3.0).

#### Automatic reward design (the dominant axis)

By 2025 the field converged on ASR-derived and speaker-verification-derived rewards over self-generated audio, removing human labels entirely. Koel-TTS (Hussain et al., 2025) ranks 6 candidates by CER and speaker similarity via Pareto optimization, using DPO and Reward-aware Preference Optimization (RPO generally beating DPO) plus classifier-free guidance. "Group Relative Policy Optimization for TTS with LLMs" (Liu et al., 2025) brought GRPO to CosyVoice2 and Llasa-1B, combining ASR CER and ASR negative-log-likelihood via a weighted harmonic mean (G=8 rollouts; Chinese CER 1.41 to 1.07). F5R-TTS (Sun et al., 2025) reformulated F5-TTS's deterministic flow-matching outputs as Gaussians to make a sampleable policy for GRPO. Align2Speak (Hussain et al., 2025) pushed this to low-resource languages using unpaired text only, with ASR plus SV plus audio-quality rewards substituting for scarce paired speech.

Several papers refine reward granularity. FPO (Yao et al., 2025) localizes problematic tokens via Whisper-timestamped, VAD, and forced alignment, then applies a token-selective DPO loss (CER-CN 8.26 to 3.92, ~4x more data-efficient). W3AR (Wang and Sun, 2025) derives word-level rewards from a frozen Whisper's cross-attention (Attention Purity, Alignment Monotonicity). TKTO (Kotoge and Sasaki, 2025) targets error-prone tokens without paired data (54% CER reduction in Japanese). MPO (Xia et al., 2025) replaces single pairs with multidimensional preference sets (intelligibility, similarity, prosody) plus cross-entropy regularization against over-optimization.

#### Differentiable and distillation-based metric optimization

A distinct branch makes rewards differentiable. DiffRO (Gao et al., 2025) computes multi-task rewards (ASR, SER, quality, age/gender) directly on codec tokens via Gumbel-Softmax (0.78% WER on Seed-TTS zh). DMOSpeech (Li et al., 2024) and DMOSpeech 2 (Li et al., 2025) combine distribution-matching distillation with direct CTC and SV metric optimization; DMOSpeech 2 adds GRPO for the duration predictor. DLPO (Chen et al., 2024; Chen et al., 2025) fine-tunes WaveGrad2 diffusion using UTMOS/NISQA reward plus the original diffusion loss as a regularizer.

#### Emotion, prosody, and reward modeling

Emotion-controllable variants include Emo-DPO (Gao et al., 2024), which mines same-text different-emotion negatives; EASPO (Shi et al., 2025), scoring noisy diffusion states stepwise; Emo-LiPO (Lin et al., 2026), casting intensity as listwise ranking; EMORL-TTS (Li et al., 2025); RRPO (Wang et al., 2025), hardening reward models against reward hacking; and RLAIF-SPA (Yang et al., 2025), using structured LLM feedback. Prosody resists verifiable rewards: "No Verifiable Reward for Prosody" (Shin et al., 2025) shows GRPO on CER/NLL collapses to monotone speech, favoring iterative DPO with small human batches. Reward modeling itself matured via GSRM (Kanda et al., 2026) and SpeechJudge (Amphion Team, 2025), both Qwen2.5-Omni-7B generative reward models with chain-of-thought scoring (SpeechJudge 77.2% human agreement).

#### Production systems and scaling

Foundation reports fold RL into pipelines: Seed-TTS (Anastassiou et al., 2024), CosyVoice 3 (Du et al., 2025, DiffRO post-training), GLM-TTS (Cui et al., 2025, GRPO multi-reward), Qwen3-TTS (Qwen Team, 2026, DPO plus GSPO), and Fish Audio S2 (Fish Audio team, 2026, GRPO with ASR/quality/voiceprint rewards; 0.54% zh WER). Dataset-construction work includes INTP (Zhang et al., 2025), a 250K-pair architecture-agnostic preference corpus, and the synthetic-scaling diagnosis of "Synthetic Erosion" (Geng et al., 2026). Related GRPO studies extend to editing (CosyEdit2, 2026), text-to-audio (Gu et al., 2026), and speech-LLM credit assignment (LEAF, 2026; Gao et al., 2025).

### 3.4 C4

**Datasets built from LLM-generated text or synthetic speech**

This category covers methods where the training corpus itself is fabricated, either by having a large language model author the text, by having a teacher TTS render the audio, or by machine-annotating real audio. The papers split into three lineages that emerged and then converged: TTS-synthesized audio for downstream task augmentation, LLM-generated text paired with TTS rendering, and pseudo-label/automatic-annotation pipelines that scale real audio.

#### Synthetic speech as augmentation for downstream tasks

The oldest thread uses a TTS as a data factory for ASR and speaker tasks. "Training Neural Speech Recognition Systems with Synthetic Speech Augmentation" (Li et al., 2018) mixed Tacotron-2-with-Global-Style-Tokens output into LibriSpeech to train Wave2Letter+ and Deep Speech 2, reaching SOTA character-level WER. "Speech Recognition with Augmented Synthesized Speech" (Rosenberg et al., 2019) extended this by manipulating Tacotron prosody/speaker/style latents to inject variety, and crucially quantified a persistent human-versus-synthetic gap. "Synth2Aug" (Huang et al., 2020) carried the idea to speaker verification (about 30% relative EER reduction), and Sharma et al. (2020) added Mixup plus a reweighted loss to make code-switched synthetic speech actually help Hindi-English ASR. Casanova et al. (2022) showed YourTTS-based cross-lingual synthesis needs only one real target speaker to nearly close the ASR gap.

The modern incarnation swaps Tacotron for zero-shot codec-LM TTS and adds LLM-written transcripts. "Enhancing Low-Resource ASR through Versatile TTS" (Yang et al., 2024) used CosyVoice-base-300M and found text diversity matters more than speaker diversity. "Hard-Synth" (anonymous, 2024) used LLaMA3.1-8B to rewrite text and VoiceCraft to clone styles the ASR found hardest. "Generating Data with TTS and LLMs for Conversational ASR" (Cornell et al., 2024) paired Llama 3 8B dialogue generation with the Parakeet diffusion TTS, cutting Fisher cpWER from 44.94% to 20.41%, with LLM text matching real transcripts. "Scaling Back-Translation for Speech Recognition" (Wang et al., 2025) pushed volume to over 500k hours of synthetic audio for Whisper continued-pretraining (over 30% error reduction). Domain-specialized variants include "Towards Improved Speech Recognition through Optimized Synthetic Data" (Perrin and Boulianne, 2025), Wagner et al. (2025) for dysarthric ASR via Parler-TTS, Do et al. (2024) for accented ASR, and the self-refining ASR<->TTS loop in Chou et al. (2025).

#### LLM-generated text plus synthetic speech for TTS itself

The boldest claim is training TTS purely on synthetic data. "Training TTS with Purely Synthetic Data" (Zhou et al., 2025) trained a 20.9M Matcha-TTS student on XTTS-v2/CosyVoice2/ChatTTS output, matching or beating real LibriSpeech. "LLM-to-Speech" (Khamis and Ali, 2026) built the 38h Egyptian-Arabic NileTTS via LLM text then synthesis then ASR then diarization. End-to-end pipelines like "SpeechWeave" (Dua et al., 2025; Mistral-7B plus MeloTTS plus OpenVoiceV2) emphasize diversity metrics. Emotional and controllable TTS lean heaviest on GPT-generated data: "EmoVoice" (Yang et al., 2025) used GPT-4o and GPT-4o-audio for the 40h EmoVoice-DB; "Emo-LiPO" (Lin et al., 2026) generated ESD-plus with gpt-4o-mini-tts; Ma et al. (2023) augmented SER with GPT-4 text plus Azure TTS; and accent-conversion work synthesizes parallel pairs via SYNTACC (Nguyen et al., 2024) or LLM transliteration in "MacST" (Inoue et al., 2024). "INSIDE" (Liu et al., 2025) slerps speaker embeddings to fabricate identities. Many systems use multiple teachers: "Bayesian Speech synthesizers" (Zhang et al., 2025) distills variance from several teachers via evidential learning; "Cross-lingual Knowledge Distillation" (Piotrowski et al., 2023) uses a flow VC teacher.

#### Machine-authored annotations and pseudo-labels

A parallel branch keeps audio real but synthesizes the labels. "Simple and Effective Unsupervised Speech Synthesis" (Liu et al., 2022) pioneered pseudo-transcripts via wav2vec-U 2.0. Style-prompt datasets generate descriptions automatically: "PromptTTS 2" (Leng et al., 2023), "TextrolSpeech" (Ji et al., 2023), "Natural Language Guidance" (Lyth and King, 2024; the Parler-TTS basis), "VoxInstruct" (Zhou et al., 2024), "ParaSpeechCaps" (Diwan et al., 2025), "AffectSpeech" (Chee et al., 2026), "FlexiVoice" (2026), and "OV-InstructTTS" (Ren et al., 2026) with LLM-generated reasoning chains. Emilia (He et al., 2024; 2025) and dialogue corpora (MoonCast, FireRedTTS-2, MOSS-TTSD, ZipVoice-Dialog, SoulX-Podcast) use Whisper/diarization pseudo-labeling, sometimes adding tens of thousands of hours of synthetic dialogue. Reward-model and benchmark work closes the loop: GSRM (Kanda et al., 2026), SpeechJudge (2025), INTP (Zhang et al., 2025), Qwen3-TTS (2026), Fish Audio S2 (2026), and InstructTTSEval (Huang et al., 2025) build synthetic preference pairs or LLM-as-judge instructions to align generation. The dominant architecture across the recent papers is an LLM/codec-LM backbone (Qwen2.5, LLaMA) over discrete speech tokens with a flow-matching plus HiFiGAN/Vocos decoder; the through-line is that synthetic supervision, text, audio, or labels, increasingly substitutes for scarce human data.

### 3.5 C5

**Self-training, pseudo-labeling & data augmentation**

This category covers methods where the training signal is generated by models rather than human annotators: a TTS or ASR system synthesizes its own data, automatic scorers stand in for human labels, and feedback loops bootstrap quality from unpaired or unlabeled corpora. Three intertwined threads emerge: (1) the speech chain and cycle-consistency lineage, (2) TTS-driven augmentation for downstream recognition, and (3) self-generated preference data for alignment.

#### The speech chain and cycle-consistency lineage

The conceptual root is "Listening while Speaking: Speech Chain by Deep Learning" (Tjandra et al., 2017), which coupled seq2seq ASR and Tacotron-style TTS in a closed loop so each model supervised the other on unlabeled data: ASR pseudo-labels speech for TTS reconstruction, TTS synthesizes speech for ASR reconstruction. "Machine Speech Chain with One-shot Speaker Adaptation" (Tjandra et al., 2018) added a speaker embedding so TTS-generated audio carried recoverable speaker identity. In parallel, "Cycle-consistency training for end-to-end speech recognition" (Hori et al., 2018) made a key refinement: rather than reconstruct the waveform, it reconstructed the ASR encoder-state sequence via a text-to-encoder module, sidestepping the lossy text bottleneck. "Semi-supervised Sequence-to-sequence ASR using Unpaired Speech and Text" (Baskar et al., 2019) made the ASR-to-TTS path differentiable, and "EAT" (Baskar et al., 2021) hardened it with an LM penalty on weak hypotheses and attention scaling to discount low-fidelity synthetic audio. The dual-learning idea peaked in TTS-and-ASR co-training: "Almost Unsupervised TTS and ASR" (Ren et al., 2019) reached 99.84% intelligibility and 11.7% PER from ~20 minutes of paired data using denoising autoencoding plus dual transformation on a shared transformer, and "LRSpeech" (Xu et al., 2020) industrialized this with rich-to-low-resource pretraining, dual transformation, and distillation. The modern incarnation is "TokenChain" (Wang and Nakamura, 2025), which moves the chain into discrete semantic-token space with straight-through/Gumbel-Softmax gradients, yielding 56% relative WER reduction in cross-domain adaptation. "Speaker consistency loss" (Makishima et al., 2022) is the diagnostic counterpoint: naive cycle synthesis degenerates into trivially-recognizable speech, which it fixes with a speaker-matching loss and step-wise optimization.

#### Unsupervised and semi-supervised pseudo-labeling

A distinct line drops the loop and uses unsupervised ASR as a one-shot labeler. "Simple and Effective Unsupervised Speech Synthesis" (Liu et al., 2022) and "Unsupervised TTS by Unsupervised ASR" (Ni et al., 2022) both use wav2vec-U-style GAN-based recognition to pseudo-transcribe untranscribed speech, then train a standard TTS on those pairs, matching supervised quality across up to seven languages from ~10-20h. Representation-based semi-supervision avoids transcripts entirely: "QS-TTS" (Guo et al., 2023), "DQR-TTS" (2023), and "Semi-supervised Multi-speaker TTS with Discrete Speech Representation" (Tu et al., 2020) learn VQ pseudo-phoneme bridges from unlabeled audio. "Virtuoso" (Saeki et al., 2022) and "Extending Multilingual TTS to 100+ Languages" (Saeki et al., 2024) scale this with Maestro-style joint speech-text encoders, reaching sub-1% CER gaps with 15 minutes of found data. "Semi-supervised Training for Data Efficiency" (Chung et al., 2018) is the early data-efficiency exemplar (pretrain encoder on text, decoder on speech).

#### TTS-driven augmentation for downstream tasks

Here a strong TTS fabricates training data for ASR, speaker verification, or SER. The architectural progression runs from Tacotron-GST generators ("Training Neural ASR with Synthetic Speech Augmentation," Li et al., 2018; "Speech Recognition with Augmented Synthesized Speech," Rosenberg et al., 2019; "SynthASR," Fazel et al., 2021) through YourTTS-class zero-shot multi-speaker VC (Casanova et al., 2022) to today's CosyVoice/XTTS/VoiceCraft foundation models. "TTS-by-TTS" (Hwang et al., 2020) is the canonical self-distillation: an AR teacher generates 179h to train a non-AR FastSpeech 2 student (MOS 3.74, ~40% gain). Recent work emphasizes which synthetic factors matter: "Enhancing Low-Resource ASR through Versatile TTS" (Yang et al., 2024) finds text diversity dominates speaker diversity; "Hard-Synth" (2024) and "Personalized Fine-Tuning with LLM-Generated Transcripts" (Wagner et al., 2025) target hard or dysarthric cases; "Scaling Back-Translation for ASR" (Wang et al., 2025) generates >500k hours for >30% error reduction; and "Generating Data with TTS and LLMs" (Cornell et al., 2024) pairs Llama-written dialogues with conversational TTS to nearly match real in-domain data (cpWER 20.41% vs 15.43% real). Augmentation also feeds verification ("Synth2Aug," Huang et al., 2020; "Adaptive Augmentation with NaturalSpeech3," Zhang et al., 2025) and SER (Ma et al., 2023; Shahid et al., 2023). The closed-loop ASR-TTS-ASR refinement reappears in "A Self-Refining Framework" (Chou et al., 2025) and "Improving Code-Switching ASR" (Yeo et al., 2026). Feature-space augmentation ("SpecAugment," Park et al., 2019) and VC-based style transfer (Ribeiro et al., 2022; Huybrechts et al., 2021; Tinchev et al., 2023) round out the non-generative-text augmentation family. Pseudo-labeling pipelines for corpus construction (Emilia, He et al., 2024/2025; synthetic-annotation datasets Lyth and King, 2024; Diwan et al., 2025) supply the data substrate.

#### Self-generated preference data and on-policy RL

The newest and densest thread reframes self-training as alignment: the model samples its own generations, automatic reward models pseudo-label them, and DPO or GRPO updates the policy. "Preference Alignment Improves LM-Based TTS" (Tian et al., 2024) established the recipe (sample 10, take top/bottom 20% by Whisper-WER/RawNet-SIM/UTMOS; WER 4.5 to 3.0). "RIO" (Hu et al., 2024) replaced the scorer with a reverse-inference self-selection criterion needing no reward model; "UNO" (Chen et al., 2024) added human binary labels with uncertainty-scaled KTO updates on VoiceCraft (WER 8.4% to 2.6%). The 2025 wave automated and refined the reward: "FPO" (Yao et al., 2025) localizes problematic tokens for selective DPO (CER 8.26 to 3.92 with 4x fewer samples); "MPO" (Xia et al., 2025) uses multi-sample preference sets across intelligibility/similarity/prosody; "Koel-TTS" (Hussain et al., 2025) ranks 6 candidates by Pareto-optimal CER/SSIM with RPO beating DPO; "INTP" (Zhang et al., 2025) builds 250k architecture-agnostic OOD pairs across AR, flow-matching, and masked-generative families. GRPO-based online variants followed: "F5R-TTS" (Sun et al., 2025) makes flow-matching outputs Gaussian for sampling (~29.5% WER reduction); "Align2Speak" (Hussain et al., 2025) uses text-only ASR-guided GRPO for low-resource languages; "DMOSpeech 2" (Li et al., 2025) applies GRPO to the previously-unoptimized duration predictor; "ARDM-DPO" (Liu et al., 2025) adapts DPO to autoregressive diffusion. Emotion-controllable extensions ("Emo-DPO," Gao et al., 2024; "EASPO," Shi et al., 2025; "RLAIF-SPA," Yang et al., 2025) and reasoning-grounded reward models ("GSRM," Kanda et al., 2026; "DiffRO," Gao et al., 2025, which makes rewards differentiable via Gumbel-Softmax over codec tokens) close the loop. "BELLE" (Zhang et al., 2025) distills variance from multiple teacher generations via Bayesian evidential learning; "Bridging the Stability-Expressivity Gap" (Geng et al., 2026) diagnoses "Synthetic Erosion" (prosodic collapse beyond 50% synthetic ratio) and recovers it with self-alignment. Production reports ("Seed-TTS," Anastassiou et al., 2024; "Fish Audio S2," 2026; "MOSS-TTSD," 2026) fold pseudo-labeling and self-generated RL into large-scale systems.

The chronological arc thus moves from differentiable cycle losses on hand-built seq2seq models (2017-2021), to unsupervised-ASR pseudo-labeling and foundation-model augmentation (2022-2024), to self-generated automatic-reward preference alignment as the dominant 2024-2026 paradigm, with the recurring lesson that synthetic data must be filtered, reweighted, or uncertainty-scaled to avoid degeneration.

### 3.6 C6

**Synthetic speaker generation, accent/style transfer & multilingual TTS**

This category groups work that uses generated or model-derived data to manufacture new voices, transfer accent and speaking style, and extend TTS to languages and speaker conditions where paired recordings are scarce. Five threads recur: voice conversion (VC) as a parallel-data factory, accent control without accented corpora, novel-speaker sampling, multilingual bootstrapping from unpaired data, and disentangled or task-vector recombination.

#### Voice conversion as a synthetic-data factory

The earliest and most durable idea is to convert an existing voice into a target identity or style, then train TTS on the converted output. Cross-speaker style transfer (Ribeiro et al., 2022) and Low-resource expressive TTS (Huybrechts et al., 2021) both pool a target speaker's neutral recordings with VC-converted expressive speech from supporting speakers, so the target never records the expressive style; the latter needs only ~15 minutes of target data plus a fine-tuning step. Cross-lingual Knowledge Distillation via Flow-based VC (Piotrowski et al., 2023) pushes this to polyglot synthesis: a text-conditioned many-to-many Flow-TTS VC teacher renders foreign-language utterances in a single target voice, and a FastSpeech 2 student trained on that synthetic speech beats a large multilingual baseline (es-MX MUSHRA naturalness 69.60 vs 64.08). Later refinements add a singing-voice-conversion source plus style-classifier filtering and F0 matching (Marques et al., 2024), and a learned style encoder trained with timbre perturbation and prototypical angular loss to prevent identity leakage (Ueda et al., 2024). Voice Filter (Gabrys et al., 2022) reframes 1-minute few-shot TTS as VC post-processing, using a duration-controllable TTS to synthesize time-aligned parallel source data. Modelling Low-Resource Accents (Tinchev et al., 2023) converts scarce accented data into a donor voice so a related-variant frontend can be reused.

#### Accent control and conversion without accented corpora

A second thread synthesizes the parallel accented/native pairs that L2 corpora lack. MacST (Inoue et al., 2024) has an LLM transliterate English so multilingual TTS renders it accented; Accent conversion with discrete units (Nguyen et al., 2024) generates pairs with the controllable SYNTACC TTS and maps to native HuBERT units (Accentedness 4.46, Speaker-Similarity 3.89). Improving Pronunciation and Accent Conversion (Nguyen et al., 2024) instead synthesizes native-pronunciation ground truth that preserves source duration/prosody, distilling a native TTS teacher into a VITS student. Google's Training TTS From Synthetic Data (Finkelstein et al., 2022) is the canonical distillation case: an unstable Tacotron accent-transfer teacher generates the entire training set for a robust CHiVE-BERT student. Disentanglement approaches avoid generated data entirely: Multi-Scale Accent Modeling (Zhou et al., 2024) and RADTTS (Badlani et al., 2023) factorize speaker/language/accent to render unseen combinations, while Scalable Controllable Accented TTS (Li Xinyuan et al., 2025) pseudo-labels accents with a speech-geolocation model and augments timbre via kNN-VC. The newest twist applies task arithmetic: Accent Vector (Lertpetchpun et al., 2026) fine-tunes XTTS-v2 on native non-English speech via LoRA, then adds the scaled weight delta to induce a foreign accent on English (+1021% relative Hindi-accent probability), and Task Vector in TTS (Feng et al., 2025) injects dialect and emotion vectors at different F5-TTS layers for emotional dialectal speech without jointly-labeled data.

#### Sampling novel speakers and identities

Speaker Generation / TacoSpawn (Stanton et al., 2021) fits a parametric mixture prior over the speaker-embedding space and samples brand-new voices; Gender-Ambiguous Voices (Markopoulos et al., 2022) samples a kernel-density region between male and female speakers; INSIDE (Liu et al., 2025) slerp-interpolates real embeddings to fabricate identities for SV augmentation (3-5% SV gains). Spark-TTS (Wang et al., 2025) decouples a fixed-length global timbre token from semantic tokens, letting a Qwen2.5 LLM create virtual voices from attribute specs.

#### Multilingual bootstrapping from unpaired data

Where no transcribed data exists, generation happens inside training loops. The machine speech chain (Tjandra et al., 2018) and LRSpeech (Xu et al., 2020) use ASR<->TTS dual transformation, each producing pseudo-paired data for the other; Unsupervised TTS (Ni et al., 2022) pseudo-labels speech with wav2vec-U; Virtuoso (Saeki et al., 2022) and its 100+-language successor (Saeki et al., 2024) learn shared speech-text representations so untranscribed audio and unspoken text substitute for transcripts (CER gap <10% with zero transcribed data, <=1% with 15 minutes). XTTS (Casanova et al., 2024) and Casanova et al. (2022) supply the dominant zero-shot multilingual backbone now used as a generator across this category. LLM-to-Speech (Khamis et al., 2026) and SpeechWeave (Dua et al., 2025) close the loop with fully synthetic LLM-text-plus-TTS pipelines for dialectal and multilingual corpora. The arc moves from VC-based pair fabrication and speech-chain pseudo-labeling toward LLM-driven text generation, foundation-model zero-shot synthesis, and lightweight task-vector recombination, with disentanglement the common substrate.

### 3.7 C7

**Corpora partially or fully generated synthetically**

This category collects work where the training corpus itself, rather than just a downstream augmentation trick, is synthetically generated. Two distinct senses of "synthetic" recur: synthetic *audio* (waveforms produced by a teacher TTS or voice-conversion model) and synthetic *annotation* (machine-generated transcripts, style captions, or attribute labels attached to real audio). The papers below span both, and the chronology shows a steady move from small distillation corpora toward web-scale pseudo-labeled and LLM-authored datasets.

#### Synthetic audio: teacher-to-student distillation corpora

The earliest thread treats a teacher TTS purely as a data generator. **TTS-by-TTS (Hwang et al., 2020)** uses an autoregressive Tacotron-style teacher to synthesize 179h of text-waveform-duration triples from a 5h real seed, then trains a non-autoregressive FastSpeech 2 student with Parallel WaveGAN, lifting MOS by roughly 40% under low-data conditions. The key insight is that the teacher supplies the phoneme durations the non-AR student needs. **Training TTS from Synthetic Data (Finkelstein et al., 2022)** generalizes this to capability transfer: a less-robust accent-transfer Tacotron generates a fully synthetic corpus that trains a stable CHiVE-BERT student, distilling accent-transfer ability into a production model.

By 2025 this matured into systematic study. **Training TTS with Purely Synthetic Data (Zhou et al., 2025)** trains a 20.9M-param Matcha-TTS (flow-matching, OT-CFM) student entirely on speech from three teachers (XTTS-v2, CosyVoice2, ChatTTS), and ablates text richness, speaker count (up to 1,000), noise, and style. Notably the XTTS-v2-trained student *beats* real LibriSpeech on WER (3.493 vs 4.428) at comparable UTMOS/DNSMOS, establishing that purely synthetic training can match or exceed real data when diversity and cleanliness are controlled. **BELLE (Zhang et al., 2025)** pushes the multi-teacher idea further: it queries several teachers on the same prompts to build a one-to-many "support set," then distills the resulting output variance into a single LM-based synthesizer via Bayesian evidential learning (Normal-Inverse-Gamma head), achieving a 25.8% relative WER reduction over models trained on 10x more data.

#### Fully synthetic corpora for new languages, domains, and emotions

A second strand builds entire corpora where no real transcribed data exists. **LLM-to-Speech (Khamis and Ali, 2026)** chains LLM text generation, audio synthesis, ASR transcription, and diarization to build the 38h Egyptian-Arabic NileTTS corpus for fine-tuning XTTS v2. **SpeechWeave (Dua et al., 2025)** is fully synthetic end-to-end: Mistral-7B writes domain text, MeloTTS synthesizes it, and OpenVoiceV2 standardizes the speaker, yielding data 10-48% more diverse than baseline LLM generation, used downstream to fine-tune StyleTTS 2. For emotion, **EmoVoice (Yang et al., 2025)** generates EmoVoice-DB (40.45h, 22,100 samples) by prompting GPT-4o for transcripts plus free-form emotion descriptions and GPT-4o-audio for the speech, WER-filtered; a Qwen2.5 backbone over CosyVoice tokens reaches emotion similarity 0.91 versus CosyVoice2's 0.86. **Emo-LiPO (Lin et al., 2026)** builds ESD-plus (45,500 samples, 13 intensity labels) with gpt-4o-mini-tts and casts intensity control as listwise ranking.

#### Synthetic annotation of real audio

The largest-scale work synthesizes *labels*, not waveforms. **Natural Language Guidance (Lyth and King, 2024)**, the basis for Parler-TTS, auto-annotates 45k+ hours with classifier-derived attributes (gender, accent, SNR, pitch, rate), quantizes them, and uses Stable Beluga to write natural-language descriptions, enabling text-prompt control. **TextrolSpeech (Ji et al., 2023)**, **VoxInstruct (Zhou et al., 2024)**, and **ParaSpeechCaps (Diwan et al., 2025)** similarly use GPT-class models to author style prompts or 59-tag captions over real speech; **OV-InstructTTS (Ren et al., 2026)** extends this to LLM-generated reasoning chains (Qwen3-32B, DeepSeek-R1) as SFT targets. **Emilia (He et al., 2024; 2025)** typifies pseudo-labeling at scale: the Emilia-Pipe (source separation, pyannote diarization, Silero-VAD, Whisper-medium ASR, DNSMOS filtering) turns 101k-216k hours of in-the-wild audio into annotated training data, capturing spontaneous speech absent from audiobooks. **Spark-TTS (Wang et al., 2025)** combines machine attribute labels (VoxBox) with the ability to sample entirely new synthetic speaker identities via global tokens.

#### Synthetic speakers and unsupervised labels

Finally, **INSIDE (Liu et al., 2025)** generates wholly synthetic *identities* by spherically interpolating real speaker embeddings and rendering them via TTS, improving speaker verification by 3-5% relative. **Unsupervised Speech Synthesis (Liu et al., 2022)** removes human labels entirely: wav2vec-U 2.0 pseudo-transcribes untranscribed audio, and a standard TTS trains on the machine-annotated pairs, matching supervised naturalness. Together these mark the field's trajectory from hand-built distillation sets to corpora where text, labels, audio, and even speaker identity are machine-originated.

---

## 4. Replication & recipes

### REPLICATION & RECIPES: Synthetic Data for TTS

A practitioner's field guide, grouped by approach. Every repo/dataset cited appears in the survey's paper set.

#### (A) Distillation / training on TTS-generated speech

**Canonical pipeline.** A strong teacher TTS (or several) synthesizes a corpus that a smaller/faster student trains on. The student inherits the teacher's behavior while gaining stability, speed, or compression.

```
1. pick teacher(s)  -> 2. synthesize corpus from text + speaker conds
3. extract durations/alignments from teacher  -> 4. train student
5. eval WER / UTMOS / SIM, ablate each synthetic factor
```

**Key knobs.** Utterance count (match real reference), speaker diversity, text richness, speaking style, added noise. TTS-by-TTS scaled 5h real to 179h synthetic with teacher-extracted phoneme durations; "Purely Synthetic Data" trained Matcha-TTS from scratch (Adam, lr 1e-4, bs 32, 100 epochs) and varied each factor independently up to 1000 speakers.

**QC / filtering.** WER between synthesized audio and target text is the standard gate. Multi-teacher distillation (BELLE) uses several teachers per prompt to learn output variance via Bayesian evidential (NIG) loss.

**Pitfalls.** Teacher artifacts propagate; a single teacher's style bias dominates the student; matching real utterance count without text diversity caps gains.

**Repos.** Nix-TTS (`github.com/rendchevi/nix-tts`), BELLE (`github.com/OpenTSLab/BELLE`), DMOSpeech2 (`dmospeech2.github.io`), Spark-TTS (`github.com/SparkAudio/Spark-TTS`).

#### (B) LLM-generated text corpora & data engines

**Canonical pipeline.** An LLM writes diverse text (or rich style descriptions), a TTS engine voices it, and a downstream model trains on the pairs. Two flavors: synthesize the speech too (end-to-end synthetic), or auto-annotate real speech with machine-written captions.

```
1. LLM generates text/instructions (domain, emotion, style)
2. TTS synthesizes audio  -> 3. ASR + WER filter  -> 4. train/fine-tune
```

**Key knobs.** Domain/sentence-type coverage, semiotic-class handling (numbers, dates), description length (15-25 words for EmoVoice). SpeechWeave uses multi-step prompting with fuzzy dedup; diversity tracked via diphone coverage, LaBSE similarity, MATTR.

**QC / filtering.** WER filter between synthesized audio and intended transcript (EmoVoice-DB), text-normalization correctness (~97% in SpeechWeave), human verification (NileTTS, ESD-plus monotonic-intensity check).

**Pitfalls.** Mode collapse in LLM text (low lexical diversity), normalization errors on numbers/acronyms, style descriptions that don't actually map to acoustics.

**Repos/datasets.** EmoVoice (`github.com/yanghaha0908/EmoVoice`, dataset `huggingface.co/datasets/yhaha/EmoVoice-DB`), Parler-TTS + Data-Speech (`github.com/huggingface/parler-tts`, `github.com/huggingface/dataspeech`), ParaSpeechCaps (`github.com/ajd12342/paraspeechcaps`), TextrolSpeech (`github.com/jishengpeng/TextrolSpeech`), VoxInstruct (`github.com/thuhcsi/VoxInstruct`), PromptTTS2 (`github.com/microsoft/NeuralSpeech`).

#### (C) Preference optimization & RL (DPO/RLHF/GRPO)

**Canonical pipeline.** The base TTS generates candidates per prompt; automatic scorers (or humans) rank them; a preference loss updates the policy. No external corpus required.

```
for each text prompt:
    sample G candidates (vary sampling/speaker prompt)
    score: WER(ASR) + SIM(spk-verif) + UTMOS [+ prosody/duration]
    GRPO: group-relative advantage, KL to ref
    DPO:  top-k chosen / bottom-k rejected pairs
```

**Key knobs.** Group size G (8 is common; 16 for duration RL), KL beta (0.1 GRPO, 0.01 DPO), reward weights (LLM-TTS-GRPO used lambda_c=0.6 CER + lambda_n=0.4 NLL), margin tau for pair selection (0.3 in FPO), candidate count (10 in MPO, 6 in Koel-TTS). lr is tiny: 1e-5 to 2e-6.

**QC / filtering.** Pareto ranking across metrics (Koel-TTS), composite-reward gap thresholds, reverse-inference self-consistency (RIO), token-level error localization (FPO, TKTO weights problem tokens ~12.8x).

**Pitfalls.** Reward hacking (RRPO hardens its SER reward against acoustic artifacts); prosody collapse to monotone when optimizing CER/NLL alone ("No Verifiable Reward for Prosody" fixes this with iterative human-labeled DPO, moving reference per round); RL alone underperforms without SFT first (GSRM). DMOSpeech2 / Align2Speak generate rollouts online rather than storing a corpus.

**Repos/datasets.** F5R-TTS (`github.com/FrontierLabs/F5R-TTS`), LLM-TTS-GRPO (`ryuclc.github.io/LLM-TTS-GRPO`), SpeechAlign (`github.com/0nutation/SpeechGPT`), INTP (`huggingface.co/datasets/amphion/INTP`), Koel-TTS in NeMo (`github.com/NVIDIA/NeMo`), Emo-LiPO (`github.com/hlt-cuhksz/Emo-LiPO`), preference alignment in ESPnet (`github.com/espnet/espnet`), SpeechJudge GRM (`github.com/AmphionTeam/SpeechJudge`).

#### (D) Synthetic speaker generation & augmentation

**Canonical pipeline.** Sample or interpolate new speaker embeddings, then synthesize speech for nonexistent identities to expand training data.

```
1. learn a speaker-embedding prior / collect real embeddings
2. sample (TacoSpawn prior) or slerp between pairs (INSIDE)
3. synthesize audio with multi-speaker TTS
4. add to downstream SV / TTS / gender-classifier training
```

**Key knobs.** Embedding sampling distribution, interpolation pairs (nearby identities give plausible voices), synthetic:real ratio. ZeSTA's key finding: naive mixing degrades speaker similarity, so tag synthetic vs real with a domain embedding and oversample real.

**QC / filtering.** Domain-embedding conditioning (ZeSTA), channel/dialect codes to factor out recording artifacts (speaker-augmentation Tacotron2), gender-ambiguity density sampling.

**Pitfalls.** Too much synthetic data hurts (Synth2Aug sweeps the ratio); text-content match beats vocabulary diversity for SV.

**Repos.** No code for TacoSpawn / INSIDE / gender-ambiguous voices, but the gender-ambiguous samples page exists (`innoetics.github.io`). Generators like XTTS (`github.com/coqui-ai/TTS`) and VoxCPM (`github.com/OpenBMB/VoxCPM`) are the practical synthesis backbones.

#### (E) Self-training / pseudo-labeling / back-translation

**Canonical pipeline.** Bootstrap from unpaired data. ASR pseudo-labels audio, TTS voices text, and the two close a loop (speech chain / dual transformation), or one pass generates pseudo-transcripts to train TTS.

```
ASR pseudo-labels unlabeled speech  -> train TTS on (audio, pseudo-text)
TTS voices text corpus -> (text, synth-speech) -> fine-tune ASR -> iterate
```

**Key knobs.** Paired-seed size (as little as 20 min in "Almost Unsupervised"; 4 utterances + 15 min in SSL-mixing), intelligibility threshold over naturalness (Speech Back-Translation gates synthetic by ASR score, scales to 500k+ h), cycle-loss weights, speaker-consistency loss.

**QC / filtering.** Intelligibility-based filtering not naturalness; DNSMOS + language-ID + duration filters (Emilia-Pipe: 6 stages, conf>80%, DNSMOS>3.0). Replace noisy pseudo-labels with SSL features (representation mixing).

**Pitfalls.** Error accumulation in long sequences (bidirectional decoding mitigates); synthetic speech too easy for ASR if it ignores speaker traits (add speaker-consistency loss + freeze TTS step-wise); pseudo-transcript quality is the bottleneck for unsupervised TTS.

**Repos.** Emilia pipeline + dataset (`github.com/open-mmlab/Amphion`, `huggingface.co/datasets/amphion/Emilia-Dataset`), UnsupTTS (`github.com/lwang114/UnsupTTS`), unsupervised TTS project (`people.csail.mit.edu/clai24/unsup-tts`), semi-TTS discrete-rep (`github.com/ttaoREtw/semi-tts`), MSMC-TTS (`github.com/hhguo/MSMC-TTS`).

#### (F) Accent / style / multilingual synthetic data

**Canonical pipeline.** Manufacture parallel accented<->native data with voice conversion or controllable accented TTS, or compose accent/style via task vectors. Also: pseudo-label accent from geolocation.

```
1. VC converts donor expressive/accented speech -> target voice
   OR controllable TTS (SYNTACC) generates accented pairs
   OR LLM transliterates text to induce accent (MacST)
2. style-filter + F0-match the converted audio
3. train style/accent-transfer TTS on real + synthetic
```

**Key knobs.** Donor selection, VC quality, accent-vector scale alpha (Accent Vector: LoRA delta added at inference, sweep alpha), task-vector layer placement (early DiT = dialect, late = emotion). Geolocation pseudo-labels expand accent classes beyond self-reported (Scalable Controllable Accented TTS).

**QC / filtering.** Style classifier filtering + F0 matching (singing-voice augmentation), DNSMOS>3.4 + duration>=3s (Accent Vector), oversample real to recover quality.

**Pitfalls.** Teacher accent-transfer models are unstable (distill to a robust student, CHiVE-BERT); pitch mismatch between donor and target needs F0 normalization; naive cross-style mixing needs disentanglement.

**Repos/datasets.** MacST (`github.com/shinshoji01/MacST-project-page`), AffectSpeech (`github.com/jeremychee4/AffectSpeech`), EmoCorrector + ECD-TSE (`github.com/AI-S2-Lab/EmoCorrector`), SelfTTS (`github.com/AI-Unicamp/SelfTTS`), YourTTS (`github.com/Edresson/YourTTS`).

#### Minimal end-to-end blueprint

A low-resource expressive TTS in one target language, combining (B), (E), (C):

```
1. CORPUS: build an Emilia-style pipeline on in-the-wild target-language
   audio: standardize 24kHz -> source-separate -> diarize -> VAD 3-30s
   -> Whisper-medium ASR -> filter (lang-conf>0.8, DNSMOS>3.0).
2. AUGMENT TEXT: prompt an LLM for domain/emotion-rich transcripts;
   voice them with an off-the-shelf TTS (XTTS-v2 / CosyVoice2).
3. FILTER: keep only utterances with WER < 0.20 vs intended text
   (regenerate up to ~10x); cap synthetic ratio near 50% to avoid
   prosodic collapse (the "Synthetic Erosion" finding).
4. SFT: fine-tune the base TTS on real + filtered synthetic.
5. ALIGN: generate G=8 candidates per prompt; score WER(Whisper) +
   SIM(spk-verif) + UTMOS; run GRPO (KL beta 0.1, lr 1e-5) or DPO
   (top-20% chosen / bottom-20% rejected, beta 0.01).
6. EVAL: WER, UTMOS, SIM, plus a hard out-of-domain set.
```

Each stage degrades gracefully if the next is skipped. Watch the two universal failure modes: synthetic-ratio-driven prosody collapse (monitor token entropy and 4-gram repetition rate) and reward hacking during alignment (harden reward models, keep a KL anchor to the reference policy).

---

## 5. Trends, open problems & future directions

### TRENDS, OPEN PROBLEMS, AND FUTURE DIRECTIONS

#### The chronological arc

Synthetic data for TTS has moved through four overlapping eras. The earliest work treated synthesis as **augmentation and semi-supervised bootstrapping**. Cycle-consistency and speech-chain methods (*Listening while Speaking*, 2017; *Machine Speech Chain*, 2018) closed an ASR-TTS loop, while *Almost Unsupervised Text to Speech and ASR* (2019), *LRSpeech* (2020), and *Semi-Supervised Training for Improving Data Efficiency* (2018) showed that paired data could be stretched with unpaired text and audio. Synthetic speech was used to feed recognizers (*SynthASR*, 2021; *Speech Recognition with Augmented Synthesized Speech*, 2019) and to expand speaker inventories (*Speaker Generation*, 2021; *Can Speaker Augmentation Improve Multi-Speaker TTS?*, 2020). Unsupervised synthesis (*Simple and Effective Unsupervised Speech Synthesis*, 2022; *QS-TTS*, 2023) pushed the paired-data floor toward zero.

The second era is the **codec and LLM-based data engine**. Discrete tokens and large pipelines turned synthesis into a scalable corpus generator: *Emilia* (2024/2025) and *SpeechWeave* (2025) industrialized in-the-wild data curation, *Natural Language Guidance with Synthetic Annotations* (2024) and *Scaling Rich Style-Prompted Datasets* (2025) attached descriptive labels at scale, and systems such as *Seed-TTS* (2024), *Spark-TTS* (2025), *CosyVoice 3* (2025), and the 2026 technical reports (*Qwen3-TTS*, *Fish Audio S2*, *IndexTTS 2.5*) consumed and re-emitted synthetic corpora as a default ingredient. *Training TTS with Purely Synthetic Data* (2025) probed the limit case directly.

The third era is **preference optimization and RL**, where synthetic candidates become training signal rather than ground truth. From *SpeechAlign* and *Preference Alignment Improves LM-Based TTS* (2024) through DPO variants (*Emo-DPO*, 2024; *Fine-grained Preference Optimization*, 2025; *MPO*, *TKTO*, *Emo-LiPO*) and GRPO-style methods (*F5R-TTS*, *GRPO for TTS with LLMs*, *Multi-Reward GRPO*, 2025), the field learned to rank its own outputs. ASR- and metric-grounded rewards (*Align2Speak*, *DMOSpeech*/*DMOSpeech 2*, *Differentiable Reward Optimization*, *Reverse Inference Optimization*) gave verifiable objectives, while reward models matured toward human-level judgment (*GSRM*, 2026; *SpeechJudge*, 2025).

The fourth, emerging era is **agentic and self-improving data loops**: *A Self-Refining Framework for Enhancing ASR Using TTS-Synthesized Data* (2025), *TTSOps* (2025) optimizing corpora from dark data, *SelfTTS* (2026), and *TokenChain* (2025) revive the speech chain inside modern token spaces, closing generation, filtering, and retraining into one cycle.

#### Recurring open problems

- **Quality and filtering.** Naive synthetic data injects systematic artifacts. Style-filtering and F0-matching work (*Improving Cross-Speaker Style Transfer*, 2024) and active selection (*Active Learning for TTS*, 2025; *TTSOps*) show filtering matters as much as generation, but no general quality metric for synthetic corpora exists.
- **Model collapse.** Recursive training on self-generated speech risks distributional narrowing. *Training TTS with Purely Synthetic Data* surfaces sensitivity and generalization concerns; the field still lacks collapse diagnostics analogous to those in text LLMs.
- **Evaluation of synthetic corpora.** Downstream task gain is the de facto metric. *InstructTTSEval* (2025) and *SpeechJudge* point toward richer evaluation, but diversity, coverage, and bias of a corpus remain hard to measure intrinsically.
- **Licensing and ethics of cloned voices.** Zero-shot cloning and instruction-driven voice creation (*PromptTTS 2*, *VoxInstruct*, *FlexiVoice*) raise consent and provenance questions that current datasets rarely document.
- **Bias.** Synthetic pipelines can amplify accent, gender, and dialect imbalance even while claiming to fix it (*Generating Gender-Ambiguous Voices*, 2022; dialectal and accent work such as *LLM-to-Speech*, 2026; *Accent Vector*, 2026).

#### Future directions

Concrete near-term targets: **verifiable rewards beyond intelligibility**, since *No Verifiable Reward for Prosody* (2025) and *RLAIF-SPA* (2025) expose that prosody and emotion still lack groundable signals; **self-improving loops with collapse guardrails** that mix real anchors with synthetic expansion (*Bridging the Stability-Expressivity Gap*, 2026); **low-resource and dialectal coverage** via online preference optimization (*Align2Speak*) and synthetic dialectal engines (*SoulX-Podcast*, 2025); **conversational and long-form synthetic data** (*FireRedTTS-2*, *MOSS-TTSD*, *MoonCast*); **provenance-aware corpora** that record consent, licensing, and synthetic origin as first-class metadata; and **unified reward models** (*GSRM*, *SpeechJudge*) that let agentic data loops filter, rank, and regenerate within a single trustable objective. The throughline is closing the loop while keeping a real-data anchor, so that synthetic scale does not quietly erode the diversity it was meant to create.

---

## 6. Full annotated paper catalog

Replication-grade entry per paper. Fields read "not reported" where the source did not state them.

### 1. Accent Vector: Controllable Accent Manipulation for Multilingual TTS Without Accented Data

_Thanathai Lertpetchpun, Thanapat Trachu, Jihwan Lee, Tiantian Feng, Dani Byrd, Shrikanth Narayanan (USC) · 2026 · arXiv preprint (submitted March 8, 2026)_

- **Categories:** C2, C6
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.07534) · arXiv:2603.07534 · [code](https://anonymous.4open.science/w/Accent-Vector-Audio-Samples-0EAA/)
- **Synthetic-data role:** No accented training data is used at all. Instead the method fine-tunes a TTS model on NATIVE speech of a non-English language and extracts a task vector (weight delta). At inference this 'accent vector' is added to the base English model to synthetically induce a foreign accent on English speech. The synthetic/generated artifact is the accented output speech and the mixed-accent speech produced by scaling/interpolating vectors; no accented corpus is recorded or generated as a dataset.
- **Method:** Apply task-vector (task arithmetic) ideas to accent control. Fine-tune a multilingual TTS on native speech of language X, compute the accent task vector as the difference between fine-tuned and pretrained weights, then add a scaled version of this vector back to the base model to inject an X-accent into English (or other languages) speech. Scaling factor alpha controls accent strength; weighted addition of multiple vectors yields mixed-accent speech. Achieves controllable, cross-lingual accent manipulation without ever training on accented (L2) data.
- **Architecture:** Backbone is XTTS-v2 (multilingual zero-shot TTS, 17 languages): VQ-VAE mel encoder-decoder codec + attention-based GPT-style conditioning over reference speech and text + HiFi-GAN-style decoder. Accent vector implemented as LoRA (rank 16) adapters, so the task vector equals the LoRA weights (~8M trainable params out of 378M).
- **Base models:** XTTS-v2 (Coqui) as the frozen base TTS; LoRA used for parameter-efficient fine-tuning. Evaluation models: VoxProfile accent classifier, Whisper-medium ASR, UTMOS MOS predictor, wavlm-base-plus-sv speaker verification, a language-ID model.
- **Datasets:** Native, non-accented speech per language: English VCTK (8.9 h, UTMOS 3.92); Spanish CommonVoice Peninsular (52.21 h); Hindi IndicVoices-R (27.2 h); German CommonVoice (375.12 h); French CommonVoice (337.56 h); Mandarin KeSpeech (125.53 h). Filtering: DNSMOS > 3.4 and utterance duration >= 3 s.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Take pretrained XTTS-v2 (theta_pre). 2) For each target accent language, LoRA-fine-tune (rank 16, Adam, lr 3e-5, 60k steps, ~8 GPU-hours on a single A40) on native speech of that language to get theta_ft. 3) Accent task vector tau_accent = theta_ft - theta_pre (with LoRA, tau is just the LoRA weights). 4) At inference build theta_accent = theta_pre + alpha * tau_accent; sweep alpha to set accent strength; combine accents via sum of weighted vectors for mixed accents. 5) Generate English (or other-language) text with the modified weights to obtain accented speech. 6) Evaluate accentedness (VoxProfile softmax+cosine, LID for cross-lingual), WER/CER (Whisper-medium), naturalness (UTMOS), speaker similarity (wavlm-sv cosine), plus 16-listener human study.
- **Key results:** British English: 56.7% target-accent probability vs 23.3% baseline. Hindi-accented English: +1021% relative accent probability (+24.2 pts). Speaker similarity preserved ~0.86-0.90 across accents. WER 5.46%-34.4% depending on accent pair. Human accent-identification accuracy 53.85%-80%. Baselines: MacST (transliteration), Onda et al. (prosody), Lertpetchpun et al. (phoneme rules) - Accent Vector adds explicit strength control, cross-accent mixing, and multilingual support.
- **Notes:** Key novelty: task arithmetic / model-merging applied to accent, requiring zero accented data. Builds directly on XTTS-v2, so licensing inherits Coqui CPML constraints. 2026 paper; arxiv ID in the 2603 (March 2026) range, consistent with the submission date.

### 2. AffectSpeech: A Large-Scale Emotional Speech Dataset with Fine-Grained Textual Descriptions for Speech Emotion Captioning and Synthesis

_Includes jeremychee4 et al. (full author list not confirmed from sources) · 2026 · not reported (arXiv preprint)_

- **Categories:** C4, C6
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.04160) · arXiv:2604.04160 · [code](https://github.com/jeremychee4/AffectSpeech)
- **Synthetic-data role:** Speech is REAL (human-recorded, aggregated from existing corpora); the synthetic component is the textual annotation layer. 1,522,794 fine-grained natural-language descriptions are generated by a human-LLM collaborative pipeline: algorithmic pre-labeling extracts prosody/emotion attributes, then multiple multimodal LLMs (GPT-based and Qwen-based MLLMs) generate multi-aspect captions conditioned on the waveform and metadata. An LLM adjudicator auto-approves 64.9% of samples; the remaining 35.1% are human-verified (28.9% via selection, 6.2% via correction/regeneration) by five expert annotators. So the dataset is a real-speech corpus with LLM-generated (machine-authored, partially human-curated) emotion/prosody captions across six description styles.
- **Method:** Construct a large-scale emotional-speech-to-text-description dataset by aggregating authentic emotional speech and enriching each utterance with multi-dimensional LLM-generated descriptions (sentiment polarity, open-vocabulary emotion caption, intensity, prosody, prominent segments, semantic content) in six stylistic formats, to support both Speech Emotion Captioning (SEC) and controllable Emotional Speech Synthesis (ESS). Validate by fine-tuning audio LLMs (SEC) and an instruction TTS model (ESS).
- **Architecture:** Dataset construction pipeline: algorithmic prosody/emotion pre-labeler + multi-MLLM caption generators (GPT-based, Qwen-based) + LLM adjudicator + human-in-the-loop verification. SEC models: Qwen2-Audio and Qwen2.5-Omni fine-tuned with LoRA (rank 16, alpha 32, dropout 0.05). ESS model: VoxInstruct backbone fine-tuned with LoRA (rank 8, alpha 16).
- **Base models:** Qwen2-Audio, Qwen2.5-Omni (SEC); VoxInstruct (ESS); GPT-based and Qwen-based MLLMs as annotation generators; standard prosody-extraction tools for pre-labeling.
- **Datasets:** AffectSpeech: 253,799 authentic English utterances, 1,522,794 descriptions, 9 emotions (angry, disgust, fear, happy, neutral, sad, surprise, contempt, calm). Source speech aggregated from SAVEE, RAVDESS, eNTERFACE, TESS, CREMA-D, ESD, MEAD, MESS, plus VCTK and LibriTTS subsets; standardized to 16 kHz mono WAV. Splits: 252,999 train, 800 held-out test.
- **Code:** https://github.com/jeremychee4/AffectSpeech
- **Recipe:** Dataset: 1) collect/clean speech from 10 source corpora to 16 kHz mono; 2) algorithmically pre-label pitch/tempo/energy and emotion; 3) prompt multiple MLLMs (GPT, Qwen) to generate six-style descriptions conditioned on audio+metadata; 4) LLM adjudicator extracts keypoints and auto-approves high-consistency samples (64.9%), routing the rest to 5 expert human annotators. SEC: fine-tune Qwen2-Audio / Qwen2.5-Omni with LoRA (r=16, alpha=32, dropout=0.05), 3 epochs, AdamW, peak LR 1e-5, 5% warmup + cosine decay, batch size 96 on RTX PRO 6000. ESS: fine-tune VoxInstruct with LoRA (r=8, alpha=16), same optimizer/epoch settings.
- **Key results:** SEC (Qwen2.5-Omni fine-tuned): emotion-category accuracy 73.25% (vs 70.13% zero-shot), intensity precision 45.75%, pitch accuracy 46.75%, tempo accuracy 65.50%; subjective preference 62.50% emotion / 68.75% prosody; Distinct-2 ~0.4, Self-BLEU ~0.67. Beats EmoVoice-DB and TextrolSpeech baselines. ESS (VoxInstruct-SFT): WER 4.36%, emotion similarity 86.40%, emotion diversity 19.91e-4, MOS naturalness 3.81+-0.25 (GT 4.31+-0.22), top perceptual ratings.
- **Notes:** arXiv id 2604.04160 implies April 2026; this is a recent/forward-dated preprint (today is 2026-06-23). Speech licensing inherits from 10 underlying corpora (mixed academic licenses). Captions are machine-generated and only ~35% human-touched, so annotation noise possible. Dataset/demos on GitHub.

### 3. Bridging the Stability-Expressivity Gap: Synthetic Data Scaling and Preference Alignment for Low-Resource Spoken Language Models

_Yizhong Geng, Yanliang Li, Jinghan Yang, Tianhan Jiang, Boxun An, Ya Li, Xiaoyu Shen · 2026 · ICML 2026 (Proc. 43rd ICML)_

- **Categories:** C1, C2, C3, C5, C6, C7
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27383) · arXiv:2605.27383 · [code](https://luoji.cn/static/multilantts-demo-main/)
- **Synthetic-data role:** Central. Off-the-shelf TTS systems (MMS-TTS, Seamless-M4T-v2, Typhoon2-Audio) generate large synthetic speech corpora to train SLMs in low-resource languages: Thai uses 300h real (Common Voice, ASR-filtered) + 1,200h synthetic; Lao uses 1,500h synthetic only (no real corpus). The paper diagnoses 'Synthetic Erosion': beyond ~50% synthetic ratio, phonetic accuracy improves but prosodic variability collapses (token entropy falls, repetition rises to 9.83% at 100% synthetic). Self-generated outputs then build DPO preference pairs.
- **Method:** Identifies a Stability-Expressivity Gap when scaling synthetic data for low-resource spoken LMs, then proposes two self-alignment frameworks to recover expressivity: DGSA (Disentanglement-Guided Self-Alignment) for complex languages with some real data, and TDSC (Temperature-Driven Self-Critique) for languages with no real speech.
- **Architecture:** Built on CosyVoice 2: a Text-Speech LM producing content/prosody tokens plus a Flow-Matching Transformer handling speaker timbre via embeddings (prosody-timbre disentanglement). Speech tokenized to discrete tokens via S3Tokenizer. Alignment stage uses DPO.
- **Base models:** CosyVoice 2 (flow-matching SLM). Synthetic-data teachers: MMS-TTS, Seamless-M4T-v2, Typhoon2-Audio. ASR for filtering/eval: Whisper-large-v3 (Thai), Dolphin-small (Lao).
- **Datasets:** Thai: 300h real (Common Voice, ASR-filtered) + 1,200h synthetic. Lao: 1,500h synthetic only. Human eval by 20 native speakers per language (double-blind within-subject).
- **Code:** Audio samples: https://luoji.cn/static/multilantts-demo-main/ ; a GitHub repo InsiderX-Pro/SE-Bridge-TTS appears in search (provenance unconfirmed); no official code URL stated in the paper.
- **Recipe:** Three stages: (1) SFT on mixed real+synthetic corpus via maximum likelihood; (2) frozen model generates dual outputs; (3) DPO preference alignment. DGSA (languages with real data): exploit disentanglement to generate an expressive output (with style conditioning) and a stable output (without), both preserving speaker identity, then auto-build preference pairs T_stab:(real, expressive-but-erroneous) and T_expr:(real, stable-but-flat); dynamic weight lambda_e = max(0,(alpha-alpha*)/(1-alpha*)) with alpha* ~ 50%. TDSC (no real data): multi-temperature exploration T in {low,mid,high} generates candidates, strict ASR filtering (WER<tau_w, repetition<tau_r, length in [gamma_min|x|, gamma_max|x|]), then iterative SFT on accepted samples followed by DPO on filtered pairs with a temperature curriculum. Diagnostics: token entropy H_p, repetition rate (k=4 consecutive tokens).
- **Key results:** Thai: DGSA NMOS 4.42 vs SFT 3.61 at matched WER 38.9%, token entropy 10.52 vs 10.36 bits; outperforms ElevenLabs (NMOS 4.2) and Azure (WER 36.5%, NMOS 4.0) on zero-shot cloning. Lao (TDSC, 100% synthetic): WER 29.8% vs 38.5% baseline, NMOS 3.94 vs 3.12, surpassing Gemini Flash and Azure. H_p correlates strongly with expressivity MOS (+0.5 MOS for high-H_p at matched WER).
- **Notes:** Future-dated arXiv ID (2605) consistent with ICML 2026. Confirmed via arXiv abs/HTML. Key concept: 'Synthetic Erosion' and the ~50% synthetic-ratio inflection point. Spans many categories: trains/fine-tunes on synthetic corpora (1,2,7), self-training/pseudo-labeling/ASR filtering (5), DPO alignment (3), multilingual/low-resource synthesis (6).

### 4. CosyEdit: Unlocking End-to-End Speech Editing Capability from Zero-Shot Text-to-Speech Models

_Junyang Chen, Yuhang Jia, Hui Wang, Jiaming Zhou, Yaxin Han, Mengying Feng, Yong Qin · 2026 · arXiv preprint_

- **Categories:** C2, C4, C7
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2601.05329) · arXiv:2601.05329
- **Synthetic-data role:** The GigaEdit training set (250 h) is synthetically constructed by rule-based, deterministic transformation of real GigaSpeech-S audio into paired (original, target) editing examples. Using Montreal Forced Aligner timestamps, segments are removed/reinserted to simulate insertion, deletion, substitution, and multi-edit operations. No learned/neural generation of audio; the 'synthetic' aspect is the automatically generated editing-task supervision pairs from real speech.
- **Method:** Reformulates speech editing as autoregressive speech-token generation in which text-speech alignment is internalized, removing the need for external forced alignment. The model reuses speech tokens from regions aligned between original and target speech and predicts new tokens for non-aligned (edited) regions, adapting a zero-shot TTS model via task-specific fine-tuning plus an optimized inference procedure for high before/after consistency.
- **Architecture:** Adapts CosyVoice (~400M params). Components: text encoder; S3 supervised semantic speech tokenizer; adapted autoregressive LLM generating speech tokens conditioned on target text + original speech; NAR Guided Optimal-Transport Conditional Flow Matching (GOT-CFM) decoder conditioned on the full probability density path of the original speech to preserve fine acoustic detail. 16 kHz.
- **Base models:** CosyVoice zero-shot TTS (text encoder, S3 tokenizer, AR LM, OT-CFM flow decoder).
- **Datasets:** Real source: GigaSpeech-S. Synthetic/derived: GigaEdit (250 h supervised editing pairs built from GigaSpeech-S). Evaluation: RealEdit benchmark.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Build GigaEdit from GigaSpeech-S using MFA timestamps: Insertion (remove random segments; shortened = input, original = target); Deletion (swap roles); Substitution (delete contiguous segment, split, reinsert parts to form paired utterances); Multi-edit (delete multiple non-contiguous segments). 2) Fine-tune CosyVoice for 16 epochs on the 250 h GigaEdit, 2x A800-80G. Hyperparameters: LLM lr 3e-6, warmup 2,000 steps; flow model lr 1e-4, warmup 2,500 steps; 16 kHz. 3) Inference: one-shot in-context (original text + target text + original speech tokens) used for reported results; zero-shot in-context variant uses original speech tokens + target text only.
- **Key results:** RealEdit: WER 4.50% (vs SSR-Speech 5.05%); SpkSIM 0.9734 (vs SSR-Speech 0.9831); Edit-MOS 4.15 (vs VoiceCraft 4.04); SMOS 4.04 (vs VoiceCraft 4.08); MCD 4.94 dB. Outperforms billion-parameter LM baselines (Step-Audio-EditX, MiMo-Audio, Ming-UniAudio) and matches SOTA cascade systems with only a 400M model.
- **Notes:** Editing-pair data is algorithmically generated (forced-alignment splicing), not neural TTS; counts as corpus partially generated synthetically. Funded by Chinese national programs (National Key R&D Program 2022ZD0116307; NSFC 62271270).

### 5. CosyEdit2: Speech-Editing-Oriented Reinforcement Learning Unlocks Better Zero-Shot TTS

_not reported (corresponding author affiliated with demo author cjy1018) · 2026 · arXiv preprint_

- **Categories:** C2, C3, C6
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.25930) · arXiv:2605.25930 · [code](https://cjy1018.github.io/CosyEdit2)
- **Synthetic-data role:** GRPO training data is target-speech-free: 3,000 GigaSpeech-XL utterances are perturbed with rule-based text edits (via nlpaug) to create editing prompts with no manually recorded target audio. Stage-1 SFT uses GigaEdit-S, a 250h editing dataset synthetically derived from GigaSpeech-S. Rewards (WER from ASR, speaker-embedding cosine similarity, DTW-aligned MCD) are computed on the model's own rollouts (G=4 per prompt), so the supervision signal itself is synthetically/automatically generated rather than from paired ground-truth recordings.
- **Method:** Two-stage post-training of a CosyVoice2-style model for end-to-end speech editing: SFT for basic editing, then editing-oriented GRPO optimizing content/speaker/acoustic-preservation rewards without paired target recordings. The RL also substantially improves general zero-shot TTS.
- **Architecture:** LLM: Qwen2.5-0.5B unified text-speech autoregressive model; Flow: GOT-CFM (guided optimal-transport conditional flow matching); Vocoder: BigVGAN (replacing CosyVoice2's HiFT-GAN); BPE text tokenizer + ASR-oriented FSQ speech tokenizer.
- **Base models:** CosyVoice2 architecture (Qwen2.5-0.5B backbone). BigVGAN vocoder.
