- **Code:** https://tts.ch.dev (demo); dataset https://huggingface.co/datasets/channelcorp/KoCC-TTS-testset (training code not found)
- **Recipe:** 1) Start from Llasa-1B, continue-pretrain on 36k h Korean, fine-tune on 18 h single-speaker (this is the SFT/channel-base model). 2) GRPO baseline: reward = harmonic mean of CER and NLL terms with weights lambda_c=0.6, lambda_l=0.4 (speaker-similarity variant adds a third term, weights 0.5/0.3/0.2), trained over 1.6M text prompts; this lowers CER but degrades prosody (monotone). 3) Iterative DPO: each round sample candidate generations from current policy, collect ~200 human preference pairs labeled for prosodic naturalness, run DPO with the moving reference pi_ref = pi_{theta_{r-1}} (previous round checkpoint) so regularization tracks the latest model; preference data is not reused across rounds; repeat for 3 rounds (Round 2 is best). Beta controls preference sharpness (value not stated).
- **Key results:** On KoCC-TTS by human-preference ELO and CER: channel-base 1150.1 ELO / 2.90% CER; GRPO (clean) 753.7 ELO / 2.20% CER (lowest preference, monotone); DPO Round 2 (best) 1190.1 ELO / 3.60% CER. Baselines: Supertone 1046.9 ELO / 2.98% CER, ElevenLabs Multilingual v2 955.1 ELO / 4.74% CER, GPT-4o-mini-tts 848.9 ELO / 2.91% CER. Iterative DPO attains highest human preference while keeping competitive CER; GRPO ranks lowest in preference despite best CER.
- **Notes:** Core lesson: optimizing verifiable rewards (CER) trades off prosody; data-efficient human-preference DPO (~200 pairs/round) recovers naturalness. Korean call-center domain; single-speaker fine-tune. Training code not released as of review.

### 53. Optimizing Multilingual Text-To-Speech with Accents and Emotions

_Pranav Pawar, Akshansh Dwivedi, Jenish Boricha, Himanshu Gohil, Aditya Dubey · 2025 · arXiv preprint_

- **Categories:** C2, C6
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2506.16310) · arXiv:2506.16310
- **Synthetic-data role:** Not a primary synthetic-data paper. The role is indirect: the system targets code-mixed/code-switched multilingual speech (Hindi + Indian English) using culture-sensitive emotion embedding layers and dynamic accent code-switching. No explicit claim of training on machine-generated/pseudo-labeled audio appears in the abstract; relevance is via accent/style transfer and multilingual TTS rather than a documented synthetic-corpus pipeline. Synthetic-data specifics not reported.
- **Method:** Extends Parler-TTS to jointly model multilingual accents and emotions for Indic languages. Combines language-specific phoneme alignment in a hybrid encoder-decoder, culture-sensitive emotion embedding layers, and dynamic accent code-switching via residual vector quantization to support real-time code-mixing (e.g., English sentence containing a Hindi phrase).
- **Architecture:** Parler-TTS-based foundation (text-conditioned TTS). Hybrid encoder-decoder with language-specific phoneme alignment; multi-scale emotion modeling via culture-sensitive emotion embedding layers; residual vector quantization (RVQ) for dynamic accent code-switching. Parameter counts not reported.
- **Base models:** Parler-TTS (extended as the foundation model).
- **Datasets:** Native-speaker corpora for Hindi and Indian English used for emotion/accent training; specific dataset names and hours not reported.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Start from a pretrained Parler-TTS model. 2) Add language-specific phoneme alignment for Hindi and Indian English in a hybrid encoder-decoder. 3) Insert culture-sensitive emotion embedding layers trained on native-speaker corpora for multi-scale emotion modeling. 4) Add an RVQ-based dynamic accent code-switching module to enable intra-sentence code-mixing. 5) Fine-tune on native-speaker Hindi/Indian-English data. Concrete hyperparameters, optimizer settings, training steps, and exact data scale are not reported in the abstract.
- **Key results:** Reported 23.7% improvement in accent accuracy with WER reduced from 15.4% to 11.8%; 85.3% emotion-recognition accuracy from native listeners; MOS 4.2/5 for cultural correctness; outperforms METTS and VECL-TTS baselines (p<0.01).
- **Notes:** Short preprint with limited methodological detail in the abstract; dataset names, scale, and code availability not disclosed. Verified as a real arXiv paper (arXiv:2506.16310). Categorization (2,6) is conservative; primarily an accent/emotion multilingual TTS fine-tuning paper rather than a dedicated synthetic-data method.

### 54. Personalized Fine-Tuning with Controllable Synthetic Speech from LLM-Generated Transcripts for Dysarthric Speech Recognition

_Wagner et al. (wagner25, Interspeech 2025) · 2025 · Interspeech 2025 (ISCA Archive)_

- **Categories:** C2, C4, C5, C6
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2505.12991) · arXiv:2505.12991
- **Synthetic-data role:** Synthetic dysarthric speech is generated to augment fine-tuning data for an ASR model. LLMs (Phi-3-medium-4k-instruct and Llama-3.1-70B-Instruct) generate corpus-consistent target transcripts by being prompted with ten randomly sampled SAP training utterances (preserving vocabulary/topics/phrasing). A fine-tuned Parler-TTS then synthesizes speech for these transcripts, conditioned on dysarthric speaker attributes (gender, pitch, speech quality) to mimic dysarthric speech. Generated utterances with Whisper WER >= 35 are filtered out. Training with this synthetic speech yields up to ~7% relative WER improvement over personalized fine-tuning alone.
- **Method:** Submission to the Speech Accessibility Project (SAP) dysarthric ASR challenge. Combines parameter-efficient fine-tuning (AdaLoRA/LoRA) of Whisper, x-vector-based speaker personalization, injection of wav2vec2 latent audio representations, and augmentation with controllable synthetic dysarthric speech generated from LLM-written, corpus-consistent transcripts via a fine-tuned Parler-TTS.
- **Architecture:** ASR backbone: Whisper-large-v3 (transformer encoder-decoder), fine-tuned with AdaLoRA/LoRA on query and value matrices of every encoder/decoder block. Speaker personalization: x-vectors (from a VoxCeleb 1+2 model) projected via a two-layer mapping network into decoder space. Audio representations: wav2vec2-base (94M params), layer 12, injected through projection networks before the decoder. TTS: parler-tts-mini-v1 (880M params). LLMs: Phi-3-medium-4k-instruct, Llama-3.1-70B-Instruct.
- **Base models:** Whisper-large-v3 (ASR), parler-tts-mini-v1 (TTS), wav2vec2-base (representations), x-vector extractor trained on VoxCeleb 1 & 2, Phi-3-medium-4k-instruct and Llama-3.1-70B-Instruct (transcript generation).
- **Datasets:** Real: Speech Accessibility Project (SAP) dataset, 430 participants (374 train / 56 dev), ~333 hours total (~290 train / ~43 dev), ~350 utterances/participant; etiology mix 74% Parkinson's, 14% ALS, 6% cerebral palsy, 4% Down syndrome, 2% stroke. Synthetic: dysarthric speech from fine-tuned Parler-TTS over LLM-generated transcripts (used at up to 100% augmentation ratio).
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Fine-tune parler-tts-mini-v1 (880M) for 10 epochs, AdamW, lr 9.5e-4, conditioning on speaker attributes (gender, pitch, speech quality) extracted from dysarthric speech. 2) Prompt Phi-3-medium / Llama-3.1-70B with ten random SAP utterances to generate corpus-consistent novel transcripts. 3) Synthesize speech for those transcripts with the fine-tuned Parler-TTS; discard any utterance with Whisper WER >= 35 (quality filter). 4) Fine-tune Whisper-large-v3 with AdaLoRA (best: lr 1e-3, alpha 32, r_initial 12-64, r_target 8-32; adapters on Q/V matrices) on real SAP + synthetic data, 15 epochs, AdamW (weight decay 1e-4), 500-step linear warmup, greedy decoding, eval every 2000 steps, select on lowest WER over ~10% dev subset. 5) Add x-vector personalization (two-layer projection into decoder) and inject wav2vec2-base layer-12 representations via projection networks.
- **Key results:** Dev-set WER: baseline (no fine-tuning) 12.31%; best config (AdaLoRA + x-vector personalization + 100% synthetic data) 7.47% (~39% relative reduction from baseline). AdaLoRA beats full fine-tuning and standard LoRA (~23% and ~22% relative WER reduction). x-vector personalization consistently beats non-personalized FT. wav2vec2 representations add ~5% relative WER reduction; synthetic dysarthric speech adds up to ~7% relative improvement over personalized fine-tuning alone.
- **Notes:** Verified: arXiv 2505.12991, Interspeech 2025 (isca-archive wagner25), ResearchGate, and a literature review. Strong multi-component ablation. No public code found. Spans fine-tuning with synthetic data (cat 2), LLM-generated text corpora (cat 4), pseudo-label/quality-filtered augmentation (cat 5), and synthetic-speaker/dysarthric-style generation (cat 6).

### 55. RLAIF-SPA: Structured AI Feedback for Semantic-Prosodic Alignment in Speech Synthesis

_Qing Yang, Zhenghao Liu, Yangfan Du, Pengcheng Huang, Tong Xiao · 2025 · arXiv preprint (v1 titled 'RLAIF-SPA: Optimizing LLM-based Emotional Speech Synthesis via RLAIF'; v2 retitled)_

- **Categories:** C3, C5
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.14628) · arXiv:2510.14628
- **Synthetic-data role:** AI feedback as synthetic preference/reward signal: instead of human emotion annotations, an LLM/AI judge produces structured fine-grained labels along four prosodic dimensions (Structure, Emotion, Speed, Tone) and ASR provides semantic-accuracy feedback. These automatically generated reward signals drive RL optimization over the TTS model's own generated speech, eliminating costly human emotion labeling.
- **Method:** RLAIF framework that optimizes an LLM-based emotional TTS model for both emotional expressiveness and intelligibility without human supervision. Uses GRPO with a structured reward: Prosodic Label Alignment evaluates generated speech against AI-derived labels on Structure, Emotion, Speed, and Tone, combined with ASR-based semantic accuracy, to enforce fine-grained semantic-prosodic consistency.
- **Architecture:** LLM-based autoregressive TTS backbone (token/codec-based, ChatTTS-style) optimized via GRPO. Reward stack: an AI/LLM judge producing structured prosodic labels (4 dimensions) plus an ASR model providing transcription-based semantic accuracy reward. No human-trained reward model.
- **Base models:** LLM-based TTS model in the ChatTTS family used as base/baseline (ChatTTS compared against); ASR model for intelligibility reward; an LLM acting as the AI-feedback judge (specific models not fully reported).
- **Datasets:** LibriSpeech (English read speech, intelligibility), MELD (multi-party conversational/emotional dialogue), and Mandarin ESD (Emotional Speech Database). Synthetic component is the AI-generated reward labels rather than synthetic audio corpora.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Start from an LLM-based emotional TTS model (ChatTTS-family). 2) Define structured rewards: (a) Prosodic Label Alignment, where an AI/LLM judge scores generated speech on Structure, Emotion, Speed, Tone against target labels; (b) ASR-based semantic accuracy (transcribe generation, compare to target text). 3) Sample groups of generations per prompt and optimize with GRPO using the combined structured reward (group-relative advantages, KL to reference). 4) Train/evaluate on LibriSpeech, MELD, ESD. Reward weights, group size, learning rate not fully reported.
- **Key results:** On LibriSpeech: ~26.1% relative WER reduction and ~9.1% SIM-O improvement over the ChatTTS baseline, plus >10% gains in human subjective evaluations; outperforms strong baselines on LibriSpeech, MELD, and ESD.
- **Notes:** Title/framing changed between v1 (RLAIF for emotional TTS) and v2 (semantic-prosodic alignment). Key novelty is fully AI-generated structured feedback (no human emotion annotation) with GRPO. No public code link found.

### 56. RRPO: Robust Reward Policy Optimization for LLM-based Emotional TTS

_Cong Wang, Changfeng Gao, Yang Xiang, Zhihao Du, Keyu An, Han Zhao, Qian Chen, Xiangang Li, Yingming Gao, Ya Li · 2025 · arXiv preprint (Dec 2025)_

- **Categories:** C3
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2512.04552) · arXiv:2512.04552 · [code](https://lrwinr.github.io/RRPO-CosyVoice)
- **Synthetic-data role:** Minimal in the data sense. No generation of synthetic training corpora. The 'generated' element is the policy model's own sampled speech during RL rollouts, scored by the reward model. The paper's concern is that the policy can produce synthetic acoustic artifacts to hack the reward (reward hacking); the contribution is a robust RM that does not reward those artifacts. No pseudo-labeled or augmented external dataset is added.
- **Method:** A differentiable-RL framework (extending DiffRO) for emotional control in an LLM/codec TTS, where the key contribution is a robust reward model trained with a hybrid regularization scheme so the policy cannot reward-hack via acoustic artifacts and instead learns genuine emotional features. Targets three RM vulnerabilities: overconfidence, brittle decision boundaries, perturbation sensitivity.
- **Architecture:** LLM-based neural codec language model TTS (CosyVoice2) as the policy. Reward model follows DiffRO structure: a transformer encoder over acoustic features producing high-level embeddings for a speech-emotion-recognition (SER) head. Reward signal is differentiable end-to-end for policy optimization.
- **Base models:** CosyVoice2 (neural codec LM TTS with in-context learning) as the TTS policy; DiffRO as the differentiable-RL baseline framework. RM built on a transformer SER head.
- **Datasets:** Training: ~10,000 Mandarin speech samples from a single male speaker across five emotion categories (angry, happy, sad, surprised, fearful). Evaluation/RM-related: IEMOCAP (English), MER2023, ESD. No synthetic corpus generation reported.
- **Code:** Demo page: https://lrwinr.github.io/RRPO-CosyVoice (no training code repo found)
- **Recipe:** 1) Build a robust SER reward model with hybrid regularization: (a) label smoothing eps=0.1 against overconfidence; (b) energy-adaptive mixup of acoustic features (weighted by relative energy) to widen decision boundaries; (c) adversarial training via fast-gradient worst-case embedding perturbations, magnitude eps_adv=0.5. RM loss L_ser = L_emo + alpha*L_adv with alpha=0.5. 2) Run differentiable RL (DiffRO-style) optimizing the CosyVoice2 policy against this robust RM for emotion expressiveness while preserving naturalness. Training: lr 1e-5, 8x NVIDIA A800 GPUs. Mandarin single-speaker data, five emotions.
- **Key results:** Subjective MOS: E-MOS (emotion) 3.78 vs DiffRO 3.65, SFT 3.52, base 3.27; N-MOS (naturalness) 3.81 vs DiffRO 3.61, SFT 3.72, base 3.65. RM cross-lingual generalization 66.0% -> 68.0% weighted accuracy on IEMOCAP (English). Mitigates reward hacking with gains in expressiveness and naturalness over all baselines.
- **Notes:** Future-dated arXiv ID (Dec 2025) consistent with listing; confirmed via arXiv abs/HTML and ResearchGate. Single-speaker Mandarin scope limits generalization. Contribution is RM robustness, not new synthetic data.

### 57. Scalable Controllable Accented TTS

_Henry Li Xinyuan, Zexin Cai, Ashi Garg, Kevin Duh, Leibny Paola Garcia-Perera, Sanjeev Khudanpur, Nicholas Andrews, Matthew Wiesner · 2025 · IEEE ASRU 2025 (accepted); arXiv preprint_

- **Categories:** C2, C5, C6, C7
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2508.07426) · arXiv:2508.07426
- **Synthetic-data role:** Two forms of synthetic/generated supervision. (1) Accent labels are SYNTHETIC/pseudo-labels: instead of relying on scarce human self-reported accent labels, a speech geolocation model automatically infers accent labels from raw speech, dramatically expanding both data volume and the number of distinct accent classes. (2) Audio is augmented via kNN voice conversion (kNN-VC), which converts/timbre-augments existing utterances to new timbres for more speaker-timbre diversity per accent. The synthetic component is pseudo-labeled accent metadata plus VC-augmented speech, used to fine-tune the TTS backbone.
- **Method:** Scale controllable accented TTS along two axes (more data, more accent labels) by (a) discovering accent labels automatically with a speech geolocation model and (b) increasing timbre robustness/diversity with kNN voice conversion augmentation, then fine-tuning a strong zero-shot TTS backbone (XTTS-v2) on this enlarged, pseudo-labeled, augmented corpus.
- **Architecture:** Autoregressive codec-language-model TTS backbone (XTTS-v2: GPT-style autoregressive transformer over discrete audio/codec tokens with a HiFi-GAN-style decoder/vocoder and speaker conditioning). Auxiliary components: a speech geolocation model for accent-label inference and a kNN-VC module (self-supervised WavLM features + kNN regression + neural vocoder) for timbre augmentation.
- **Base models:** XTTS-v2 (Coqui) as the TTS backbone, fine-tuned; a pretrained speech geolocation model for accent-label discovery; kNN-VC voice conversion for augmentation.
- **Datasets:** Real: Mozilla Common Voice (English, multi-accent crowd-sourced) as scaling corpus, with accent labels either self-reported or geolocation-discovered. Synthetic/derived: geolocation-inferred accent pseudo-labels and kNN-VC timbre-augmented audio. Exact hours not stated in abstract.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Take a large multi-accent corpus (Common Voice English). 2) Run a speech geolocation model over raw audio to automatically assign accent labels, yielding far more labeled data and more accent classes than self-reported labels. 3) Apply kNN voice conversion to convert utterances to additional target timbres, augmenting data and improving timbre robustness. 4) Fine-tune XTTS-v2 on the combined geolocation-labeled + VC-augmented data, conditioning on the discovered accent labels for controllability. 5) Evaluate against XTTS-v2 fine-tuned only on self-reported accent labels and prior accented-TTS baselines. Specific hyperparameters not reported in the abstract.
- **Key results:** The proposed model outperforms XTTS-v2 fine-tuned on self-reported accent labels and exceeds prior accented-TTS baselines on accentedness/controllability and quality; exact numeric MOS/SIM/accent-accuracy values not given in the abstract.
- **Notes:** Accepted at ASRU 2025. Authors from Johns Hopkins (HLTCOE/CLSP). Headline numeric metrics not in the abstract; verified via arXiv abstract fetch.

### 58. Scaling Rich Style-Prompted Text-to-Speech Datasets

_Anuj Diwan, Zhisheng Zheng, David Harwath, Eunsol Choi · 2025 · EMNLP 2025_

- **Categories:** C4, C5, C6, C7
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2503.04713) · arXiv:2503.04713 · [code](https://github.com/ajd12342/paraspeechcaps)
- **Synthetic-data role:** The dataset's style-tag annotations are synthetic/pseudo-labeled, not the audio. An automatic pipeline (off-the-shelf text and speech embedders, classifiers, and an audio language model) auto-scales rich 59-tag style annotations onto real speech, producing PSC-Scaled (2,427 hours of automatically annotated data) to complement PSC-Base (342 hours human-labeled). The real audio is sourced from existing corpora; the rich style captions/tags are machine-generated.
- **Method:** Builds ParaSpeechCaps (PSC), a large rich style-prompted TTS dataset with 59 speaker-level and utterance-level style tags, by combining human labeling (Base) with an automatic annotation pipeline (Scaled) that scales rich tags for the first time. Fine-tunes a style-prompted TTS model on this data to improve style adherence and quality.
- **Architecture:** Style-prompted TTS = Parler-TTS (autoregressive codec language model with a text/description encoder conditioning on natural-language style prompts) + neural audio codec (DAC). Annotation pipeline uses pretrained text and speech embedders, attribute classifiers, and an audio language model.
- **Base models:** TTS base: Parler-TTS (open-source style-prompted TTS). Annotation: off-the-shelf text/speech embedders, classifiers, and an audio LM (used as labelers, not trained from scratch).
- **Datasets:** ParaSpeechCaps: PSC-Base = 342 hours human-labeled; PSC-Scaled = 2,427 hours automatically annotated; total 2,769 hours. Real source audio drawn from existing speech corpora (e.g., expressive/emotional speech sources); 59 style tags spanning speaker-level and utterance-level traits.
- **Code:** https://github.com/ajd12342/paraspeechcaps
- **Recipe:** 1) Define a taxonomy of 59 rich style tags (speaker-level: pitch, age, gender, accent, etc.; utterance-level: emotion, expressivity, etc.). 2) Human-label PSC-Base (342h). 3) Build automatic annotation pipeline: run text/speech embedders + classifiers + an audio LM over real speech to assign tags, producing PSC-Scaled (2,427h). 4) Convert tags to natural-language style prompts. 5) Fine-tune Parler-TTS on combined PSC data conditioning on the style prompts. 6) Evaluate style consistency (Consistency MOS) and naturalness (Naturalness MOS) vs models trained on prior rich-tag datasets.
- **Key results:** Style consistency +7.9% (Consistency MOS) and speech quality +15.5% (Naturalness MOS) vs best baseline combining existing rich style-tag datasets.
- **Notes:** Dataset, models, and code released. The synthetic element is the annotation/caption layer (pseudo-labels), enabling category 7 (corpus partially generated synthetically via auto-annotation) and category 4 (style-prompt text).

### 59. SoulX-Podcast: Towards Realistic Long-form Podcasts with Dialectal and Paralinguistic Diversity

_Hanke Xie, Haopeng Lin, Wenxiao Cao, Dake Guo, Wenjie Tian, Jun Wu, Hanlin Wen, Ruixuan Shang, Hongmei Liu, Zhiqi Jiang, Yuepeng Jiang, Wenxi Chen, Ruiqi Yan, Jiale Qian, Yichao Yan, Shunshun Yin, Ming Tao, Xie Chen, Lei Xie, Xinsheng Wang (Soul AI Lab / NWPU / SJTU) · 2025 · arXiv preprint_

- **Categories:** C2, C4, C5, C6, C7
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.23541) · arXiv:2510.23541 · [code](https://github.com/Soul-AILab/SoulX-Podcast)
- **Synthetic-data role:** Multiple synthetic/automatic-labeling roles. (1) Dialect retrieval: a trained dialect-identification model mines dialectal utterances from in-the-wild data (pseudo-labeling), supplementing collected public dialect recordings. (2) Paralinguistic annotation: a two-stage framework with automatic event detection (BEATs for Mandarin, WhisperD for English) followed by model-driven verification + fine-grained labeling using the Gemini-2.5-Pro API (LLM-generated labels). (3) LLM-generated TEXT: GPT-generated scripts are used to build test sets for paralinguistic and dialect capabilities (20 utterances/label; 1,000 samples/dialect).
- **Method:** Long-form podcast / multi-speaker conversational TTS that also keeps SOTA monologue TTS, adding controllable Chinese dialects (Sichuanese, Henanese, Cantonese) and paralinguistic events (laughter, sigh, breathing, coughing, throat clearing) via special tokens, plus a context-regularization mechanism for >90-min stable generation.
- **Architecture:** Two-stage generative system: an LLM (Qwen3-1.7B backbone) predicts semantic tokens from a text-speech interleaved sequence with speaker/dialect/paralinguistic special tokens, then a flow-matching module converts semantic tokens to acoustic features, and a vocoder synthesizes the waveform.
- **Base models:** Qwen3-1.7B (LLM backbone initialization); BEATs and WhisperD for paralinguistic detection; Gemini-2.5-Pro API for verification/labeling; GPT for test-text generation.
- **Datasets:** ~1.3M h total (~1.0M h monologue + ~0.3M h dialogue); dialect speech ~2,000 h Sichuanese, ~1,000 h Cantonese, ~500 h Henanese; ~1,000 h paralinguistic-annotated. Eval on Seed-TTS-eval and ZipVoice-Dia.
- **Code:** https://github.com/Soul-AILab/SoulX-Podcast ; demo https://soul-ailab.github.io/soulx-podcast/
- **Recipe:** 1) Build data: dialect-ID model + public dialect corpora; paralinguistic two-stage pipeline (BEATs/WhisperD detect -> Gemini-2.5-Pro verify+label); interleave text+speech with speaker/dialect/paralinguistic special tokens. 2) Three-stage curriculum from Qwen3-1.7B init: (a) monologue+dialogue training; (b) multi-speaker dialogue incorporating dialects + paralinguistics; (c) extra fine-tuning on dialect data. 3) Context-regularization mechanism progressively drops historical speech tokens for long-form stability. 4) Two-stage synthesis: LLM semantic tokens -> flow matching -> vocoder.
- **Key results:** Monologue (Seed-TTS-eval): ZH CER 1.10, EN WER 1.91; SIM 0.743 ZH / 0.661 EN. Dialogue (ZipVoice-Dia): ZH CER 2.2 / cpSIM 0.599; EN WER 2.27 / cpSIM 0.484. Paralinguistic control overall accuracy 0.82 (laughter 1.00, sigh 0.85, breathing 0.75, coughing 0.70, throat-clearing 0.80). Dialect SIM: Sichuanese 0.704, Henanese 0.705, Cantonese 0.680. >90 min stable conversation with consistent timbre. Claims SOTA on both monologue and multi-turn dialogue.
- **Notes:** Distinctive contributions are controllable Chinese dialects + paralinguistic tokens and the Gemini/BEATs/WhisperD annotation pipeline. GPT-generated text and pseudo-labeled dialect data are the main synthetic-data elements; training audio is largely real and pseudo-labeled.

### 60. Spark-TTS: An Efficient LLM-Based Text-to-Speech Model with Single-Stream Decoupled Speech Tokens

_Xinsheng Wang, Mingqi Jiang, Ziyang Ma, et al. (SparkAudio, ~25 authors) · 2025 · arXiv preprint (2025)_

- **Categories:** C4, C6, C7
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2503.01710) · arXiv:2503.01710 · [code](https://github.com/SparkAudio/Spark-TTS)
- **Synthetic-data role:** Primarily a dataset-curation/annotation play rather than synthetic-audio generation. VoxBox (100k hours) is curated from real audio but enriched with comprehensive attribute annotations (gender, pitch, speaking rate, etc.), many produced by automatic models/classifiers (machine-generated attribute labels). The model also supports synthetic-speaker generation: it can create entirely novel virtual voices not tied to any reference speaker by sampling global tokens / attribute conditions, i.e. generating new synthetic speaker identities.
- **Method:** LLM-based controllable TTS that represents speech with BiCodec, a single-stream codec decoupling low-bitrate semantic tokens (linguistic content) from a fixed-length global token (speaker/timbre attributes). A Qwen2.5 LLM autoregressively predicts these tokens, using chain-of-thought generation to produce coarse-to-fine attribute control, enabling both zero-shot voice cloning and creation of new synthetic voices from attribute specifications.
- **Architecture:** Autoregressive LLM (Qwen2.5) operating over BiCodec single-stream decoupled speech tokens. BiCodec = neural audio codec producing (a) semantic tokens for content and (b) a fixed-length global token vector for time-invariant speaker attributes. Chain-of-thought generation predicts coarse attribute labels then fine-grained tokens. Decoder reconstructs waveform from tokens. Single-stream design avoids multi-codebook acoustic modeling.
- **Base models:** Qwen2.5 LLM as the language-model backbone; BiCodec (introduced in the paper) as the speech tokenizer/codec. Builds on the LLM-codec TTS paradigm.
- **Datasets:** VoxBox: a ~100,000-hour open dataset curated by the authors with comprehensive attribute annotations for controllable TTS. Used for training and benchmarking.
- **Code:** https://github.com/SparkAudio/Spark-TTS
- **Recipe:** 1) Train BiCodec to encode speech into a single stream of low-bitrate semantic tokens plus one fixed-length global token capturing speaker attributes; train codec with reconstruction objectives. 2) Build/curate VoxBox (~100k hrs) and attach attribute annotations (gender, pitch level, speed, etc.), largely via automatic labeling. 3) Fine-tune/train the Qwen2.5 LLM to autoregressively generate BiCodec semantic + global tokens conditioned on text and (optionally) attribute prompts, using a chain-of-thought formulation: first predict coarse attribute labels, then fine-grained acoustic tokens. 4) For zero-shot cloning, condition on a reference clip's global token; for synthetic voice creation, specify desired attributes and let the model synthesize a novel global token. 5) Decode tokens with BiCodec to waveform. Specific LLM/codec sizes and step counts on GitHub.
- **Key results:** Reports state-of-the-art zero-shot voice cloning with strong controllability; supports creating new virtual speakers and fine/coarse attribute control surpassing reference-only approaches. Exact WER/SIM/MOS figures are in the paper tables (not fully extracted from abstract).
- **Notes:** Open-source code, model, and VoxBox dataset released by SparkAudio. Efficiency claim comes from the single-stream decoupled-token design. Synthetic-data angle is mainly automatic attribute annotation + synthetic-speaker generation, not synthetic audio for training.

### 61. Speech Recognition Model Improves Text-to-Speech Synthesis using Fine-Grained Reward

_Guansu Wang, Peijie Sun · 2025 · arXiv preprint (also appears in AAAI proceedings listing)_

- **Categories:** C3
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2511.17555) · arXiv:2511.17555
- **Synthetic-data role:** The TTS policy generates N=8 candidate speech samples per text prompt (group/online rollouts). These self-generated samples are not a static dataset but on-policy RL rollouts that a frozen pretrained Whisper ASR scores word-by-word; the reward then optimizes the TTS. So synthetic data here is the model's own generated audio used for reward-based policy optimization, not fixed pseudo-labeled corpora.
- **Method:** W3AR (Word-level TTS Alignment by ASR-driven Attentive Reward) uses cross-attention from a frozen encoder-decoder ASR (Whisper) to derive fine-grained, word-level quality signals without human reward annotations. Two metrics, Attention Purity (per-word articulatory clarity) and Alignment Monotonicity (prosodic fluency), are combined into a word-level reward guiding group-relative policy optimization of the TTS with KL regularization.
- **Architecture:** Frozen Whisper ASR (encoder-decoder, cross-attention exposed) as reward model; policy is a pretrained autoregressive neural-codec TTS (CosyVoice, ~170k h). GRPO-style group-relative policy optimization over N=8 candidates with word-level advantage and KL regularization. Generalization validated on VoiceCraft and MaskGCT.
- **Base models:** Policy/base TTS: CosyVoice (primary), plus VoiceCraft and MaskGCT for generalization. Reward model: pretrained Whisper ASR (frozen, not fine-tuned).
- **Datasets:** LibriTTS (in-domain, 2,500 samples for optimization/eval); Emilia + GigaSpeech (out-of-domain, 2,500 samples). CosyVoice pretrained on ~170k h audio. No new corpus released.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Take a strong pretrained AR codec TTS (CosyVoice) as policy. 2) For each text prompt sample N=8 candidate generations. 3) Pass each through frozen Whisper; from decoder cross-attention compute Attention Purity (per-word clarity) and Alignment Monotonicity (monotonic/prosodic fluency). 4) Combine into a word-level reward; compute group-relative advantages across the 8 candidates. 5) Update the TTS policy via group-relative (GRPO-style) policy optimization with KL regularization to the reference for stability. 6) Evaluate WER, MOS-N, SECS in-domain (LibriTTS) and out-of-domain (Emilia+GigaSpeech). No reward-model training and no human labels needed.
- **Key results:** In-domain LibriTTS: WER 5.25 -> 3.21 (-38.9%), MOS-N 4.07 -> 4.38, SECS 0.69 -> 0.71. Out-of-domain: WER 8.92 -> 4.54 (~49% reduction), MOS-N 3.81 -> 4.15. Beats baselines SpeechAlign (utterance-level), UNO (uncertainty-aware MOS), and FPO (first fine-grained TTS reward), with stronger out-of-domain/zero-shot robustness.
- **Notes:** Reward is annotation-free, derived purely from ASR cross-attention. No code released. Naming varies across sources (also expanded as 'Word-level Whisper-guided Audio Refinement via Adversarial Rewards'); method substance consistent.

### 62. SpeechAccentLLM: A Unified Framework for Foreign Accent Conversion and Text to Speech

_Zhuangfei Cheng, Guangyan Zhang, Zehai Tu, Yangyang Song, Shuiyang Mao, Xiaoqi Jiao, Jingyu Li, Yiwen Guo, Jiasong Wu · 2025 · arXiv_

- **Categories:** C2, C5, C6
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2507.01348) · arXiv:2507.01348
- **Synthetic-data role:** Synthetic native-accent speech is the key enabler for parallel foreign-accent-conversion (FAC) training. A pretrained VITS TTS model (trained on LJSpeech) generates native-English counterparts paired with the nonnative L2-ARCTIC utterances, creating a synthetic parallel accented<->native corpus. Synthetic VITS speech also augments the TTS task data (LibriSpeech train-clean-360 + VITS-synthesized speech = 370h). FAC and TTS tasks are mixed 1:1 via oversampling during joint multitask training to overcome FAC data scarcity.
- **Method:** A unified LLM-style framework that handles both foreign accent conversion and TTS in one transformer-decoder model over discrete speech-content tokens. A novel codec (SpeechCodeVAE) tokenizes speech content with CTC-guided discretization; a BERT-style SpeechRestorer post-processes the LM token outputs to fix stochastic decoding errors and improve prosodic continuity.
- **Architecture:** (1) SpeechCodeVAE: frozen Whisper-medium encoder (50Hz) -> Pre-VQ encoder (conv + transformer) trained with CTC loss -> vector quantization (1024-entry codebook, EMA) -> Post-VQ encoder + variance adapter (f0 predictor, dual-stream) + VITS-based flow module + waveform decoder; speaker info from frozen ECAPA-TDNN (512-dim). (2) FAC&TTS model: 8-layer transformer decoder, 512-dim, 8 heads, trained from scratch, conditioned on Task-ID tokens. (3) SpeechRestorer: 4-layer bidirectional BERT-style encoder, 512-dim, 4 heads, masked-token recovery (10% replaced, 10% masked).
- **Base models:** Frozen Whisper-medium encoder (content features) and frozen ECAPA-TDNN (speaker embeddings); VITS model trained on LJSpeech used to synthesize native-accent parallel data; VITS-based flow/waveform decoder. The LM (FAC&TTS) and SpeechRestorer are trained from scratch (no commercial base LLM like Qwen/LLaMA).
- **Datasets:** SpeechCodeVAE multilingual base corpus ~562h / 250k segments: AISHELL-1 (Chinese, 180h, 400 spk) + LibriSpeech train-clean-360 (English, 360h, 921 spk) + JVS (Japanese, 22h, 100 spk). FAC&TTS corpus: L2-ARCTIC (9.3h, 20 nonnative speakers, 6 L1 backgrounds) + LibriSpeech train-clean-360 + VITS-synthesized native speech (~370h for TTS). VITS native-accent synthesis trained on LJSpeech.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** Stage 1: train SpeechCodeVAE on the ~562h multilingual base corpus, Whisper-medium encoder frozen, Pre-VQ encoder with CTC loss, VQ (1024 codebook, EMA), Post-VQ encoder with L2 reconstruction, variance adapter (f0), VITS flow + waveform decoder, ECAPA-TDNN speaker encoder frozen. Stage 2: generate synthetic native-English counterparts for L2-ARCTIC using a VITS model trained on LJSpeech to form a parallel FAC corpus; jointly train the 8-layer (512-dim, 8-head) transformer-decoder FAC&TTS model on the FAC&TTS corpus with Task-ID tokens, mixing FAC and TTS 1:1 by oversampling. Stage 3: train SpeechRestorer (4-layer BERT, 512-dim, 4-head) separately on target tokens produced by the stage-2 model, using 10% token replacement + 10% masking recovery objectives to correct LM decoding errors.
- **Key results:** On L2-ARCTIC FAC: Accentedness reduced to 1.86 vs baseline 2.48; WER reduced to 9.1% vs baseline 14.4%; improved CMOS and Sim-O speaker similarity. SpeechRestorer further improves synthetic-speech fluency by correcting acoustic discontinuities.
- **Notes:** First to integrate CTC directly into codebook discretization. No public code found at time of check. Verified via arXiv abstract and full HTML.

### 63. SpeechJudge: Towards Human-Level Judgment for Speech Naturalness

_RMSnow et al. (Amphion Team) · 2025 · arXiv preprint (Amphion team), 2025_

- **Categories:** C3, C4, C7
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2511.07931) · arXiv:2511.07931 · [code](https://github.com/AmphionTeam/SpeechJudge)
- **Synthetic-data role:** The core dataset (SpeechJudge-Data, 99K speech pairs) is built almost entirely from synthetic speech: pairs are generated by a diverse set of advanced zero-shot TTS models across many styles and multiple languages, then human-annotated for intelligibility and naturalness preference. The synthetic-speech pairs are the substrate for both the benchmark (SpeechJudge-Eval) and for training the reward model. RL (GRPO) further trains on challenging synthetic cases. So synthetic speech is the evaluated/preference-labeled content, not the model output being learned.
- **Method:** Construct a large human-preference corpus over zero-shot-TTS-generated speech pairs, build a hard naturalness-judgment benchmark, and train a generative reward model (GRM) that predicts which of two speech samples is more natural, matching human judgment for TTS evaluation and RLHF reward modeling.
- **Architecture:** Generative reward model built on Qwen2.5-Omni-7B (audio-language model). Post-training: SFT with chain-of-thought rationales, then GRPO RL on hard cases. Compared against a classic Bradley-Terry scalar reward model.
- **Base models:** Qwen2.5-Omni-7B (audio LLM) as GRM base; a diverse set of advanced zero-shot TTS systems used only to generate the speech pairs; Gemini-2.5-Flash and other AudioLLMs as evaluation baselines.
- **Datasets:** SpeechJudge-Data: 99K speech preference pairs from multiple zero-shot TTS models, multilingual, multi-style, with human intelligibility + naturalness labels. SpeechJudge-Eval: challenging naturalness-judgment benchmark. Released on HuggingFace (RMSnow/SpeechJudge-Data, RMSnow/SpeechJudge-GRM).
- **Code:** https://github.com/AmphionTeam/SpeechJudge (project page: https://speechjudge.github.io/ ; models/data on HuggingFace RMSnow/SpeechJudge-GRM and RMSnow/SpeechJudge-Data)
- **Recipe:** 1) Generate 99K speech pairs using diverse advanced zero-shot TTS models across styles/languages. 2) Collect human preference annotations for intelligibility and naturalness on each pair. 3) Carve out SpeechJudge-Eval as a hard benchmark subset. 4) Stage-1 SFT: fine-tune Qwen2.5-Omni-7B to produce chain-of-thought rationales plus a preference verdict on each pair. 5) Stage-2 RL: apply GRPO on challenging/ambiguous cases to sharpen judgment. 6) At inference, optionally use inference-time scaling (sample @10 and aggregate) to boost agreement. Use the resulting GRM as a reward model for TTS RLHF.
- **Key results:** Best prior AudioLLM (Gemini-2.5-Flash) reaches <70% agreement with humans. SpeechJudge-GRM achieves 77.2% accuracy (79.4% with inference-time scaling @10) vs 72.7% for a classic Bradley-Terry reward model, approaching human-level naturalness judgment.
- **Notes:** Primary contribution is an evaluation/reward model rather than a TTS generator, but it is directly aimed at TTS preference optimization and is built on a fully synthetic-speech corpus. Listed as 'under development' on GitHub.

### 64. SpeechWeave: Diverse Multilingual Synthetic Text & Audio Data Generation Pipeline for Training Text to Speech Models

_Karan Dua, Puneet Mittal, Ranjeet Gupta, Hitesh Laxmichand Patel · 2025 · ACL 2025 (Industry Track), pages 718-737, Vienna, Austria_

- **Categories:** C4, C7, C2, C6
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2509.14270) · arXiv:2509.14270
- **Synthetic-data role:** Entirely synthetic end-to-end pipeline. Both text and audio are synthetic: an LLM (Mistral-7B-Instruct) generates domain-specific text scripts, then a TTS engine (MeloTTS) synthesizes audio that is speaker-standardized via OpenVoiceV2 tone-color conversion. No human voice artists or scraped corpora. Scale: 3,000 datapoints per language (English and Spanish) across 16 business domains, 5 sentence types, 9 semiotic classes, 2 reference speakers. The generated corpus is then used to fine-tune a downstream TTS model (StyleTTS 2).
- **Method:** An automated pipeline producing diverse, domain-specific, normalized synthetic text plus speaker-standardized synthetic audio for TTS training, addressing repetitive LLM text, text-normalization anomalies, and reliance on human voice talent. Multi-step LLM prompting with keyphrase and semiotic-class injection increases linguistic/phonetic diversity; voice cloning standardizes the speaker across all utterances.
- **Architecture:** Pipeline of off-the-shelf components rather than a new model: Mistral-7B-Instruct for text generation; MeloTTS as the base TTS synthesizer; OpenVoiceV2 tone-color converter for cross-lingual voice cloning / speaker standardization. Downstream evaluation fine-tunes StyleTTS 2 (style-diffusion + adversarial VITS-style acoustic model with HiFiGAN-type decoder).
- **Base models:** Mistral-7B-Instruct (text LLM); MeloTTS (base TTS); OpenVoiceV2 (tone-color/voice converter); StyleTTS 2 (downstream TTS being fine-tuned).
- **Datasets:** Self-generated synthetic corpus: 3,000 datapoints/language for English and Spanish across 16 business domains; no external real speech corpus introduced. Compared against baseline LLM-text generation approaches for diversity.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Keyphrase Sampling: multi-step LLM prompting (Mistral-7B-Instruct) with fuzzy deduplication for diverse domain keyphrases. 2) Entity/semiotic-class sampling: generate addresses, dates, emails, numbers, etc., each with a normalized written/spoken form (9 semiotic classes). 3) Text-script generation: combine keyphrases + entities into domain-specific sentences across 5 sentence types and 16 business domains. 4) Text normalization post-processing: expand acronyms, convert numbers to cardinal/spoken forms (~97% correct normalization). 5) Audio generation: synthesize with MeloTTS, then apply OpenVoiceV2 tone-color conversion to standardize to 2 fixed reference speakers. 6) Use the 3k/language paired text-audio set to fine-tune StyleTTS 2. Diversity measured with diphone coverage (phonetic), mean pairwise LaBSE-embedding similarity (semantic), TTR and MATTR (lexical).
- **Key results:** Generated data is 10-48% more diverse than baseline LLM-generation approaches across diversity metrics (diphone coverage, LaBSE similarity, TTR, MATTR); ~97% of text correctly normalized; produces speaker-standardized audio without human voice artists. Downstream StyleTTS 2 fine-tuned on the data is evaluated for quality (MOS/WER reported in paper).
- **Notes:** Industry-track paper. No code released. Only English and Spanish demonstrated. Audio quality bounded by MeloTTS/OpenVoiceV2 teachers.

### 65. Task Vector in TTS: Toward Emotionally Expressive Dialectal Speech Synthesis

_Pengchao Feng, Yao Xiao, Ziyang Ma, Zhikang Niu, Shuai Fan, Yao Li, Sheng Wang, Xie Chen · 2025 · Likely ICASSP 2026 (IEEE Xplore 11462894)_

- **Categories:** C2, C6
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2512.18699) · arXiv:2512.18699
- **Synthetic-data role:** No synthetic, pseudo-labeled, or generated training data is used. The contribution is explicitly to AVOID needing jointly-labeled (dialect+emotion) data: instead of synthesizing or pseudo-labeling cross-style examples, the method composes separately-trained task vectors arithmetically. The generated artifacts are the inference-time emotional-dialectal speech outputs, not training data.
- **Method:** Two-stage task-vector arithmetic for emotional dialectal TTS. Stage 1 builds an Expressive Vector (E-Vector): fine-tune F5-TTS independently on dialect data and on emotion data, take the weight-difference task vectors, and linearly scale them with enhancement coefficients (alpha for dialect, beta for emotion) to amplify single-style expressivity. Stage 2 builds the Hierarchical Expressive Vector (HE-Vector): inject dialect and emotion task vectors at different network depths (dialect into text embedding + early DiT blocks, emotion into later DiT blocks) so both styles combine controllably without any jointly-labeled dialect+emotion corpus.
- **Architecture:** Base TTS: F5-TTS (flow-matching with Diffusion Transformer / DiT). Style adaptation via LoRA-based PEFT (rank r=8) inserted into modules with the largest parameter variation; task vectors are weight deltas applied/scaled at inference (alpha^2-style scaling for LoRA E-Vector). Hierarchical layer-wise application separates dialect (early/text-embedding) from emotion (late DiT blocks).
- **Base models:** F5-TTS (flow-matching DiT TTS) as the backbone; LoRA adapters for dialect and emotion fine-tuning.
- **Datasets:** In-house dialect corpus: 8 Chinese dialects (Tianjin, Guangdong, Shanghai, Sichuan, Henan, Shaanxi, Hunan, Shandong), ~10h each. Emotion speech data: 4 emotions (Happy, Sad, Angry, Surprise), ~5.33-6.83h each. Evaluation: CV3-Eval subset.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Fine-tune F5-TTS separately on each dialect dataset and each emotion dataset using LoRA (r=8), ~60k steps each (vs ~340k for an over-fine-tuned baseline). 2) Compute task vectors as the LoRA/weight deltas; form E-Vector by linear scaling with alpha=3.0 for dialects and beta in [0, beta_max] for emotions. 3) For HE-Vector, apply the dialect E-Vector to the text embedding and early DiT blocks and the emotion E-Vector to the later DiT blocks, merging hierarchically at inference. 4) Synthesize emotional dialectal speech with no jointly-labeled data.
- **Key results:** MOS (1-5): Dialect synthesis HE-Vector 3.18 vs CosyVoice2 2.62. Emotional dialectal synthesis HE-Vector 2.83 vs fully-merged E-Vector 2.76 vs CosyVoice2 1.87. Objective (dialect task): WER 15.41%, speaker similarity 0.65. Outperforms full fine-tuning, over-fine-tuned F5-TTS (FT-last), dual-stage pipeline, and naive fully-merged E-Vector.
- **Notes:** Mandarin/Chinese-dialect focus. Demonstrates model-merging / task-arithmetic as an alternative to data synthesis for compositional style control. No public code; dialect corpus is in-house. WER ~15% indicates intelligibility is still limited under heavy style stacking.

### 66. TokenChain: A Discrete Speech Chain via Semantic Token Modeling

_Mingxuan Wang, Satoshi Nakamura · 2025 · ICASSP 2026 (accepted)_

- **Categories:** C5
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.06201) · arXiv:2510.06201
- **Synthetic-data role:** A fully discrete speech chain where semantic-token ASR and a two-stage TTS pass discrete tokens/text back to each other as pseudo-supervision. On unpaired/cross-domain data the TTS branch (text-to-semantic) and ASR branch generate intermediate tokens/text that train the opposite model. End-to-end feedback across the discrete text interface is made differentiable via straight-through argmax / Gumbel-Softmax, so the 'synthetic' signal is the generated semantic tokens and decoded text exchanged in the loop.
- **Method:** Reformulates the speech chain in a fully discrete (semantic-token) space. Couples a semantic-token ASR with a two-stage TTS: an autoregressive text-to-semantic (T2S) model co-trained with ASR, and a masked-generative semantic-to-acoustic (S2A) model. Uses straight-through argmax/Gumbel-Softmax for end-to-end gradient feedback across the text interface, dynamic weight averaging to balance the chain loss against supervised ASR, and a temperature schedule for in-domain and cross-domain transfer.
- **Architecture:** Semantic-token ASR; autoregressive transformer text-to-semantic LM (co-trained with ASR); masked-generative (MaskGIT-style) semantic-to-acoustic model operating on neural-codec/semantic tokens. Discrete token interface bridged with straight-through estimator / Gumbel-Softmax. Two-stage TTS (text->semantic tokens->acoustic tokens).
- **Base models:** Builds on discrete semantic-token / neural-codec speech representations and the machine speech chain paradigm; relies on pretrained semantic tokenizer and acoustic codec for tokenization (specific tokenizer/codec names not detailed in abstract).
- **Datasets:** LibriSpeech (in-domain) and TED-LIUM (cross-domain transfer). Hours not specified in abstract.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Tokenize speech into semantic tokens (and acoustic tokens for S2A) using a pretrained tokenizer/codec. 2) Train semantic-token ASR and the autoregressive text-to-semantic (T2S) model, co-training them. 3) Train the masked-generative semantic-to-acoustic (S2A) stage. 4) Close the discrete chain: pass ASR output text and T2S semantic tokens between models, using straight-through argmax / Gumbel-Softmax to allow gradients across the discrete text interface. 5) Balance the chain (self-supervised) loss with supervised ASR loss using dynamic weight averaging; apply a temperature schedule tuned separately for in-domain vs cross-domain transfer. Targets stable T2S and minimal catastrophic forgetting on the source domain.
- **Key results:** On LibriSpeech the chain reaches baseline accuracy 2-6 epochs earlier and gives 5-13% lower equal-epoch error with stable T2S. On TED-LIUM cross-domain adaptation it yields 56% relative ASR WER reduction and 31% relative T2S WER reduction with minimal forgetting of the source domain.
- **Notes:** Recent (2025) discrete reformulation of the speech chain; ICASSP 2026 accepted. Demonstrates discrete-token mutual learning as a strong semi-supervised / domain-transfer method. Code not yet located.

### 67. Towards Emotionally Consistent Text-Based Speech Editing: EmoCorrector and the ECD-TSE Dataset

_Rui Liu, Pu Gao, Jiatian Xi, Berrak Sisman, Carlos Busso, Haizhou Li · 2025 · INTERSPEECH 2025_

- **Categories:** C4, C5, C7
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2505.20341) · arXiv:2505.20341 · [code](https://github.com/AI-S2-Lab/EmoCorrector)
- **Synthetic-data role:** Heavily synthetic on both modalities. (1) Text is LLM-generated: ChatGPT-4 writes neutral 4-10-word sentences then alters 1-2 words to induce each target emotion (minimal-pair emotional text variants). (2) Speech is TTS-synthesized by three systems - Microsoft Azure (emotion label set directly), CosyVoice2 and F5-TTS (guided by reference samples from ESD/MEAD matching the target emotion). Result: 84,000 synthetic speech samples (1,400 texts x 5 emotions x 12 speakers), ~90 hours. EmoCorrector itself then retrieves emotion embeddings from this synthetic database to post-correct TSE outputs (retrieval-augmented synthesis).
- **Method:** A retrieval-augmented post-correction scheme (EmoCorrector) that fixes emotional inconsistency after text-based speech editing: extract the edited text's emotional features, retrieve emotionally-matching speech embeddings from a cross-modal database, disentangle speaker from emotion, and resynthesize speech that matches the intended emotion while preserving speaker identity. Introduces the ECD-TSE benchmark of minimal-pair emotional text variants with synthesized speech to train/evaluate emotional consistency in TSE.
- **Architecture:** Three-stage pipeline. (1) EmoCLAP cross-modal retrieval DB: RoBERTa text encoder (768-d) + emotion2vec speech encoder (1024-d), both MLP-projected to a shared 1024-d space, trained contrastively. (2) Speaker-emotion disentanglement pretraining: emotion representation (5-class) and speaker representation separated via a Gradient Reversal Layer with adversarial losses. (3) Post-correction: retrieve Top-K=5 emotion embeddings (cosine), aggregate, add a speaker embedding from the edited speech, feed through Content Adaptor -> Mel Decoder -> Flow Post-Net, then HiFiGAN vocoder.
- **Base models:** RoBERTa (text encoder), emotion2vec (speech emotion encoder), HiFiGAN (vocoder). Data-gen relies on Azure TTS, CosyVoice2, F5-TTS, and reference data from ESD/MEAD.
- **Datasets:** ECD-TSE (new): 84,000 synthesized samples = 1,400 texts x 5 emotions (Happy, Sadness, Neutral, Angry, Fear) x 12 speakers (6M/6F), 7,000 unique texts with 1-2 word variations, ~90 hours at 16 kHz; split 98% train / 1% val / 1% test. Reference corpora: ESD, MEAD.
- **Code:** https://github.com/AI-S2-Lab/EmoCorrector
- **Recipe:** Build ECD-TSE: 1) ChatGPT-4 generates neutral sentences then perturbs 1-2 words per emotion to create minimal-pair emotional texts; 2) synthesize with Azure (direct emotion label), CosyVoice2 and F5-TTS (ESD/MEAD references) over 12 speakers x 5 emotions -> 84k clips, 16 kHz. Train EmoCorrector: a) EmoCLAP contrastive pretraining 200,000 steps (mu=10, LR 1e-7); b) speaker-emotion disentanglement 300,000 steps with GRL adversarial losses; Adam optimizer, single A800 GPU. Inference: given edited text, retrieve Top-5 emotion embeddings via EmoCLAP, aggregate, combine with extracted speaker embedding, decode via Content Adaptor -> Mel Decoder -> Flow Post-Net -> HiFiGAN to post-correct the TSE segment.
- **Key results:** Applied on top of four TSE baselines (EditSpeech, A3T, FluentSpeech, VoiceCraft). TSE-MOS (emotional match) avg +0.73 (EditSpeech 3.31->4.04, A3T 3.48->4.13, FluentSpeech 3.17->3.93, VoiceCraft 3.24->4.12). TSEAcc (Qwen2-Audio LLM emotion accuracy) avg +7.2% to 48% improvement. Emotional Cosine Similarity gains +0.18 to +0.26.
- **Notes:** Dataset on HuggingFace (Gaphy/ECD-TSE); code at AI-S2-Lab/EmoCorrector. Speech is fully synthetic, so 'ground-truth' emotion is defined by the generating TTS/reference rather than human acting. Minimal-pair design isolates emotion-from-text effects. INTERSPEECH 2025 accepted.

### 68. Towards Improved Speech Recognition through Optimized Synthetic Data Generation

_Yanis Perrin, Gilles Boulianne · 2025 · arXiv preprint (CRIM); 12 pages, 3 figures; CC BY-NC-SA 4.0_

- **Categories:** C4, C5, C7
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2508.21631) · arXiv:2508.21631 · [code](https://github.com/coqui-ai/TTS)
- **Synthetic-data role:** Synthetic audio is the PRIMARY (and in some runs the only) ASR training data. Text-only corpora are voiced by a fine-tuned XTTS-v2 voice-cloning TTS to bypass confidentiality constraints that prevent sharing real transcribed audio. They generate up to ~360h of synthetic speech (90h and 360h variants) plus hybrid sets mixing large synthetic + small real (e.g. 350h synth + 10h real, 710h synth + 60h real). A Whisper-large-v3 verifier filters/regenerates low-quality utterances.
- **Method:** Train ASR for confidential domains without real audio by optimizing a TTS-based synthetic-speech generation pipeline: fine-tune a voice-cloning TTS on target-domain audio, generate synthetic speech from text-only data, filter with a generator-verifier loop, then train an end-to-end ASR on the synthetic (or hybrid synthetic+small-real) corpus. Optimizing generation (fine-tuning + filtering) yields large downstream ASR gains.
- **Architecture:** TTS generator: XTTS-v2 (Coqui), autoregressive LM + neural audio codec style voice-cloning TTS, conditioned on reference speaker samples. Verifier: Whisper large-v3 ASR for WER-based filtering. ASR model: end-to-end Transformer encoder-decoder, 29.4M params, trained in ESPnet with hybrid CTC(0.3)/attention(0.7) loss; also Whisper-medium fine-tuning experiments.
- **Base models:** XTTS-v2 (Coqui AI) as the TTS backbone; Whisper large-v3 as verifier; Whisper-medium as an alternative ASR initialization. ESPnet Transformer trained from scratch for the from-scratch ASR baseline.
- **Datasets:** Real: Bast corpus (85.7h, target-domain Quebec French spontaneous conversational speech), Charb corpus (724h, out-of-domain Quebec French; 12h subset used for TTS fine-tuning). Synthetic: TTS-generated sets of 90h and 360h; hybrid sets (e.g. 350h synth/10h real, 710h synth/10h real, 710h synth/60h real). Evaluation on dev/test of the target domain.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) TTS fine-tuning: take a 12h subset of Charb, filter utterances to 5-15s and drop shortest/longest 10% by duration; fine-tune XTTS-v2 at LR 5e-6 for 60k steps; select checkpoint by lowest WER / highest UTMOS on dev/test. 2) Synthetic generation: pair target text with reference voice samples, sample at temperature 0.65. 3) Generator-verifier filtering: transcribe each generated utterance with Whisper large-v3, compute WER vs target text; if WER > 0.20 regenerate (up to 10 attempts). Filtering gave ~2.4% absolute dev WER reduction. 4) ASR training: ESPnet Transformer encoder-decoder (29.4M params), 70 epochs, Adam, hybrid CTC 0.3 + attention 0.7; decode with beam search width 20, CTC/attn 0.3/0.7. 5) Optionally fine-tune Whisper-medium on synthetic or hybrid data.
- **Key results:** Transformer from scratch on real data: 13.6% dev / 14.2% test WER. Pure synthetic: 90h 26.9/28.2, 360h 26.2/27.1. Hybrid: 350S/10R 24.5/25.2; 710S/10R 22.4/22.8; 710S/60R 20.4/20.5. Whisper-medium fine-tuning: 360h synth 10.5/14.8; 350S/10R 7.2/9.5; 10h real 6.5/8.0; full real 5.7/7.1. Optimized generation (fine-tune+filter) substantially closes the synthetic-vs-real gap, especially under Whisper fine-tuning.
- **Notes:** Domain is Quebec (Quebecois) French spontaneous speech. Pure-synthetic ASR still lags real-data ASR by a wide margin from scratch, but adding a small amount of real data (hybrid) and using a pretrained Whisper backbone narrows it dramatically. Motivated by data confidentiality (cannot release real transcribed audio).

### 69. Towards Lightweight and Stable Zero-shot TTS with Self-distilled Representation Disentanglement

_Qianniu Chen, Xiaoyang Hao, Bowen Li, Yue Liu, Li Lu · 2025 · arXiv preprint (Jan 2025); 5-page conference-style paper_

- **Categories:** C1, C5, C6
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2501.08566) · arXiv:2501.08566
- **Synthetic-data role:** A two-stage self-distillation pipeline manufactures parallel data pairs: a Stage-1 teacher (trained on real speech to reconstruct mel and extract content + speaker representations) synthesizes utterances that keep the same linguistic content but swap the speaker, yielding (synthetic-converted, real) pairs with identical content and differing speaker. These self-generated parallel pairs supervise the Stage-2 student to cleanly disentangle linguistic content from speaker attributes. A coefficient sigma (default 0.8) sets the synthetic-to-real ratio in each training batch.
- **Method:** A lightweight, stable zero-shot voice-cloning TTS that models linguistic content from source speech and multi-level speaker attributes (global timbre + temporal style: speed/pitch/energy) from prompt speech, trained with a two-stage self-distillation scheme that builds parallel content-preserving/speaker-changing pairs to disentangle content from speaker without huge models or datasets.
- **Architecture:** Mel-VAE with a flow-based model (Volume-Preserving Flow, VP-Flow) for latent/content construction; multi-level speaker encoder (global timbre + temporal style features); decoder using multi-level ResNet with Adaptive Instance Normalization (AdaIN); NSF-HiFiGAN vocoder. ~22.5M parameters total (vs 370M-447M for baselines).
- **Base models:** Trained from scratch (no large pretrained LM or codec). Self-distillation uses its own Stage-1 model as the teacher. NSF-HiFiGAN vocoder for waveform reconstruction.
- **Datasets:** Custom multi-speaker corpus: 4,678 speakers, ~531 hours (not LibriTTS); 80-dim mel-spectrograms.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Extract 80-dim mel-spectrograms. 2) Stage 1: train teacher on real speech to reconstruct mel while separating linguistic content (Mel-VAE + VP-Flow latent) and multi-level speaker representations (global timbre + temporal style: speed/pitch/energy); decoder = multi-level ResNet with AdaIN. 3) Use the teacher to synthesize parallel pairs that preserve text content but change speaker. 4) Stage 2: train the student on a mix of synthetic-converted and real samples, ratio controlled by sigma=0.8, to disentangle content vs speaker. 5) Vocode with NSF-HiFiGAN. 6) For zero-shot inference, take linguistic content from source and speaker attributes from a prompt clip. Model ~22.5M params; report RTF 0.13 (CPU) / 0.012 (GPU).
- **Key results:** CER 1.80; SIM 0.73 (cosine speaker similarity); MOS-content-consistency 4.43+/-0.09; MOS-speaker-similarity 3.31+/-0.11. RTF 0.13 CPU / 0.012 GPU. Baselines: VALL-E, XTTSv2, CosyVoice, GPT-SoVITS; achieves competitive quality/stability at ~22.5M params vs 370M-447M baselines.
- **Notes:** Licensed CC BY-SA 4.0. Key selling point is lightweight (22.5M) and stable zero-shot TTS via self-distilled parallel data; no public code repo found. Metrics from ar5iv full-text. Verified via arXiv, ar5iv, alphaXiv.

### 70. Training Text-to-Speech Model with Purely Synthetic Data: Feasibility, Sensitivity, and Generalization Capability

_Tingxiao Zhou, Leying Zhang, Zhengyang Chen, Yanmin Qian · 2025 · NCMMSC 2025 (National Conference on Man-Machine Speech Communication)_

- **Categories:** C1, C4, C6, C7
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2512.17356) · arXiv:2512.17356
- **Synthetic-data role:** The entire TTS training set is synthetic. Three large pretrained teacher TTS systems generate the speech corpus: XTTS-v2 (reading style), CosyVoice2 (natural style), and ChatTTS (oral/conversational style). The synthetic corpus is matched in utterance count to the real reference data. The study systematically varies what is synthesized: text richness, speaker diversity (up to 1,000 speakers), noise level, and speaking style, to probe sensitivity of the student model to synthetic-data attributes.
- **Method:** A generation-training-evaluation framework that trains a small non-autoregressive flow-matching TTS student model (Matcha-TTS) entirely on speech synthesized by large teacher TTS models, then systematically ablates how synthetic-data factors (text/speaker diversity, noise, style) affect the student's quality, robustness, and out-of-domain generalization. Finds purely synthetic training can match or beat real-data training under similar conditions.
- **Architecture:** Student: Matcha-TTS, a non-autoregressive flow-matching (OT-CFM) TTS, ~20.9M params, phoneme-based, U-Net of 1D conv residual blocks, speaker embeddings from WeSpeaker ResNet221_LM. Teachers: XTTS-v2 (750M, codec-LM style), CosyVoice2 (500M, LM + flow), ChatTTS (3B).
- **Base models:** Student initialized from scratch (Matcha-TTS recipe). Teacher/data generators are off-the-shelf pretrained: XTTS-v2 (16k h), CosyVoice2 (~150k h), ChatTTS (40k h). Speaker encoder: WeSpeaker ResNet221_LM.
- **Datasets:** Real reference: LibriSpeech train-clean-100 (~100 h, 251 speakers), expanded with train-360 speakers up to ~1,000 speakers. Synthetic: utterance-count-matched corpora generated by XTTS-v2, CosyVoice2, ChatTTS. Evaluation includes in-domain and out-of-domain test sets.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Pick teacher TTS (XTTS-v2 / CosyVoice2 / ChatTTS) and synthesize a corpus matching the real LibriSpeech-100 utterance count, controlling text richness, speaker count (up to 1000 via train-360), added noise, and speaking style. 2) Extract speaker embeddings with WeSpeaker ResNet221_LM. 3) Train Matcha-TTS (~20.9M params) from scratch: Adam, lr 1e-4, batch size 32, 100 epochs, 2x Nvidia A10 GPUs. 4) Evaluate WER, UTMOS, DNSMOS, SIM on in- and out-of-domain sets. 5) Ablate each synthetic-data factor independently.
- **Key results:** Matcha-TTS trained on XTTS-v2 synthetic data vs real LibriSpeech: WER 3.493 vs 4.428 (better), UTMOS 3.75 vs 3.74, DNSMOS 3.72 vs 3.71, SIM 0.404 vs 0.389. Greater speaker/text diversity and cleaner, standard-style synthetic data improve quality and robustness. Out-of-domain shows slight speaker-similarity degradation but WER/DNSMOS/UTMOS remain comparable to in-domain.
- **Notes:** Conference paper (NCMMSC 2025). Core contribution is an empirical sensitivity/feasibility study rather than a new model. No code released. Demonstrates synthetic data can outperform real data for small flow-matching TTS under matched conditions.

### 71. TTSOps: A Closed-Loop Corpus Optimization Framework for Training Multi-Speaker TTS Models from Dark Data

_Kentaro Seki, Shinnosuke Takamichi, Takaaki Saeki, Hiroshi Saruwatari (University of Tokyo) · 2025 · arXiv preprint (IEEE-style journal submission)_

- **Categories:** C1, C5, C7, C6
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2506.15614) · arXiv:2506.15614 · [code](https://github.com/sarulab-speech/jtubespeech;)
- **Synthetic-data role:** Synthetic speech is generated inside an evaluation-in-the-loop. For each candidate utterance from noisy YouTube 'dark data', a TTS model is trained with that utterance included, then synthesizes speech on a fixed common test set; a pseudo-MOS predictor (UTMOS) scores the synthesis to estimate that utterance's contribution to downstream quality. These pseudo-MOS scores become regression targets to select training data. Synthetic speech is thus an internal quality signal rather than added training audio; the actual TTS corpus is real (mined) web audio that is filtered/cleansed.
- **Method:** A data-centric closed-loop framework that defines training-data quality as each utterance's estimated contribution to downstream TTS performance (not acoustic quality), uses model-aware evaluation loops (train -> synthesize -> pseudo-MOS) to learn a per-utterance quality regressor, then jointly decides (1) whether to include an utterance and (2) which cleansing method to apply, to build multi-speaker TTS from uncurated web data.
- **Architecture:** Acoustic models: FastSpeech 2 and Matcha-TTS. Vocoder: pre-trained HiFi-GAN (UNIVERSAL_V1). Speaker representation: 512-dim x-vectors. Quality regressor: 11-layer bidirectional LSTM (256 units) on wav2vec 2.0 SSL features predicting MOS. Pseudo-MOS predictor: pre-trained UTMOS (5-scale naturalness).
- **Base models:** FastSpeech 2 and Matcha-TTS pre-trained on 10,000 JVS utterances (100 speakers) for 300k steps; pre-trained HiFi-GAN UNIVERSAL_V1 vocoder; pre-trained UTMOS pseudo-MOS predictor; wav2vec 2.0 for SSL features.
- **Datasets:** Real: Japanese YouTube 'dark data' (~3,500 h collected; 66 h / ~60,000 utterances / 2,719 speakers after pre-screening); JVS corpus (pre-training + 100 phoneme-balanced eval sentences); ITA corpus (324 test sentences); English comparison on GigaSpeech L subset (300 h, 34 speakers after pre-screening). Synthetic: in-loop TTS syntheses used only for scoring.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Collect YouTube text-audio pairs with manual subtitles via JTubeSpeech-style pipeline. 2) Pre-screen by CTC alignment score (threshold -0.3) and x-vector speaker compactness (range [1,7]). 3) Initial TTS training from pre-trained checkpoint: 100k steps, batch size 16. 4) Synthesize the fixed common test sentences and score with UTMOS pseudo-MOS to estimate per-utterance quality. 5) Train the 11-layer BiLSTM regressor on wav2vec 2.0 features to predict per-utterance quality. 6) Select top-scoring utterances and retrain TTS. Cleansing extension: for each utterance compare three variants (uncleansed, Demucs enhancement, Miipher restoration) and keep the one maximizing predicted quality. Compute: FastSpeech 2 ~4.25 h, Matcha-TTS ~12.5 h on H200.
- **Key results:** Utterance-level selection ('Ours-Utt') yielded 1942 high-quality speakers vs 1737 for the acoustic-quality (NISQA) baseline at n=12,172. Pseudo-MOS correlated with human MOS (r>0.8). Consistent improvements across corpus sizes; switching (per-utterance cleansing choice) helped. Baselines: unselected pre-screened data, NISQA acoustic thresholds (theta=4.0/3.5/3.0/2.5), speaker-wise selection.
- **Notes:** Synthetic speech is used as an evaluation signal, not as augmented training audio; categorize accordingly. Japanese-focused with English (GigaSpeech) cross-check. No released code; relies on public JTubeSpeech tooling, Demucs, Miipher, UTMOS, HiFi-GAN.

### 72. VoxCPM: Tokenizer-Free TTS for Context-Aware Speech Generation and True-to-Life Voice Cloning

_Yixuan Zhou, Guoyang Zeng, Xin Liu, Xiang Li, Renjie Yu, Ziyang Wang, Runchuan Ye, Weiyue Sun, Jiancheng Gui, Kehan Li, Zhiyong Wu, Zhiyuan Liu · 2025 · arXiv preprint (OpenBMB / Tsinghua); Apache-2.0_

- **Categories:** C1, C6
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2509.24650) · arXiv:2509.24650 · [code](https://github.com/OpenBMB/VoxCPM)
- **Synthetic-data role:** Not a synthetic-data-centric paper: it is primarily a large-scale TTS architecture trained on a massive 1.8M-hour real bilingual corpus. Synthetic data role is not explicitly featured in the abstract/method; included here as a base TTS model relevant to a synthetic-data survey because it is a strong open generator that can produce synthetic speech (zero-shot cloning) for downstream data generation.
- **Method:** A tokenizer-free TTS that generates continuous speech latents end-to-end via diffusion autoregressive modeling, avoiding discrete tokenization. Hierarchical semantic-acoustic modeling with a differentiable quantization (FSQ) bottleneck induces implicit semantic/acoustic specialization, combining the stability of discrete tokens with the expressivity of continuous signals.
- **Architecture:** Two-stage hierarchical LM: a Text-Semantic Language Model produces semantic-prosodic plans and a Residual Acoustic Model recovers fine acoustic detail, with a differentiable quantization (FSQ) bottleneck for semi-discrete residual representations; a local diffusion-based decoder generates high-fidelity continuous speech latents, trained end-to-end with a simple diffusion objective. Backbone is MiniCPM-4; VoxCPM-0.5B model (0.5B params). RTF 0.17 on a single RTX 4090.
- **Base models:** Built on the MiniCPM-4 language-model backbone (OpenBMB). Tokenizer-free, so no separate discrete audio codec; uses continuous-latent diffusion decoding.
- **Datasets:** Trained on a ~1.8 million-hour bilingual (Chinese-English) speech corpus. (VoxCPM2, a later 2B release, scales to >2M hours, 30 languages, 48kHz; the 2509.24650 paper concerns VoxCPM-0.5B.)
- **Code:** https://github.com/OpenBMB/VoxCPM (weights: https://huggingface.co/openbmb/VoxCPM-0.5B ; demo: https://openbmb.github.io/VoxCPM-demopage/)
- **Recipe:** Architecture/training recipe: 1) Initialize the text-semantic LM from MiniCPM-4. 2) Hierarchical design: Text-Semantic LM emits semantic-prosodic plans; Residual Acoustic Model adds acoustic residuals; a differentiable FSQ quantization bottleneck separates semantic vs acoustic information. 3) A local diffusion decoder maps representations to continuous speech latents; train the whole stack end-to-end with a diffusion (denoising) objective on ~1.8M hours of bilingual speech. 4) Inference: zero-shot voice cloning from a reference clip; context-aware prosody inference. Released under Apache-2.0.
- **Key results:** State-of-the-art zero-shot TTS among open-source systems on multiple TTS benchmarks (context-aware expressiveness, natural prosody, true-to-life cloning). RTF 0.17 on a single RTX 4090 confirms practical real-time deployment. (Specific WER/SIM/CMOS numbers reported in paper benchmarks not all extracted here.)
- **Notes:** Bilingual Chinese-English at the 0.5B scale; the GitHub repo now hosts the newer VoxCPM2 (2B, 30 languages, 48kHz) so the repo README may describe VoxCPM2 rather than the exact 0.5B paper. Apache-2.0 license makes it suitable as a synthetic-speech generator for building synthetic TTS/ASR corpora.

### 73. ZipVoice-Dialog: Non-Autoregressive Spoken Dialogue Generation with Flow Matching

_Han Zhu, Wei Kang, Liyong Guo, Zengwei Yao, Fangjun Kuang, Weiji Zhuang, Zhaoqing Li, Zhifeng Han, Dong Zhang, Xin Zhang, Xingchen Song, Lingxuan Ye, Long Lin, Daniel Povey (k2-fsa / Xiaomi) · 2025 · arXiv preprint_

- **Categories:** C1, C4, C5, C6, C7
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2507.09318) · arXiv:2507.09318 · [code](https://github.com/k2-fsa/ZipVoice)
- **Synthetic-data role:** Pseudo-labeling / automatic data construction is central. The OpenDialog dataset (6.8k h: 1,759 h Chinese + 5,074 h English) is built from in-the-wild speech via VAD -> speaker diarization -> ASR with speaker labels -> LLM classifier to detect dialogue content -> WhisperD (fine-tuned Whisper) for speaker-attributed transcription -> rule-based + DNSMOS P.835 OVRL>=2.8 filtering. A custom Chinese WhisperD was trained by fine-tuning Whisper on in-house dialogue. No TTS-synthesized audio is used for training; the 'synthetic' element is fully machine-generated labels/segmentation (pseudo-labels).
- **Method:** Non-autoregressive zero-shot spoken-dialogue TTS that adapts flow matching to multi-speaker dialogue via (1) a curriculum-learning strategy for robust speech-text alignment and (2) learnable speaker-turn embeddings for precise turn-taking; also introduces stereo (per-channel) dialogue generation. Very small and fast.
- **Architecture:** Built on ZipVoice: conditional flow-matching TTS with a Zipformer backbone for the text encoder and vector-field estimator; speech infilling objective; text features average-upsampled to speech length (uniform duration assumption); Vocos vocoder to waveform. Adds two learnable speaker-turn embeddings ([S1],[S2]). Only 123M parameters (vs MoonCast 2.67B, Dia 1.61B).
- **Base models:** ZipVoice monologue model pretrained on 100k h monologue speech (initialization); Whisper -> fine-tuned WhisperD for transcription; Vocos vocoder.
- **Datasets:** OpenDialog (released): 6.8k h = 1,759 h Chinese + 5,074 h English, from in-the-wild speech. Initialization from 100k h monologue (ZipVoice).
- **Code:** https://github.com/k2-fsa/ZipVoice (code, checkpoints, and OpenDialog dataset public)
- **Recipe:** 1) Build OpenDialog with the VAD/diarization/ASR/LLM-classifier/WhisperD/DNSMOS pipeline (train Chinese WhisperD via Whisper fine-tuning; 30s segment limit handled by VAD). 2) Filter by rules + DNSMOS OVRL>=2.8. 3) Two-stage curriculum: init from ZipVoice (100k h monologue) -> fine-tune on dialogue data for 60k updates, batch size 4k seconds. 4) Add speaker-turn embeddings post text-encoder (Eq.2). 5) Conditional flow matching with speech infilling; optional stereo branch.
- **Key results:** RTF 0.063 (vs MoonCast 0.953, Dia 1.663); English WER 3.25 (MoonCast 23.62, Dia 11.80); English cpSIM 0.437 (MoonCast 0.356, Dia 0.333); UTMOS 3.07 (MoonCast 2.37, Dia 1.87); cpWER (EN short) 2.79 (MoonCast 16.53, Dia 12.59); Chinese CMOS 0.00 vs MoonCast -1.17. Best intelligibility, turn-taking, speaker similarity, and speed at 123M params.
- **Notes:** Headline contributions are the open 6.8k-h OpenDialog corpus and a tiny 123M NAR flow-matching dialogue model. All training audio is real; synthetic component is the automatic pseudo-labeling pipeline.

### 74. Accent conversion using discrete units with parallel data synthesized from controllable accented TTS

_Tuan-Nam Nguyen, Ngoc-Quan Pham, Alexander Waibel (Karlsruhe Institute of Technology) · 2024 · arXiv; accepted at Syndata4GenAI (Interspeech 2024 workshop on synthetic data)_

- **Categories:** C4, C5, C6, C7
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2410.03734) · arXiv:2410.03734
- **Synthetic-data role:** Synthetic PARALLEL data (accented <-> native pairs sharing content and speaker) is generated by a controllable accented TTS (SYNTACC) to avoid scarce real non-native recordings. SYNTACC produces accented speech with controllable accent labels, giving aligned accented/native pairs that train the accent-conversion model. The native target side is represented as HuBERT discrete units; the synthetic accented audio serves as model input, so the bulk of training pairs are TTS-generated rather than real L2 recordings.
- **Method:** Many-to-one accent conversion: convert various non-native accents to native speech while preserving content and speaker identity, without needing a reference utterance at inference and without one-model-per-accent. Speech is mapped to native HuBERT discrete units (speech-to-unit, S2U), then a multi-speaker TTS converts units + speaker embedding back to native-accent speech (unit-to-speech, U2S). A controllable accented TTS (SYNTACC) synthesizes the parallel accented/native training data as augmentation.
- **Architecture:** S2U: HuBERT encoder (6th layer, 20 ms frames) + k-means (K=100) producing discrete units with consecutive-duplicate removal. U2S: YourTTS-style multi-speaker VITS treating discrete units as 'text' input plus speaker embedding (flow-based VITS with HiFiGAN decoder). Data generator: SYNTACC, built on YourTTS with weight factorization (each weight = shared component + accent-specific rank-1 component) for controllable accents.
- **Base models:** YourTTS (base for both the U2S synthesizer and the SYNTACC accented generator); HuBERT (pretrained SSL) for discrete units; SYNTACC fine-tuned from YourTTS on L2-Arctic.
- **Datasets:** S2U training: LJSpeech (13,100 utts, single native female). U2S training: LibriTTS-R (2,300+ speakers). SYNTACC fine-tuning / accented synthesis: L2-Arctic (6 accents - Hindi, Mandarin, Vietnamese, Korean, Spanish, Arabic; 24 speakers; ~3 h per accent). Test: in-house ~1,000 sentences / ~3 h from unseen Chinese, Indian, Arabic, Vietnamese speakers.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Train S2U: run HuBERT (layer 6) on native speech, fit k-means with K=100 to get discrete units, collapse consecutive duplicates. 2) Train U2S: train a YourTTS multi-speaker model on LibriTTS-R that takes native discrete units (as token input) + speaker embedding and outputs native waveform, so it reconstructs native speech preserving speaker identity. 3) Build SYNTACC: fine-tune YourTTS on L2-Arctic with weight factorization (shared weights + rank-1 accent-specific components) to controllably synthesize speech in 6 accents. 4) Generate synthetic parallel data: use SYNTACC to produce accented utterances paired with native references (same content/speaker) as augmentation, reducing reliance on real non-native data. 5) Train the accent-conversion model to map accented input -> native discrete units -> native waveform via the U2S model. 6) Evaluate accentedness, fluency, and speaker-similarity MOS plus unit perplexity on unseen non-native speakers.
- **Key results:** Subjective (5-point): Accentedness 4.46 +/- 0.12, Speaker-Similarity MOS 3.89 +/- 0.09, Fluency 4.55 +/- 0.07; best-config unit test perplexity 2.16. Improves non-native fluency, yields native-like accent, and preserves speaker identity; generalizes to unseen accents/speakers.
- **Notes:** Workshop paper (Syndata4GenAI) centered on synthetic-data augmentation for accent conversion. Spans dataset-from-synthetic-speech (4), data augmentation/self-training (5), accent transfer + multilingual (6), and synthetic corpus (7). No official code repo found; demo/PDF only. The TTS-synthesized parallel data is the load-bearing contribution.

### 75. DLPO: Diffusion Model Loss-Guided Reinforcement Learning for Fine-Tuning Text-to-Speech Diffusion Models

_Jingyi Chen, Ju-Seung Byun, Micha Elsner, Andrew Perrault · 2024 · arXiv preprint (v1 May 2024, v2 Nov 2024)_

- **Categories:** C2, C3
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2405.14632) · arXiv:2405.14632
- **Synthetic-data role:** No external synthetic corpus; the RLHF loop is self-generated. The diffusion TTS model produces its own audio samples during fine-tuning, which are scored by a learned MOS predictor (UTMOS) acting as the reward. Reward signal is thus model-generated audio judged by a proxy model rather than human labels.
- **Method:** Apply RLHF to a diffusion TTS model using UTMOS predictions as reward, adding the original diffusion loss as a penalty term (DLPO) to keep the policy aligned with the diffusion objective and prevent quality collapse seen in DDPO/DPOK.
