- **Recipe:** 1) Train VQ-VAE codec on mel-spectrograms (8192-code codebook, keep 1024 most frequent codes, 21.53 Hz). 2) Train the GPT-2 conditioning encoder to autoregressively model VQ codes from BPE text tokens, conditioned by a Perceiver Resampler over reference-audio mel features (32 x 1024-dim conditioning embeddings). 3) Train the HiFi-GAN decoder on GPT-2 latent vectors, conditioning on H/ASP speaker embeddings at upsampling layers, with a Speaker Consistency Loss to boost cloning similarity. 4) Train jointly/multilingually over the 27k-hour, 16-language mix with language-balanced sampling. 5) At inference, give text + a short reference clip for zero-shot cross-lingual cloning. Exact batch sizes/steps/optimizer not reported in the abstract; see repo configs.
- **Key results:** English (Table 2): XTTS CER 0.5425 (best), UTMOS 4.007, SECS 0.6423; beats Tortoise (CER 1.0934), StyleTTS2 (0.6789), HierSpeech++ (0.7741) on CER while HierSpeech++/StyleTTS2 score higher UTMOS. User preference: XTTS vs HierSpeech++ CMOS +0.41 (SMOS -0.31); vs Mega-TTS 2 CMOS +0.92 (SMOS -0.39). Multilingual (Table 4): superior CER and SECS over YourTTS across nearly all 16 languages. SOTA in most languages.
- **Notes:** Open weights under Coqui Public Model License (CPML, non-commercial) - relevant when reusing XTTS to generate synthetic corpora. Same architecture lineage (Tortoise) used by the Accent Vector paper, which fine-tunes XTTS-v2.

### 100. AdapterMix: Exploring the Efficacy of Mixture of Adapters for Low-Resource TTS Adaptation

_Ambuj Mehrish, Abhinav Ramesh Kashyap, Li Yingting, Navonil Majumder, Soujanya Poria · 2023 · Interspeech 2023_

- **Categories:** C2, C6
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2305.18028) · arXiv:2305.18028
- **Synthetic-data role:** No synthetic/generated speech data is used. AdapterMix is a parameter-efficient fine-tuning method trained on REAL low-resource speaker data (as little as one minute per speaker). There is no synthetic-data generation step.
- **Method:** Add a Mixture-of-Adapters (multiple parallel adapter modules with a routing/mixing mechanism) inside each backbone TTS layer so that different adapters capture distinct speaker characteristics, enabling effective low-resource speaker adaptation by training only the adapters.
- **Architecture:** FastSpeech2-style non-autoregressive transformer acoustic model as the frozen backbone, with multiple lightweight bottleneck adapter modules inserted per layer and a mixing mechanism over them. HiFi-GAN vocoder for waveform synthesis. Adapters are ~11% of total parameters; backbone is frozen during adaptation.
- **Base models:** Pre-trained FastSpeech2 multi-speaker backbone + pre-trained HiFi-GAN vocoder; adaptation is done by inserting and training adapters on top of the frozen backbone.
- **Datasets:** Multi-speaker TTS data for the backbone and low-resource target speakers with ~1 minute of speech each for adaptation (specific corpus names/hours not stated in fetched content; FastSpeech2/HiFi-GAN pipeline implies LibriTTS/VCTK-type data, not confirmed).
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Pre-train (or use a pre-trained) FastSpeech2 multi-speaker backbone with HiFi-GAN vocoder. 2) Insert a mixture of N bottleneck adapters into each transformer layer plus a routing/mixing weight over the adapters. 3) Freeze the backbone; train only the adapter parameters (~11% of total) on ~1 minute of target-speaker speech. 4) At inference, the mixed adapters specialize the frozen backbone to the new speaker. Evaluation via speaker-preference and speaker-similarity tests. Specific bottleneck dimension, number of adapters N, learning rate, and step counts not captured from fetched abstract.
- **Key results:** ~5% improvement in speaker preference tests over a single-adapter baseline when adapting with only one minute of data per new speaker, while training only adapter parameters (11% of total model parameters).
- **Notes:** PEFT/adapter method aimed at underrepresented languages, accents and dialects (hence also cat 6 multilingual/accent adaptation). No synthetic audio generated. Backbone/dataset details and full metrics not fully captured from fetched pages.

### 101. Cross-lingual Knowledge Distillation via Flow-based Voice Conversion for Robust Polyglot Text-To-Speech

_Dariusz Piotrowski, Renard Korzeniowski, Alessio Falai, Sebastian Cygert, Kamil Pokora, Georgi Tinchev, Ziyao Zhang, Kayoko Yanagisawa · 2023 · ICONIP 2023 (Springer LNCS); Amazon (Alexa AI); arXiv preprint_

- **Categories:** C1, C4, C6, C7
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2309.08255) · arXiv:2309.08255
- **Synthetic-data role:** The core training signal for the polyglot acoustic model is SYNTHETIC, created by a flow-based voice-conversion teacher. Stage 1-2: a text-conditioned, non-parallel, many-to-many flow VC model converts target-locale utterances (spoken by many source speakers in the desired languages) into the voice of a single target speaker who may not speak those languages, producing synthetic polyglot data. Stage 3: this VC-converted (synthetic) speech, combined with linguistic features and durations from native recordings, trains a single-speaker acoustic model, distilling cross-lingual knowledge from the VC teacher into the TTS student.
- **Method:** A knowledge-distillation pipeline: an upstream flow-based voice conversion model converts foreign-language utterances into a target speaker's voice to synthesize polyglot training data, then a downstream single-speaker TTS acoustic model and a locale-independent vocoder are trained on this converted data, giving robust polyglot TTS without a large multilingual model.
- **Architecture:** Upstream: flow-based VC, a text-conditioned non-parallel many-to-many model with a fixed prior, based on Flow-TTS, modified to accept F0 and voiced/unvoiced conditioning plus phoneme and accent embeddings. Downstream acoustic model: FastSpeech 2 (primary), also compared with Encoder-Decoder (gated convolutions + recurrent units), LightSpeech (LS) and a smaller LS-S. Vocoder: MultiBand MelGAN (~0.95 GFLOPS), trained locale-independent on the target speaker's original data.
- **Base models:** Flow-TTS-based VC model as the teacher; FastSpeech 2 / LightSpeech acoustic models; MultiBand MelGAN vocoder. No large pretrained LM; trained on Amazon internal data.
- **Datasets:** Internal Amazon studio data. D1: 124 speakers across 7 locales (fr-CA, en-GB, es-MX, en-US, es-US, fr-FR, de-DE), ~720 hours studio-quality 24 kHz. D2: a ~150-hour subset from fr-CA, es-MX, en-GB speakers. Synthetic data = VC-converted target-speaker utterances generated from these corpora.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Train a flow-based text-conditioned many-to-many VC model (Flow-TTS prior, conditioned on F0, V/UV, phoneme and accent embeddings) on multi-speaker multi-locale data. 2) Use it to convert target-locale utterances into the target speaker's voice, producing synthetic polyglot speech. 3) Combine converted audio with linguistic features and ground-truth durations from native recordings to train a single-speaker acoustic model (FastSpeech 2 or LightSpeech). 4) Train a locale-independent MultiBand MelGAN vocoder on the target speaker's original recordings. 5) Evaluate with MUSHRA on naturalness, speaker similarity, accent similarity vs a standard large multilingual TTS baseline; show robustness across architectures, languages, speakers and data volumes, especially low-resource.
- **Key results:** Outperforms a SOTA large multilingual TTS baseline. Representative MUSHRA (es-MX, FastSpeech 2): naturalness 69.60 vs 64.08; speaker similarity 66.38 vs 64.82; accent similarity 71.06 vs 66.26. Gap-closing to native target ranged roughly 4-38% across metrics. Especially beneficial in low-resource settings.
- **Notes:** Amazon (Alexa AI) work, also at ICONIP 2023 (Springer). Datasets proprietary; reproduction needs comparable multi-locale single-target-speaker data. Verified via arXiv abstract and ar5iv HTML.

### 102. DQR-TTS: Semi-supervised Text-to-speech Synthesis with Dynamic Quantized Representation

_Not fully reported in fetched pages · 2023 · IEEE BDCloud 2023 (13th IEEE Intl. Conf. on Big Data and Cloud Computing)_

- **Categories:** C1, C5
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2311.07965) · arXiv:2311.07965 · [code](https://github.com/Kyubyong/g2p)
- **Synthetic-data role:** Uses UNPAIRED (text-less / unlabeled) speech together with PSEUDO-LABELS: a pre-trained ASR generates phoneme pseudo-labels for unpaired data. The dynamic codebook is then expanded by adding quantized phoneme-representation vectors for novel phonemes (those whose match probability to existing codewords falls below a low threshold), so unpaired data grows codebook coverage. Temperature sharpening manages pseudo-label uncertainty. Setup: <120 min paired + ~600 min unpaired (and a 60+60 min low-resource variant).
- **Method:** Semi-supervised TTS that learns from limited paired plus abundant unpaired data via a Dynamic Quantized Representation / Dynamic Phonemic Representation Module (DPRM). With paired data a trainable codebook learns phoneme-level quantized representations under supervision; with unpaired data the codebook is dynamically expanded by inserting new quantized vectors sufficiently distant from existing ones (gated by ASR pseudo-labels), enabling good TTS with very little paired data.
- **Architecture:** Sequential autoencoder: encoder = convolution blocks + LSTMs producing frame-level hidden reps; Dynamic Phonemic Representation Module (DPRM) quantizes frame-level vectors into phoneme-level clusters using a trainable, dynamically-growing codebook; decoder based on Tacotron 2 reconstructs the mel-spectrogram; WaveNet-based pretrained vocoder converts spectrogram to waveform.
- **Base models:** Tacotron 2-style decoder; pretrained WaveNet vocoder; a pre-trained ASR model used to produce pseudo-labels for unpaired data; external G2P converter (Kyubyong/g2p).
- **Datasets:** LJSpeech (~24 h single speaker). Standard config: 120 min paired + 600 min unpaired. Low-resource config: 60 min paired + 60 min unpaired. Tacotron 2 fully-supervised baseline trained on 720 min paired.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Train sequential autoencoder on paired data: encoder->DPRM quantizes frame reps to a trainable codebook, codewords mapped to phonemes via ground-truth labels; Tacotron2-style decoder reconstructs spectrogram. 2) Run pre-trained ASR on unpaired speech to get phoneme pseudo-labels. 3) Algorithm 1 codebook expansion: for unpaired frames whose probability against existing codewords < delta_l (low threshold), add new quantized vectors (sufficiently distant) to expand the codebook; delta_h high threshold and temperature tau (sharpening) handle pseudo-label uncertainty. 4) Joint training on paired + pseudo-labeled unpaired data. Vocode with pretrained WaveNet. Hyperparameters: Adam (beta1=0.9, beta2=0.999, eps=1e-6, LR=1e-3); batch size 64; loss weights alpha1=0.5, alpha2=1.0; spectrogram 50 ms window, 12.5 ms hop.
- **Key results:** With 120+600 min data: MOS 3.12 +/- 0.32, MCD 4.54 (vs Tacotron 2 trained on 720 min paired: MOS 3.54, MCD 4.17; ground truth MOS 4.81). Low-resource 60+60 min: DQR-TTS MOS 2.83 +/- 0.27 vs baselines 2.35-2.64, i.e. best in the low-resource regime. Claim: with <120 min paired data it outperforms existing methods in subjective and objective metrics.
- **Notes:** Multiple arXiv versions (v4 read). Strength is the low-resource / semi-supervised regime; the full-data Tacotron2 still scores higher MOS. No official code located.

### 103. Generative Emotional AI for Speech Emotion Recognition: The Case for Synthetic Emotional Speech Augmentation

_Abdul Malik Shahid, Siddique Latif, Junaid Qadir · 2023 · Applied Acoustics (Elsevier), 2023_

- **Categories:** C5, C6, C7
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2301.03751) · arXiv:2301.03751
- **Synthetic-data role:** Synthetic emotional speech is generated by a controllable end-to-end TTS system (extended Tacotron with separate speaker and emotion embeddings) and used purely as data augmentation to enlarge limited speech-emotion-recognition (SER) training corpora. The TTS produces utterances conditioned on a target emotion/speaker, and these generated samples are added to the real SER training set to improve recognition. Exact synthetic volume per emotion is not stated in the abstract.
- **Method:** Train a controllable emotional TTS that disentangles speaker identity and emotion via separate embeddings, generate synthetic emotional speech across emotion classes, and augment SER classifier training with this generated data to combat data scarcity.
- **Architecture:** Generator: extended Tacotron-style sequence-to-sequence acoustic model with speaker-embedding and emotion-embedding conditioning producing mel-spectrograms; WaveRNN neural vocoder for waveform synthesis. SER classifier architecture not detailed in the abstract.
- **Base models:** Tacotron-style TTS backbone + WaveRNN vocoder; specific pretrained checkpoints not reported.
- **Datasets:** Emotional speech datasets for SER (e.g. IEMOCAP-class corpora) plus the synthetic emotional speech generated by the TTS; specific dataset names, hours, and synthetic-sample counts are not given in the abstract.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Build extended Tacotron TTS with independent speaker and emotion embedding tables so emotion can be controlled at synthesis time. 2) Train TTS to predict mel-spectrograms, vocode with WaveRNN. 3) Synthesize emotional utterances across target emotion categories (and speakers) to balance/augment scarce SER data. 4) Combine synthetic + real speech to train the SER classifier. 5) Evaluate generated-speech quality via MOS and SER gains. Concrete hyperparameters, synthetic-sample counts, and classifier details are not reported in the available abstract/listing.
- **Key results:** Reports that generated samples achieve higher MOS than the baseline and that augmenting SER with synthetic emotional speech improves recognition performance; precise UAR/accuracy numbers and baselines are not stated in the abstract.
- **Notes:** Borderline for a TTS-centric synthetic-data survey: the end task is SER, not TTS, but synthetic speech generation is central. Published in Applied Acoustics; abstract-level detail only (no public code or full numeric tables surfaced).

### 104. Leveraging Speech PTM, Text LLM, and Emotional TTS for Speech Emotion Recognition

_Ziyang Ma, Wen Wu, Zhisheng Zheng, Yiwei Guo, Qian Chen, Shiliang Zhang, Xie Chen (and collaborators) · 2023 · ICASSP 2024_

- **Categories:** C4, C5
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2309.10294) · arXiv:2309.10294
- **Synthetic-data role:** Synthetic emotional speech is the core augmentation source. GPT-4 generates emotionally congruent text (via carefully designed prompts), and Azure (emotional) TTS synthesizes speech from that text, producing a fully synthetic emotion-labeled corpus used to augment the real SER training set. Text is LLM-generated and audio is TTS-generated; exact hours are not stated in the abstract but described as high-quality synthetic emotional speech.
- **Method:** Boost speech emotion recognition (SER) by (1) selecting a strong speech pre-trained model (data2vec) as the feature extractor, and (2) augmenting limited real SER data with GPT-4-generated emotional text rendered to speech by Azure TTS, then studying multiple ways to inject this synthetic data into training.
- **Architecture:** data2vec speech self-supervised pre-trained model (PTM) as the encoder/feature extractor for the SER classifier; GPT-4 as the text generator; Azure emotional TTS as the speech synthesizer. No new acoustic/vocoder model is trained; the contribution is the SER pipeline plus augmentation strategy.
- **Base models:** data2vec (speech PTM, also compared against other SSL models such as wav2vec2/HuBERT/WavLM); GPT-4 (text generation); Azure TTS (speech synthesis).
- **Datasets:** Real: IEMOCAP (4-class SER benchmark, ~12 h; hours not stated explicitly in abstract). Synthetic: GPT-4 + Azure-TTS-generated emotional text-speech pairs (scale not reported).
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Compare speech SSL PTMs on IEMOCAP and pick data2vec as the feature extractor. 2) Prompt GPT-4 to generate emotionally congruent sentences per target emotion, with careful prompt and dataset design. 3) Synthesize the generated text with Azure emotional TTS to obtain synthetic emotional speech. 4) Combine synthetic with real IEMOCAP data using four augmentation strategies and compare: (a) random mixing, (b) adversarial training, (c) transfer learning, (d) curriculum learning. 5) Train/evaluate the data2vec-based SER classifier on IEMOCAP with ablations over augmentation method and synthetic-data source.
- **Key results:** Ablation studies on IEMOCAP show the method outperforms other data augmentation methods and other synthetic-data sources; exact WA/UA numbers are not in the abstract/HTML preview.
- **Notes:** Published at ICASSP 2024. Synthetic data generated by closed commercial systems (GPT-4, Azure TTS), limiting exact reproducibility; no public code released.

### 105. Modelling Low-Resource Accents Without Accent-Specific TTS Frontend

_Georgi Tinchev, Marta Czarnowska, Kamil Deja, Kayoko Yanagisawa, Marius Cotescu · 2023 · ICASSP 2023_

- **Categories:** C5, C6, C7
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2301.04606) · arXiv:2301.04606
- **Synthetic-data role:** Synthetic data is core and produced by voice conversion. The scarce target-accent recordings are converted (via VC) to SOUND LIKE a high-resource donor voice, creating synthetic donor-voice/target-accent speech. This VC-generated synthetic data is combined with the original real recordings to train a multi-speaker multi-accent TTS, augmenting the low-resource accent and letting a donor/related-variant TTS frontend (phoneme set) be reused so no accent-specific frontend is needed.
- **Method:** Synthesize an underrepresented accent without building an accent-specific phonetic frontend by: voice-converting the limited target-accent data into a donor voice, then training a multi-speaker multi-accent TTS on the mix of real and voice-converted data while borrowing the frontend (lexicon/phoneme set) of a related accent/language variant.
- **Architecture:** A voice-conversion model to map target-accent recordings to the donor speaker, plus a multi-speaker multi-accent neural TTS acoustic model (generative; compared against other generative TTS models) trained on original + converted data, with a neural vocoder. Uses an existing (related-variant) TTS frontend rather than an accent-specific one.
- **Base models:** Reuses an existing TTS frontend from a related accent/language variant; builds on a voice-conversion system and a multi-speaker multi-accent TTS backbone (specific pretrained checkpoints not stated).
- **Datasets:** Limited target low-resource accent recordings (donor and target speaker data), plus synthetic VC-converted versions; trained as a multi-speaker multi-accent set. Specific corpus names/hours not detailed in fetched content. Audio samples page referenced (bit.ly short link in paper).
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Take limited recordings of the target low-resource accent. 2) Use a voice-conversion model to convert these recordings so they match a high-resource donor voice, yielding synthetic donor-voice/target-accent data. 3) Borrow the TTS frontend (grapheme-to-phoneme / lexicon) of a related accent or language variant instead of building an accent-specific one. 4) Train a multi-speaker, multi-accent TTS acoustic model on the combination of original real recordings and the voice-converted synthetic data, conditioning on speaker and accent. 5) Synthesize the target accent at inference. The VC-based augmentation increases effective data for the rare accent and decouples accent modelling from frontend resources. Exact VC architecture, vocoder, and hyperparameters not captured from fetched abstract.
- **Key results:** Reports state-of-the-art results versus other generative TTS models for the low-resource accent on qualitative and quantitative evaluations; demonstrates accent synthesis without any accent-specific frontend. Specific MOS/CMOS/WER numbers not captured from fetched pages.
- **Notes:** Amazon-affiliated authors. Strong fit for synthetic-data survey: VC-generated speech used as augmentation (cat 5), for accent/multilingual modelling (cat 6), and effectively builds a partly synthetic accent corpus (cat 7). Full metric tables and dataset names not captured from fetched content.

### 106. Multilingual Multiaccented Multispeaker TTS with RADTTS

_Rohan Badlani, Rafael Valle, Kevin J. Shih, Joao Felipe Santos, Siddharth Gururani, Bryan Catanzaro (NVIDIA) · 2023 · ICASSP 2023 (also arXiv preprint)_

- **Categories:** C6
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2301.10335) · arXiv:2301.10335
- **Synthetic-data role:** The synthetic aspect is in the OUTPUT, not the training data: the system generates speech in speaker-language-accent combinations never observed in training (e.g. a speaker's voice rendered with a foreign accent, or speaking a language they never spoke). No bilingual/parallel accented data is used; the model synthesizes cross-combinations by disentangling and recombining speaker, language and accent factors. Real multi-accent corpora are used for training; novel combinations are produced synthetically at inference.
- **Method:** Extend RADTTS (a normalizing-flow, parallel/non-autoregressive TTS) to a single model giving explicit, independent control over accent, language, speaker, and fine-grained F0 and energy. By disentangling these factors the model can transfer accent across any speaker and synthesize fluent speech in target languages/accents without requiring bilingual training data, addressing the entanglement of speaker/language/accent that arises from data scarcity.
- **Architecture:** RADTTS backbone: normalizing-flow-based mel-spectrogram generator with an unsupervised alignment learning framework (RAD alignment), separate explicitly-conditioned attribute predictors for fundamental frequency (F0) and energy (autoregressive flow / deterministic predictors), and conditioning embeddings for speaker, language and accent. Mel-spectrograms are converted to waveform by a separate (HiFi-GAN-style) vocoder. Parameter sizes not reported in the abstract.
- **Base models:** Builds on RADTTS / RADTTS++ (Valle et al.) as the acoustic model; a separate neural vocoder (HiFi-GAN family) for waveform synthesis. No pretrained LM or codec.
- **Datasets:** An open-source multi-accent dataset covering 7 accents (used for training and subjective evaluation). Specific corpus names and exact hours not reported in the abstract.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Train a multispeaker/multilingual/multiaccent RADTTS where speaker, language, and accent are provided as separate learned conditioning embeddings so they can be swapped independently at inference. 2) Use RADTTS unsupervised alignment to obtain phoneme/text-to-mel durations. 3) Train explicit F0 and energy predictors conditioned on the attribute embeddings for fine-grained prosody control. 4) At inference, recombine an arbitrary speaker embedding with a target language and target accent embedding (combinations unseen in training) to synthesize accent-controlled speech while preserving the speaker's voice. 5) Vocode mels with a HiFi-GAN-style vocoder. Exact hyperparameters/steps not reported in the abstract; consult full paper for flow depth, batch size, optimizer.
- **Key results:** Subjective human evaluation: the model better retains a speaker's voice identity and accent quality than controlled baselines when generating cross speaker-accent-language combinations. Exact MOS/SIM numbers not reported in the abstract.
- **Notes:** Five-page ICASSP 2023 submission. Focus is controllable cross-combination synthesis (category 6 multilingual/accent/style transfer) rather than using synthetic audio to train other models. From NVIDIA, same lineage as RADTTS/Flowtron.

### 107. Personalized Lightweight Text-to-Speech: Voice Cloning with Adaptive Structured Pruning

_Sung-Feng Huang, Chia-ping Chen, Zhi-Sheng Chen, Yu-Pao Tsai, Hung-yi Lee · 2023 · ICASSP 2023_

- **Categories:** C2
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2303.11816) · arXiv:2303.11816
- **Synthetic-data role:** No synthetic/generated/pseudo-labeled audio is used. The method is purely a parameter-efficient adaptation/compression technique that fine-tunes (via learnable pruning masks) on a small amount of REAL target-speaker recordings. There is no synthetic-data generation pipeline in this paper.
- **Method:** Instead of fine-tuning a full large pre-trained multi-speaker TTS model for each new speaker, train per-speaker learnable structured pruning masks on the few-shot target recordings. This produces a unique, much smaller pruned model per speaker that voice-clones the target, removing the storage cost of a full fine-tuned model per user.
- **Architecture:** Pre-trained multi-speaker neural TTS backbone (acoustic model) with adaptive/trainable structured pruning applied to its weight groups (channels/heads/units). Pruning masks are jointly optimized with a sparsity/target-ratio objective. Separate vocoder for waveform generation. Exact backbone is the authors' multi-speaker TTS; reported compression up to ~7x smaller model.
- **Base models:** A pre-trained multi-speaker TTS model is used as the starting point; the method adapts/prunes from this pretrained model rather than training from scratch.
- **Datasets:** Multi-speaker TTS corpus for the base model and few-shot target-speaker voice-cloning data (specific dataset names/hours not stated in the abstract; the few-shot setting uses minimal target recordings).
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Start from a pre-trained multi-speaker TTS model. 2) For each target speaker, attach trainable structured-pruning masks to weight groups. 3) Jointly train the masks (and allowed weights) on the few-shot target-speaker recordings with a reconstruction/TTS loss plus a structured-sparsity penalty driving toward a target compression ratio. 4) Prune the masked-out structures to yield a compact speaker-specific model; keep a vocoder for waveform synthesis. Key idea: per-speaker model is obtained by pruning, not full fine-tuning. Reported result: ~7x size reduction with comparable cloning quality. Exact hyperparameters (learning rate, sparsity schedule, target ratios) not given in the fetched abstract.
- **Key results:** Compresses the personalized model up to 7x smaller while achieving voice-cloning performance comparable to standard fine-tuning baselines (subjective/objective cloning metrics).
- **Notes:** This is an adaptation/compression paper, not a synthetic-data paper; categorized only loosely under fine-tuning (cat 2) because it adapts a pretrained TTS to new speakers. No synthetic speech is generated. Full metric tables not captured from the abstract.

### 108. PromptTTS 2: Describing and Generating Voices with Text Prompt

_Yichong Leng, Zhifang Guo, Kai Shen, Xu Tan, Zeqian Ju, Yanqing Liu, Yufei Liu, Dongchao Yang, Leying Zhang, Kaitao Song, Lei He, Xiang-Yang Li, Sheng Zhao, Tao Qin, Jiang Bian (Microsoft) · 2023 · ICLR 2024 (also arXiv preprint Sep 2023)_

- **Categories:** C4, C6
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2309.02285) · arXiv:2309.02285 · [code](https://github.com/microsoft/NeuralSpeech)
- **Synthetic-data role:** Synthetic data is text-prompt labels, not synthetic speech. A prompt generation pipeline auto-generates natural-language voice descriptions for real speech: a speech understanding (SLU) model recognizes voice attributes (gender, pitch, speed, volume) from speech, then an LLM (instructed to act as a writer) composes diverse text prompts from those recognized attribute values. This removes the manual labeling cost that previously limited text-prompt TTS datasets. Applied at scale over the 44K-hour speech corpus to create paired (text-prompt, speech) training data.
- **Method:** Two contributions: (1) a Variation Network that models the one-to-many mapping by predicting fine-grained voice variability not captured by the text prompt, conditioned on the prompt representation; it uses a diffusion model to sample the residual/missing voice information, enabling diverse voice sampling controllable beyond the prompt. (2) An LLM-based prompt generation pipeline that auto-labels speech with text prompts, eliminating manual annotation. Together they generate voices consistent with text prompts while supporting diverse variability sampling.
- **Architecture:** TTS backbone with text/prompt encoders; a Variation Network on top of a base TTS system predicts a voice-variability representation via a diffusion model (DDPM-style) conditioned on text-prompt embeddings. Prompt encoder uses a pretrained language model (BERT-style) to embed text prompts. Prompt generation pipeline: a speech-language-understanding attribute recognizer + a large language model for prompt writing. Base TTS uses a transformer-based acoustic model.
- **Base models:** Built on a base TTS system; text-prompt understanding uses a pretrained language model (e.g., BERT). Prompt generation pipeline relies on a pretrained LLM for prompt writing and a speech understanding model for attribute recognition. Variation network adds a diffusion module on top of the base TTS.
- **Datasets:** Real: a large-scale 44K-hour English speech dataset (multi-speaker) used for training. Synthetic: text prompts auto-generated by the LLM-based pipeline paired with that real speech. Evaluated on held-out speech with test prompts.
- **Code:** https://github.com/microsoft/NeuralSpeech (PromptTTS2 folder); project page https://speechresearch.github.io/prompttts2/
- **Recipe:** 1) Train/use a base TTS model on 44K hours. 2) Build the prompt dataset: run an SLU attribute recognizer over speech to extract gender/pitch/speed/volume; feed recognized attributes to an LLM with a writer-style instruction to compose multiple diverse text prompts per utterance. 3) Train the prompt encoder + TTS to condition on prompt embeddings. 4) Train the Variation Network as a diffusion model that, given prompt representation, predicts the residual voice-variability vector representing details absent from the prompt; at inference sample this vector to obtain diverse voices consistent with the prompt. Key components: diffusion-based variation prediction; reference-speech-derived target variability during training.
- **Key results:** Reports improved consistency between generated voice and text prompt and the ability to sample diverse voices, versus prior text-prompt TTS (PromptTTS, InstructTTS) baselines; gains shown via prompt-attribute accuracy and subjective/objective consistency and diversity metrics on the 44K-hour setup.
- **Notes:** Microsoft Research. The 'synthetic' contribution is automatic text-prompt labeling, not synthetic audio. Diffusion-based variation modeling is the core of the diversity claim.

### 109. QS-TTS: Towards Semi-Supervised Text-to-Speech Synthesis via Vector-Quantized Self-Supervised Speech Representation Learning

_Haohan Guo, Fenglong Xie, Jiawen Kang, Yujia Xiao, Xixin Wu, Helen Meng · 2023 · arXiv (CUHK / Xiaomi); related MSMC-TTS line published at ICASSP/Interspeech_

- **Categories:** C5
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2309.00126) · arXiv:2309.00126 · [code](https://github.com/hhguo/MSMC-TTS)
- **Synthetic-data role:** No generated speech is synthesized for data augmentation; instead large amounts of UNLABELED real speech audio are exploited via vector-quantized self-supervised representation learning (VQ-S3RL). The 'synthetic'/self-supervised element is the learned discrete VQ representation (MSMC tokens and a compact VQ-VAE abstraction) derived self-supervisedly from unlabeled audio, which acts as an intermediate pseudo-target so that the text-to-representation model can be trained with very little paired (text,audio) supervised data. Unlabeled audio reduces the supervised-data requirement in low-resource settings.
- **Method:** Semi-supervised TTS framework (VQ-S3RL) using two vector-quantized self-supervised representation learners. A principal learner produces a generative Multi-Stage Multi-Codebook (MSMC) VQ representation via an MSMC-VQ-GAN combined with contrastive self-supervised learning and decodes it back to high-quality audio; an associate learner further abstracts the MSMC representation into a highly compact VQ representation through a VQ-VAE. TTS then predicts these compact discrete tokens from text and expands them to MSMC and to waveform, so most of the pipeline can be trained on unlabeled speech with only a small labeled subset.
- **Architecture:** MSMC-VQ-GAN autoencoder (multi-stage multi-codebook VQ-VAE encoder/decoder fused with a HiFiGAN-style GAN decoder/vocoder) + contrastive S3RL; associate VQ-VAE for compact tokenization; transformer/acoustic text-to-token (text-to-MSMC) predictor. Builds on the MSMC-TTS-v2 design (MSMC-VQ-VAE + HiFiGAN).
- **Base models:** Builds on the authors' prior MSMC-TTS / MSMC-VQ-GAN (Multi-Stage Multi-Codebook VQ-VAE + HiFiGAN). Self-supervised contrastive learning component initializes representation learners. No external pretrained LLM.
- **Datasets:** Standard TTS corpora used in low-resource/semi-supervised splits (multilingual/low-resource setups; LJSpeech-style and low-resource language data per the MSMC-TTS line). Unlabeled speech audio used for self-supervised representation learning; supervised labeled data deliberately reduced. Exact hours not consistently reported in abstract.
- **Code:** https://github.com/hhguo/MSMC-TTS (official MSMC-TTS / MSMC-VQ-GAN implementation); demo https://hhguo.github.io/DemoQSTTS/
- **Recipe:** 1) Train principal VQ-S3R learner: an MSMC-VQ-GAN autoencoder on (largely unlabeled) speech, jointly with contrastive self-supervised learning, to encode audio into multi-stage multi-codebook discrete tokens and decode them back to high-fidelity waveform (HiFiGAN-style adversarial training). 2) Train associate learner: a VQ-VAE that compresses the MSMC representation into a highly compact VQ token sequence. 3) Train the text-to-token TTS model to map text to the compact VQ tokens using the limited labeled (text,audio) pairs; expand compact tokens -> MSMC -> waveform via the pretrained decoders. 4) Evaluate by progressively reducing the amount of supervised data to test low-resource robustness. Reproduce stages via the hhguo/MSMC-TTS repo (MSMC-TTS-v2 config).
- **Key results:** Highest MOS versus supervised and semi-supervised baseline TTS systems, with the advantage most pronounced in low-resource conditions; synthesis quality degrades more gracefully than baselines as supervised data is reduced; superior intelligibility and audio quality across representations tested. Exact MOS values not given in abstract.
- **Notes:** Primary contribution is unlabeled-data efficiency rather than generated-speech augmentation, so categorized under self-training/pseudo-labeling/representation learning (5). Strong for low-resource TTS. Code is the MSMC-TTS repo rather than a QS-TTS-specific repo.

### 110. TextrolSpeech: A Text Style Control Speech Corpus With Codec Language Text-to-Speech Models

_Shengpeng Ji, Jialong Zuo, Minghui Fang, Ziyue Jiang, Feiyang Chen, Xinyu Duan, Baoxing Huai, Zhou Zhao · 2023 · ICASSP 2024 (arXiv 2023)_

- **Categories:** C4, C6, C7
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2308.14430) · arXiv:2308.14430 · [code](https://github.com/jishengpeng/TextrolSpeech)
- **Synthetic-data role:** LLM-generated text: natural-language style descriptions/prompts are synthesized at scale by GPT via a multi-stage prompt-programming approach, then paired with (largely existing) labeled speech to form 236,220 style-prompt to speech pairs. The audio is real/existing labeled speech; the natural-language style captions are the synthetic component.
- **Method:** Two contributions: (1) TextrolSpeech, the first large-scale text-style-controllable TTS corpus, whose natural style descriptions are generated by a GPT model through multi-stage prompt programming over five style factors; (2) Salle, a codec-language-model TTS that treats text-controllable TTS as a language-model task over audio codec codes.
- **Architecture:** Salle: autoregressive codec language model (VALL-E-style) using neural audio codec discrete codes as the intermediate representation, conditioned on the natural-language style prompt; codec for tokenization and waveform synthesis. Data side: GPT for caption generation.
- **Base models:** GPT (used for generating natural style descriptions via prompt programming). Salle TTS builds on the codec-LM / VALL-E paradigm with a neural audio codec. Source speech aggregated from existing labeled emotional/style speech datasets.
- **Datasets:** TextrolSpeech: 236,220 (style-prompt, speech) pairs covering five style factors (gender, pitch, speaking speed, volume/energy, emotion) with natural-language descriptions. Source audio aggregated from existing public speech corpora (emotion + read speech).
- **Code:** https://github.com/jishengpeng/TextrolSpeech (demo: sall-e.github.io)
- **Recipe:** 1) Aggregate existing labeled speech and derive five discrete style factors per utterance. 2) Multi-stage prompt programming with GPT: convert structured style labels into diverse natural-language style descriptions at scale, yielding 236,220 prompt-speech pairs. 3) Tokenize audio with a neural audio codec into discrete codes. 4) Train Salle as an autoregressive codec language model conditioned on the style prompt to predict codec codes, then decode to waveform. 5) Evaluate controllability across the five style factors plus speech quality vs baselines.
- **Key results:** Reports comparable/superior performance on the text-controllable TTS task; evaluates style-control accuracy across the five factors plus audio quality (specific numeric MOS/accuracy not given in abstract).
- **Notes:** Pioneering large style-prompt corpus; the synthetic-data angle is GPT-generated natural-language style captions (category 4/7). Audio itself is not synthetic.

### 111. ASR data augmentation in low-resource settings using cross-lingual multi-speaker TTS and cross-lingual voice conversion

_Edresson Casanova, Christopher Shulby, Alexander Korolev, Arnaldo Candido Junior, Anderson da Silva Soares, Sandra Aluisio, Moacir Antonelli Ponti · 2022 (Interspeech 2023) · Interspeech 2023 (arXiv 2022)_

- **Categories:** C4, C5, C6
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2204.00618) · arXiv:2204.00618 · [code](https://github.com/Edresson/YourTTS)
- **Synthetic-data role:** Synthetic speech is the augmentation data for ASR training. A cross-lingual multi-speaker TTS (YourTTS) and cross-lingual voice conversion synthesize new utterances in the target language. The central finding: using only ONE real target-language speaker, the TTS/VC can generate diverse multi-speaker synthetic audio that augments ASR, closing the gap to ASR trained on real speech and to prior work that needed many real speakers.
- **Method:** Use zero-shot cross-lingual multi-speaker TTS and cross-lingual voice conversion to fabricate training audio for ASR in low/medium-resource languages. Demonstrate that a single real speaker in the target language is enough seed material: synthesize varied speech (text from target-language corpora; voices/styles transferred cross-lingually) and add it to the ASR training set, substantially reducing WER and nearly matching ASR trained on human speech.
- **Architecture:** TTS/VC: YourTTS - a VITS-based (flow + variational autoencoder + adversarial training, HiFi-GAN decoder) zero-shot multi-speaker, multilingual model with an external speaker encoder (H/ASP / speaker embeddings) enabling zero-shot voice cloning and cross-lingual voice conversion. ASR: a downstream end-to-end recognizer (the augmentation target) trained on real + synthetic mixes.
- **Base models:** YourTTS (Casanova et al. 2022) as the cross-lingual TTS and voice-conversion engine, with a pretrained speaker encoder for speaker conditioning. The ASR model is trained/evaluated on the augmented data.
- **Datasets:** Low/medium-resource target-language corpora (text and limited real audio) plus higher-resource source-language data for the cross-lingual TTS/VC; synthetic speech generated by YourTTS. Exact corpus names and hours not reported in the abstract.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Take YourTTS pretrained on higher-resource languages with zero-shot multi-speaker capability. 2) For the low-resource target language, use as little as a single real target-language speaker to enable cross-lingual synthesis/voice conversion. 3) Generate synthetic target-language utterances: feed target-language text and transfer speaker identities/styles cross-lingually (TTS) and/or convert source-language audio to the target speaker (VC). 4) Mix synthetic audio with available real audio to train the ASR model. 5) Evaluate WER on real test data; compare ASR trained on synthetic vs human speech and against multi-speaker-augmentation baselines. Exact ASR architecture, synthetic hours, and optimizer settings not reported in the abstract.
- **Key results:** Reported WER improvement from roughly 64-74% down to ~34-37% (about a 33% absolute reduction) via the augmentation. Closes the gap between ASR trained on synthesized vs human speech, and matches prior multi-speaker augmentation while using only a single real target-language speaker.
- **Notes:** Same first author (Casanova) as YourTTS and XTTS. Strong example of category 5 (data augmentation / synthetic-speech-for-ASR) and category 6 (cross-lingual). Metrics quoted from secondary search sources; verify exact numbers and language pairs in the Interspeech 2023 camera-ready (ISCA archive casanova23_interspeech).

### 112. Cross-speaker style transfer for text-to-speech using data augmentation

_Manuel Sam Ribeiro, Julian Roth, Giulia Comini, Goeric Huybrechts, Adam Gabrys, Jaime Lorenzo-Trueba · 2022 · ICASSP 2022_

- **Categories:** C6, C5, C2
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2202.05083) · arXiv:2202.05083
- **Synthetic-data role:** Synthetic data is voice-converted expressive speech. Expressive recordings from one or more supporting speakers are converted via voice conversion to the target speaker's voice, producing synthetic expressive samples in the target identity. This synthetic expressive data is pooled with the target speaker's natural neutral recordings to train a single-speaker multi-style TTS, so the target speaker never needs to record the expressive style.
- **Method:** Build an expressive TTS in a target speaker's voice using only neutral target data plus expressive data from supporting speakers: use VC to transfer the supporting speakers' expressive style into the target voice, creating augmented training data, then train one multi-style TTS on natural neutral + synthetic expressive speech. Scales to many supporting speakers and languages.
- **Architecture:** Pipeline of a voice-conversion model (to generate target-voice expressive data) feeding a neural TTS acoustic model with a neural vocoder (Tacotron-class sequence-to-sequence acoustic model + neural vocoder; style/speaker conditioning). Exact sizes not reported.
- **Base models:** Relies on a voice conversion model and a neural TTS+vocoder stack; specific pretrained checkpoints not reported.
- **Datasets:** Internal multi-speaker, multi-style corpora: target speaker neutral recordings plus expressive recordings from supporting speakers; scales to 14 speakers across 7 languages. Specific hours not reported.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Collect neutral recordings from the target speaker and expressive recordings from one or more supporting speakers. 2) Train/apply voice conversion to convert supporting-speaker expressive speech into the target speaker's voice, producing synthetic expressive samples (variable number of supporting speakers and data quantities supported). 3) Merge synthetic expressive data with the target's natural neutral data into one training set. 4) Train a single-speaker multi-style TTS (acoustic model + neural vocoder) with style conditioning so it can render the expressive style in the target voice. 5) Optionally use multiple reference voices for style controllability. Evaluate style similarity vs naturalness/speaker-identity trade-off. Cross-lingual scaling tested to 14 speakers / 7 languages.
- **Key results:** Synthetic samples improve style similarity to the intended expressive style while retaining the target speaker's identity, consistently across the multi-speaker/multi-language setup; evaluated via subjective listening tests. Specific MOS numbers not reported in available materials.
- **Notes:** Amazon (Alexa TTS) work; ICASSP 2022, 5 pages. Companion to the 2020 low-resource data-augmentation paper by overlapping authors. Internal proprietary data; no code or precise hours released.

### 113. Generating Multilingual Gender-Ambiguous Text-to-Speech Voices

_Konstantinos Markopoulos, Georgia Maniati, Georgios Vamvoukakis, Nikolaos Ellinas, Georgios Vardaxoglou, Panos Kakoulidis, Junkwang Oh, Gunu Jho, Inchul Hwang, Aimilios Chalamandaris, Pirros Tsiakoulis, Spyros Raptis (Innoetics / Samsung Electronics) · 2022 · Interspeech 2023_

- **Categories:** C6
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2211.00375) · arXiv:2211.00375 · [code](https://innoetics.github.io/publications/gender-ambiguous/index.html)
- **Synthetic-data role:** The synthetic artifact is the speaker identity itself: novel gender-ambiguous voices are generated by sampling new points in the learned 256-dim speaker-embedding space (no such speaker exists in the training data). These synthetic speaker embeddings drive the multi-speaker TTS to render entirely new, non-recorded voices. No synthetic training audio is added to the corpus; the synthesis is of new speakers/voices at inference time.
- **Method:** Reduce the multi-speaker embedding space to 2D via PCA, fit kernel-density estimates for male and female speakers, then define a gender-aware pseudo-density that peaks where male and female densities are similar (i.e., gender-ambiguous regions). Sample new embeddings along the local-maxima path of this density and map them back to the full embedding dimension to synthesize novel gender-ambiguous voices across languages.
- **Architecture:** Non-attentive Tacotron acoustic model with 256-dim learnable speaker embeddings (lookup table, broadcast-concatenated to phoneme encoder outputs), LSTM-based duration predictor, Gaussian upsampler, 5-layer convolutional postnet; LPCNet vocoder. Speaker-space modeling via PCA (to 2D) + kernel density estimation; gender measured with d-vectors.
- **Base models:** Multi-speaker non-attentive Tacotron + LPCNet trained in-house on the multilingual corpus; gender classification/verification uses d-vector speaker embeddings. No external pretrained LM. Not reported beyond these.
- **Datasets:** 1,391 hours across 1,196 gender-balanced speakers in 5 languages (English, Korean, Spanish, German, French). Sources include LibriTTS, VCTK, and proprietary recordings. Synthetic = newly sampled speaker embeddings (5 generated gender-ambiguous voices), not new corpus audio.
- **Code:** https://innoetics.github.io/publications/gender-ambiguous/index.html
- **Recipe:** 1) Train multi-speaker non-attentive Tacotron + LPCNet on the 1,391h / 1,196-speaker multilingual data with 256-dim learnable speaker embeddings. 2) Collect all speaker embeddings; apply PCA to reduce to 2D. 3) Fit separate kernel-density estimates for male and female speakers in 2D. 4) Define a pseudo-density function that is high where male/female densities are similar. 5) Sample candidate embeddings along the local-maxima path of this gender-ambiguous density (5 voices). 6) Populate remaining (non-2D) dimensions by either (a) inverse-PCA with zeros, or (b) interpolating between nearest male/female speakers. 7) Synthesize and evaluate gender ambiguity (d-vectors, UMAP) and naturalness (MOS) per language.
- **Key results:** Objective (d-vector) and subjective MOS tests across 5 languages, 114 subjects, ~15,432 gender ratings: generated voices judged more gender-ambiguous than the baseline (mean-of-all-speakers) voice, are diverse and consistent, and gender perception is robust across listener native-language and gender. Claimed first systematic, validated approach for generating multiple gender-ambiguous voices. Exact MOS numbers not reported here.
- **Notes:** Proprietary corpus and TTS; only demo page public, no model weights/code. Builds on the related work 'Generating gender-ambiguous voices for privacy-preserving speech recognition.'

### 114. Nix-TTS: Lightweight and End-to-End Text-to-Speech via Module-wise Distillation

_Rendi Chevi, Radityo Eko Prasojo, Alham Fikri Aji, Andros Tjandra, Sakriani Sakti · 2022 · IEEE SLT 2022_

- **Categories:** C2, C5
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2203.15643) · arXiv:2203.15643 · [code](https://github.com/rendchevi/nix-tts)
- **Synthetic-data role:** Knowledge distillation rather than explicit synthetic corpora: a large non-autoregressive end-to-end teacher (VITS-style) provides soft targets / intermediate module outputs that supervise the compact student. The teacher's predicted alignments and latent/intermediate representations act as pseudo-labels for module-wise distillation, letting the student learn without a separate vocoder. No external machine-generated dataset beyond teacher-derived supervision is reported.
- **Method:** Compresses a large end-to-end (VITS-style) TTS teacher into a 5.23M-parameter student via module-wise (encoder and decoder) knowledge distillation, enabling flexible, independent compression of each module while remaining fully end-to-end (no separate vocoder).
- **Architecture:** End-to-end non-autoregressive TTS in the VITS family (text encoder + stochastic duration/alignment + flow/variational decoder generating waveform directly). Student is a heavily compressed version: 5.23M parameters total (89.34% reduction vs. teacher). Distillation applied independently to encoder and decoder modules.
- **Base models:** Teacher is a large pretrained non-autoregressive end-to-end TTS model (VITS-style) trained on LJSpeech (per the paper); student is distilled from this teacher.
- **Datasets:** LJSpeech (single-speaker English, ~24h) used for teacher and student (per the paper). Pretrained student models and audio samples released. Exact splits follow standard LJSpeech.
- **Code:** https://github.com/rendchevi/nix-tts
- **Recipe:** 1) Train or take a large end-to-end VITS-style teacher on LJSpeech. 2) Define a compact student with the same modular structure (encoder, decoder) but far fewer channels/layers (target ~5.23M params). 3) Apply module-wise distillation: independently distill the encoder and decoder from the teacher, matching intermediate representations/outputs and using teacher-derived alignments to avoid needing ground-truth durations. 4) Train the student end-to-end so it generates waveform directly (no separate vocoder). 5) Evaluate naturalness/intelligibility and CPU/edge latency (Intel i7, Raspberry Pi 3B). Exact loss weights and layer configs are in the repo.
- **Key results:** 5.23M params (89.34% smaller than teacher); 3.04x faster inference on Intel i7 CPU and 8.36x faster on Raspberry Pi 3B; maintains fair voice naturalness and intelligibility (MOS) compared to the teacher.
- **Notes:** Accepted at IEEE SLT 2022. Verified via arXiv and official GitHub (rendchevi/nix-tts). Teacher=VITS and dataset=LJSpeech inferred from the paper; specific MOS numbers and exact distillation losses are in the full text/repo.

### 115. Simple and Effective Unsupervised Speech Synthesis

_Alexander H. Liu, Cheng-I Jeff Lai, Wei-Ning Hsu, Michael Auli, Alexei Baevski, James Glass (MIT CSAIL, Meta AI) · 2022 · Interspeech 2022_

- **Categories:** C1, C4, C5, C7
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2204.02524) · arXiv:2204.02524 · [code](https://people.csail.mit.edu/clai24/unsup-tts/)
- **Synthetic-data role:** Core mechanism. The system has no human-labeled speech-text pairs. An unsupervised ASR model (wav2vec-U 2.0), trained without paired data, transcribes untranscribed speech to produce machine-generated pseudo-labels (pseudo-transcripts). These ASR-generated pseudo-labels are paired with the original audio to form a fully machine-annotated training corpus, which then trains a standard supervised-style TTS model. The labels are entirely synthetic; the audio and text are real but unaligned/unlabeled.
- **Method:** First unsupervised TTS recipe: combine unsupervised ASR (wav2vec-U 2.0) to pseudo-label untranscribed speech, then train an otherwise standard neural TTS model on the resulting machine-annotated (audio, pseudo-transcript) pairs. Requires only unlabeled audio, unlabeled text, and a lexicon (for phonemization), no human-annotated corpus.
- **Architecture:** Two-stage pipeline. Stage 1: wav2vec-U 2.0 unsupervised ASR (self-supervised wav2vec 2.0 features + GAN-based adversarial training mapping speech representations to phonemes) generates pseudo-labels. Stage 2: a conventional neural TTS acoustic model trained with the same supervised learning paradigm as standard TTS, fed the pseudo-labeled data, paired with a neural vocoder for waveform generation. Exact acoustic-model/vocoder backbone sizes not detailed on the abstract page.
- **Base models:** wav2vec-U 2.0 (built on self-supervised wav2vec 2.0 speech representations) for the unsupervised ASR / pseudo-labeling; a standard neural TTS acoustic model + neural vocoder for synthesis. Initializes the labeling pipeline from pretrained self-supervised speech features.
- **Datasets:** Uses unlabeled speech audio + unlabeled text + a lexicon (standard English TTS/ASR corpora used in unpaired fashion; LJSpeech-style single-speaker setup and LibriSpeech-style unlabeled speech are typical for this line of work). Synthetic component is the wav2vec-U 2.0 pseudo-transcripts. Exact hours not stated on abstract page.
- **Code:** Project page: https://people.csail.mit.edu/clai24/unsup-tts/ ; paper PDF: https://sls.csail.mit.edu/publications/2022/AlexLiu_Interspeech_2022.pdf
- **Recipe:** 1) Collect unlabeled speech, unlabeled text, and a pronunciation lexicon (phonemize text). 2) Train wav2vec-U 2.0: extract wav2vec 2.0 self-supervised features from speech, train a GAN that adversarially maps quantized/segmented speech features to phoneme sequences using only the unpaired text distribution; no paired supervision. 3) Run wav2vec-U 2.0 over the untranscribed speech to emit pseudo-phoneme/pseudo-text labels, forming a machine-annotated parallel corpus. 4) Train a standard supervised-style neural TTS model (acoustic model + vocoder) on these (audio, pseudo-label) pairs exactly as if they were ground-truth transcripts. 5) Evaluate with human MOS for naturalness/intelligibility against a supervised TTS counterpart trained on the same audio with true labels.
- **Key results:** Human evaluation shows the unsupervised TTS produces speech of similar naturalness and intelligibility to its supervised counterpart trained with ground-truth transcripts, demonstrating viable TTS without any human annotation.
- **Notes:** First demonstration of fully unsupervised TTS. Quality is bounded by wav2vec-U 2.0 pseudo-label accuracy. A NAACL-SRW companion ('Towards Unsupervised Speech Synthesis') exists. Concrete dataset hours and backbone sizes require the full PDF.

### 116. Speaker consistency loss and step-wise optimization for semi-supervised joint training of TTS and ASR using unpaired text data

_Naoki Makishima, Satoshi Suzuki, Atsushi Ando, Ryo Masumura (NTT Corporation, Japan) · 2022 · INTERSPEECH 2022 (Makishima et al.)_

- **Categories:** C2, C5
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2207.04659) · arXiv:2207.04659
- **Synthetic-data role:** Synthetic SPEECH is produced by a multispeaker TTS model from large amounts of UNPAIRED TEXT (with a reference speech), forming a TTS->ASR cycle; the ASR model reconstructs text from the synthesized speech and both are trained with cycle-consistency loss. Synthetic speech augments ASR/TTS training from text-only data. The paper notes a failure mode: vanilla synthesized speech ignores reference speaker traits and becomes too easy for ASR, hurting both models; their fixes (speaker consistency loss + step-wise optimization) constrain how this synthetic data is used.
- **Method:** Improves cycle-consistency-based semi-supervised joint TTS+ASR training with two additions: (1) a speaker consistency loss that pulls synthesized speech's speaker characteristics toward the reference speech, and (2) step-wise optimization that first freezes the TTS model before jointly training both, to prevent the TTS from over-adapting to the ASR (which would make synthetic speech degenerate and trivially recognizable).
- **Architecture:** TTS-ASR pipeline: a multispeaker TTS (reference-speech conditioned) synthesizes speech from text; an end-to-end ASR reconstructs text. Trained with cycle-consistency loss plus an added speaker consistency loss (speaker-embedding matching between synthesized and reference speech). Exact backbone sizes not reported in fetched material.
- **Base models:** Pretrained multispeaker TTS and ASR models used as the cycle endpoints; specific pretrained checkpoints not reported.
- **Datasets:** Small paired data + large unpaired text data (semi-supervised setting). Specific corpus names/hours not reported in fetched material.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Pre-train multispeaker TTS and ASR on the small paired set. 2) For unpaired text, synthesize speech with TTS conditioned on a reference utterance; feed synthetic speech to ASR; apply cycle-consistency loss (text reconstruction). 3) Add speaker consistency loss = match speaker characteristics/embedding of synthesized speech to the reference speech. 4) Step-wise optimization: first FREEZE TTS parameters while training ASR (and the cycle), avoiding TTS over-adaptation, before any joint update. Specific LR/batch hyperparameters not reported in fetched material; see ISCA archive PDF for details.
- **Key results:** Reports improvements in both TTS quality and ASR accuracy over the vanilla cycle-consistency baseline by adding speaker consistency loss and step-wise optimization; exact WER/MOS numbers not captured in fetched material (available in ISCA archive PDF).
- **Notes:** Published at INTERSPEECH 2022. Directly addresses the degeneracy of TTS->ASR cycle training when synthetic speech loses speaker traits. Builds conceptually on cycle-consistency ASR work (Hori et al. 2018). Numeric metrics not extracted here.

### 117. Training Text-To-Speech Systems From Synthetic Data: A Practical Approach For Accent Transfer Tasks

_Lev Finkelstein, Heiga Zen, Norman Casagrande, Chun-an Chan, Ye Jia, Tom Kenter, Alexey Petelin, Jonathan Shen, Vincent Wan, Yu Zhang, Yonghui Wu, Rob Clark (Google) · 2022 · Interspeech 2022_

- **Categories:** C1, C6, C7
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2208.13183) · arXiv:2208.13183
- **Synthetic-data role:** The entire training corpus for the target (student) TTS system is synthetic: a less-robust but high-quality accent-transfer Tacotron model generates accent-transferred speech (a speaker's voice rendered in a target accent), and this fully synthetic audio is used to train a robust production TTS model. ~100% of the student's acoustic training targets are teacher-generated; the teacher itself was trained on real multi-speaker, multi-accent data.
- **Method:** Knowledge-distillation-style accent transfer: a high-quality but unstable Tacotron model performs the hard accent-transfer task and is used purely as a data generator. Its outputs train a separate, robust TTS system (CHiVE-BERT), transferring the accent-transfer capability into a stable model while preserving target speaker identity and speaking style.
- **Architecture:** Teacher: Tacotron-style attention-based seq2seq acoustic model capable of accent transfer (less robust). Student: CHiVE-BERT, a monolingual TTS system using a hierarchical variational prosody model (CHiVE) conditioned on BERT-derived linguistic features, plus a neural vocoder for waveform generation.
- **Base models:** Teacher = Tacotron accent-transfer model trained on real speech; Student = CHiVE-BERT TTS architecture (trained fresh on synthetic teacher outputs).
- **Datasets:** Real data for the teacher: proprietary Google multi-speaker, multi-accent English TTS speech data (hours not reported). Synthetic data: accent-transferred utterances generated by the Tacotron teacher, used as the student's training set (scale not reported).
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Train a Tacotron-based accent-transfer TTS teacher on real multi-speaker multi-accent data; it renders a source speaker's voice in a target accent but is unstable. 2) Use the teacher to synthesize a large set of accent-transferred utterances (target speaker + target accent), accepting some quality loss. 3) Train a robust CHiVE-BERT student TTS model from this synthetic corpus only. 4) Student inherits accent transfer while gaining production-grade stability and preserving speaker characteristics/style. Hyperparameters and data scale not reported.
- **Key results:** Subjective evaluations (MOS / preference) show the student trained on synthetic data produces high-quality audio with successful accent transfer while preserving speaker style; demonstrates synthetic-data training as a practical path to robust accent transfer. Exact MOS values not reported in abstract.
- **Notes:** Google authors; proprietary data, no public code or corpus. Foundational example of teacher-to-student fully-synthetic TTS training corpora for accent transfer.

### 118. Unsupervised Text-to-Speech Synthesis by Unsupervised Automatic Speech Recognition

_Junrui Ni, Liming Wang, Heting Gao, Kaizhi Qian, Yang Zhang, Shiyu Chang, Mark Hasegawa-Johnson · 2022 · Interspeech 2022_

- **Categories:** C5, C6
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2203.15796) · arXiv:2203.15796 · [code](https://github.com/lwang114/UnsupTTS)
- **Synthetic-data role:** Pseudo-labels are the entire supervision signal: an unsupervised ASR (alignment module) transcribes untranscribed speech into pseudo-text/pseudo-phoneme labels, and the TTS synthesis module is trained on these (speech, pseudo-text) pairs. No human transcriptions are used; the pseudo-transcripts bridge the unpaired speech and unpaired text. At inference, real text replaces pseudo-text. About 10-20 hours of untranscribed speech per language is pseudo-labeled.
- **Method:** First fully unsupervised end-to-end TTS: uses unsupervised ASR (wav2vec-U style) to produce pseudo-transcriptions for untranscribed speech, then trains a TTS synthesis model on the pseudo-text-to-speech pairs. Training uses pseudo-text; inference uses real text. Avoids any parallel speech-text data, enabling TTS for low-resource languages.
- **Architecture:** Two modules: (1) an alignment/recognition module based on unsupervised ASR (wav2vec-U-style, using self-supervised speech features + GAN-based unsupervised phoneme recognition) that outputs pseudo-phoneme transcriptions; (2) a synthesis module, a sequence-to-sequence / feed-forward neural TTS (FastSpeech-style with duration modeling) trained on pseudo-text, plus a neural vocoder for waveform generation.
- **Base models:** Builds on unsupervised ASR (wav2vec-U / wav2vec 2.0 self-supervised speech representations for the alignment module) and a standard neural TTS acoustic model + vocoder for synthesis. Initializes the alignment module from self-supervised speech features.
- **Datasets:** Multilingual untranscribed speech plus unpaired text across seven languages, roughly 10-20 hours of speech each. (LibriSpeech-style English and additional language corpora; exact corpus names per language not all listed in abstract.)
- **Code:** https://github.com/lwang114/UnsupTTS
- **Recipe:** 1) Extract self-supervised speech features (wav2vec 2.0) from untranscribed speech. 2) Train an unsupervised ASR (wav2vec-U: adversarial/GAN training to map speech segments to phonemes using only unpaired text for the phoneme language model) to produce pseudo-phoneme transcriptions of the speech. 3) Pair each untranscribed utterance with its pseudo-transcription to form (pseudo-text, speech) training pairs. 4) Train a neural TTS acoustic model (FastSpeech-style with duration prediction) on these pseudo-paired data, plus a neural vocoder. 5) At inference, feed real text (converted to phonemes) instead of pseudo-text to synthesize speech. Repeat per language with ~10-20 h speech each. Quality of pseudo-transcripts from unsupervised ASR is the main bottleneck.
- **Key results:** Achieves performance comparable to a supervised TTS system across seven languages using only ~10-20 hours of untranscribed speech and unpaired text per language, with no parallel data. (Exact per-language MOS/CER numbers in the full paper; demo samples and code released.)
- **Notes:** Interspeech 2022; first end-to-end fully unsupervised TTS. Strong multilingual/low-resource relevance. Official code at github.com/lwang114/UnsupTTS; demo page available. Headline metric magnitudes not fully surfaced from search snippets.

### 119. Virtuoso: Massive Multilingual Speech-Text Joint Semi-Supervised Learning for Text-To-Speech

_Takaaki Saeki, Heiga Zen, Zhehuai Chen, Nobuyuki Morioka, Gary Wang, Yu Zhang, Ankur Bapna, Andrew Rosenberg, Bhuvana Ramabhadran · 2022 · ICASSP 2023 (arXiv Oct 2022)_

- **Categories:** C1, C5, C6
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2210.15447) · arXiv:2210.15447 · [code](https://google.github.io/tacotron/publications/virtuoso/)
- **Synthetic-data role:** Semi-supervised joint learning that exploits non-paired data: untranscribed speech and unspoken (text-only) data are folded into TTS training alongside paired TTS and ASR data. Rather than generating explicit synthetic speech corpora, it learns shared speech-text representations (extending Maestro's RNN-T/duration-based alignment and consistency objectives) so that text-only and speech-only data act as pseudo-supervision, enabling intelligible TTS even for unseen languages with no high-quality paired TTS data.
- **Method:** Extends the Maestro speech-text joint pretraining framework (built for ASR) to TTS generation, scaling multilingual TTS toward hundreds of languages by jointly using four data types under tailored training schemes so low-resource and zero-paired-data languages benefit from shared representations.
- **Architecture:** Speech-text joint encoder built on Maestro (shared encoder with text/duration-upsampled representations and modality-matching/consistency losses); generative TTS decoder producing acoustic features plus a neural vocoder. Distinct training schemes for supervised (paired TTS + ASR) vs unsupervised (untranscribed speech, unspoken text) streams. Exact layer/parameter sizes not reported in abstract.
- **Base models:** Maestro (speech-text semi-supervised joint pretraining framework originally for ASR) as the foundation it extends to generation.
- **Datasets:** Paired TTS data, ASR data (transcribed speech), untranscribed speech, and unspoken text spanning a large multilingual set ('hundreds of languages'). Specific corpus names and hours not enumerated in the abstract.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Initialize/extend a Maestro-style speech-text joint encoder. 2) Ingest four data streams: (a) paired TTS, (b) ASR, (c) untranscribed speech, (d) unspoken text. 3) Apply differentiated training schemes per stream (supervised generation/recognition objectives on paired data; representation-alignment / consistency objectives leveraging speech-only and text-only data). 4) Attach a TTS decoder + vocoder to synthesize speech from learned shared representations. 5) Evaluate naturalness (MOS) and intelligibility (CER/WER) on seen and unseen languages vs baseline multilingual TTS. Exact loss weights, encoder sizes, vocoder choice, and per-language hours are not reported in the abstract; full paper / Google Research publication required for precise replication.
- **Key results:** Qualitative/relative: significantly better naturalness and intelligibility than baseline multilingual TTS on seen languages; produces reasonably intelligible and natural speech for unseen languages with no high-quality paired TTS data. Specific MOS/CER values not provided in abstract.
- **Notes:** Google Research / Tacotron team. Demo page exists; no public code. Numerical tables require the full ICASSP 2023 paper. Verified via arXiv, Google Research publications page, and IEEE Xplore.

### 120. Voice Filter: Few-shot Text-to-Speech Speaker Adaptation Using Voice Conversion as a Post-Processing Module

_Adam Gabrys, Goeric Huybrechts, Manuel Sam Ribeiro, Chung-Ming Chien, Julian Roth, Giulia Comini, Roberto Barra-Chicote, Bartek Perz, Jaime Lorenzo-Trueba · 2022 · ICASSP 2022_

- **Categories:** C2, C5, C6
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2202.08164) · arXiv:2202.08164
- **Synthetic-data role:** Synthetic speech is central. A duration-controllable pre-existing TTS system is used to GENERATE a parallel speech corpus: for each target-speaker utterance, the TTS produces a time-aligned source-voice version of the same content. These TTS-generated synthetic source recordings, paired with the real target-speaker audio, form the parallel training data for the voice-conversion post-processing module. Thus one half of every training pair is synthetic, produced by the system's own TTS.
- **Method:** Reframe extremely low-resource (about 1 minute) few-shot TTS as a voice-conversion task. Keep a fixed high-quality source TTS voice, then train a VC module that converts the source TTS output into the target speaker's voice. The VC module is appended as a post-processing stage to any existing TTS, so the TTS itself never needs target-speaker fine-tuning.
- **Architecture:** Two-stage pipeline: (1) a pre-existing high-quality, duration-controllable multi-speaker TTS acoustic model that produces source-voice mel-spectrograms; (2) a voice-conversion post-processing module (mel-to-mel converter conditioned on target speaker embedding) followed by a neural vocoder (HiFi-GAN-class) to render the target-speaker waveform. Duration controllability is used to make the generated source data time-aligned (parallel) with the target recordings.
- **Base models:** A pre-existing high-quality duration-controllable multi-speaker TTS system (used both to synthesize parallel source data and as the runtime front stage) plus a neural vocoder; the VC module is trained on top.
- **Datasets:** Multi-speaker data to build the source TTS and VC module; target speakers each with as little as 1 minute of real speech; synthetic parallel corpus generated by the duration-controllable TTS. Specific corpus names/hours not stated in fetched content; evaluation on a diverse set of voices.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Build/obtain a high-quality duration-controllable multi-speaker source TTS. 2) For the VC training data, run the source TTS on the transcripts of available speech to synthesize source-voice utterances that are duration-matched (parallel) to the natural recordings, creating (synthetic-source, natural-target) mel pairs. 3) Train the VC post-processing module to map source-voice mel to target-voice mel, conditioned on a target speaker representation; train the vocoder to render waveforms. 4) For a new target speaker with about 1 minute of audio, adapt the VC module (and/or speaker embedding) to that speaker. 5) Inference: source TTS generates speech in the fixed source voice, then the VC module converts it to the target speaker as post-processing. Key design point: parallel data is obtained for free from the duration-controllable TTS, avoiding the need for natural parallel corpora.
- **Key results:** With only 1 minute of target speech, Voice Filter outperforms state-of-the-art few-shot TTS baselines on both objective and subjective metrics, and is competitive with models trained on roughly 30x more target data.
- **Notes:** Amazon Science / Alexa work. Uses synthetic TTS-generated parallel data to train a VC module (cats 5 data augmentation and 2 adaptation; 6 because it generalizes across diverse voices). Exact dataset names, vocoder identity, and full MOS/SIM numbers not captured from fetched pages.

### 121. EAT: Enhanced ASR-TTS for Self-supervised Speech Recognition

_Murali Karthick Baskar, Lukas Burget, Shinji Watanabe, Ramon Fernandez Astudillo, Jan Cernocky · 2021 · ICASSP 2021 (arXiv preprint)_

- **Categories:** C5, C7
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2104.07474) · arXiv:2104.07474
- **Synthetic-data role:** Synthetic speech and pseudo-text are generated in the ASR<->TTS loop for self-supervised training. In the ASR->TTS direction, an external language model rescores/penalizes weak ASR hypotheses before they are turned into synthetic speech, raising the quality of the generated pseudo-pairs. In the TTS->ASR direction, a scaling hyperparameter weights the attention/context from synthesized speech so that low-fidelity synthetic audio (especially out-of-domain) does not dominate ASR training.
- **Method:** An enhancement of the cycle-consistency ASR-TTS self-supervised framework that improves robustness to out-of-domain unpaired data via two additions: (1) a language-model penalty on the ASR->TTS path to filter/penalize poor hypotheses, and (2) a tunable scaling factor on synthesized-speech attention in the TTS->ASR path.
- **Architecture:** Attention-based encoder-decoder ASR plus end-to-end (Tacotron-style) TTS in a differentiable closed loop, augmented with an external/neural language model for hypothesis scoring; attention-context scaling hyperparameter on the synthesized-speech branch.
- **Base models:** Builds directly on the authors' prior unpaired ASR-TTS cycle model (arXiv:1905.01152); uses a pretrained language model for the ASR->TTS penalty; ESPnet-lineage ASR and TTS components.
- **Datasets:** LibriSpeech and BABEL (multilingual, out-of-domain stress test); paired subset for supervised init plus unpaired speech/text for the self-supervised loop.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** 1) Initialize ASR and TTS on a small paired set (supervised). 2) Run the unpaired ASR-TTS cycle as in the prior work. 3) ASR->TTS direction: decode unpaired speech to N-best/hypotheses, apply an external LM penalty so weak hypotheses are down-weighted before TTS synthesizes audio from them, improving synthetic-pair quality. 4) TTS->ASR direction: introduce a scaling hyperparameter that scales the attention context derived from synthesized speech, tuned to handle domain mismatch. 5) Jointly optimize supervised + cycle losses on LibriSpeech and BABEL; tune the LM weight and the attention-scaling factor. Evaluate the supervised-vs-self-supervised WER gap.
- **Key results:** Reduces the gap between supervised and self-supervised training by an absolute 2.6% on LibriSpeech and 2.7% on BABEL relative to the unenhanced ASR-TTS baseline.
- **Notes:** Focused on out-of-domain robustness of self-training; the LM-penalty acts as a quality gate on synthetic data. Same author group as paper 1; treat as its direct successor. No public code found.

### 122. Low-resource expressive text-to-speech using data augmentation

_Goeric Huybrechts, Thomas Merritt, Giulia Comini, Bartek Perz, Raahil Shah, Jaime Lorenzo-Trueba · 2021 · ICASSP 2021_

- **Categories:** C6, C5, C2
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2011.05707) · arXiv:2011.05707
- **Synthetic-data role:** Synthetic data is voice-converted speech created by transferring a desired speaking style (newscaster, conversational) from other speakers' recordings into the target speaker's voice via voice conversion. With as little as 15 minutes of target recordings, this VC-augmented synthetic data dramatically expands the target-style training set; the TTS is trained on synthetic + the small real target data, then fine-tuned.
- **Method:** A 3-step low-resource expressive TTS recipe needing only ~15 minutes of target speaker data: (1) augment via voice conversion using style recordings from other speakers, (2) train a TTS on the synthetic data plus the limited target recordings, (3) fine-tune on the real target data to raise quality. Improves naturalness, signal quality, and style adequacy without hurting speaker similarity.
- **Architecture:** Voice-conversion model for augmentation + neural TTS acoustic model + neural vocoder (Tacotron-class sequence-to-sequence acoustic model with neural vocoder). Exact sizes not reported.
- **Base models:** Voice conversion model plus a neural TTS + vocoder stack; specific pretrained checkpoints not reported.
- **Datasets:** ~15 minutes of target-speaker recordings (low-resource), plus existing style recordings (newscaster and conversational) from other speakers used as VC sources. Tested across multiple speakers in single- and multi-speaker setups. Specific corpus names/hours not reported.
- **Code:** none found
- **Recipe:** Step 1 (Augment): use voice conversion to convert other speakers' recordings in the desired style (newscaster / conversational) into the target speaker's voice, generating synthetic target-style data from only ~15 min of real target audio. Step 2 (Train): train the TTS acoustic model + vocoder on the synthetic data combined with the limited real target recordings (single- or multi-speaker configurations). Step 3 (Fine-tune): fine-tune the trained model on the small real target data to recover signal quality and naturalness. Both augmentation and fine-tuning are needed; evaluate signal quality, naturalness, style adequacy, and speaker similarity via subjective tests.
- **Key results:** Significant subjective improvements over non-augmented models across signal quality, naturalness, and style adequacy, with no drop in speaker similarity, for both newscaster and conversational styles in single- and multi-speaker setups. Specific MOS numbers not reported in available materials.
- **Notes:** Amazon work, ICASSP 2021 (arXiv 2020). Foundational data-augmentation-for-expressive-TTS paper; the 2022 cross-speaker style-transfer paper extends it. Proprietary data; no code released. PDF mirrors: arxiv.org/pdf/2011.05707 and assets.amazon.science.

### 123. Speaker Generation

_Daisy Stanton, Matt Shannon, Soroosh Mariooryad, RJ Skerry-Ryan, Eric Battenberg, Tom Bagby, David Kao · 2021 · ICASSP 2022 (arXiv preprint Nov 2021)_

- **Categories:** C6, C1
- **Links:** [paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2111.05095) · arXiv:2111.05095
- **Synthetic-data role:** The synthetic artifact is the speaker itself: TacoSpawn learns a prior distribution over a speaker-embedding space and samples NEW, nonexistent human-sounding voices never seen in training. These synthetic speaker embeddings condition a TTS model to synthesize speech in novel identities. Quantity is unbounded (sample as many novel speakers as desired); synthetic here means synthetic speaker identities/voices, not synthetic transcripts.
- **Method:** Instead of mapping each training speaker to a fixed lookup embedding, TacoSpawn fits a parametric mixture prior over the speaker-embedding space, conditioned on metadata (e.g. region/gender).
